Completeness of the First Figure and Conversion of Possible Premises
Author discusses completeness of first figure and refutes objections regarding possible premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze why the first figure is intrinsically complete and evaluate the effect of converting a possible premise on syllogistic self-evidence.

Core Idea
The first figure's completeness lies in direct mediation; conversion of a possible premise requires auxiliary reasoning.

In this passage, the author deepens our understanding of what makes a syllogistic figure 'complete' (kamil). He defends the first figure's absolute completeness against a relative interpretation and then addresses a subtle objection: what happens when a premise is a possible negative? The discussion reveals how conversion can salvage a conclusion but at the cost of self-evidence. This lesson will guide you through the author's logic and its implications for modal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's definition of completeness
Find where the author states that completeness depends on the middle term's direct mediation: the major is predicated of the middle and the middle of the minor. This is the benchmark for self-evidence.
The author contrasts this with the indirect relation in other figures.
2
Understand why the first figure is complete absolutely
The author refutes the claim that completeness is only relative. He argues that the first figure's structure makes the conclusion evident by itself, without needing conversion or reduction.
Look for the phrase 'لا يلزم من كون القياس إذ ذاك غير بين أن يكون ما نحن فيه غير بيّن'.
3
Analyze the objection about possible premises
The objection: if a premise is a possible negative (mumkin salib), it must be converted to an affirmative to produce a conclusion, making the syllogism not self-evident. The author agrees this particular syllogism becomes incomplete.
The author uses variables 'ب' under 'أ' via 'ج' and the universal premise 'كل ما هو ممكن أن لا يكون'.
4
Follow the author's two-part refutation
First, the philosopher investigates all types of existence, not only perpetual or majority. Second, if the premise is majority before conversion, the conclusion after conversion can be converted back to majority; if minority, the conclusion remains minority, but that does not invalidate the syllogism.
This refutation addresses the objection that converting a majority possible to minority makes it useless.

Source Text

واحد من الحدود تحت الآخر لم يكن بالقوة في حكم الحاكم، بل في نفس الأمر، ولا يلزم من كونه غير بين عند دخوله بالقوة في حكم الحاكم أن يكون غير بين عند دخوله في حكم الحاكم بالفعل، وإن كان بالقوة في نفس الأمر أيضاً، فإن الحكم بالحد الأكبر على الأصغر في الثاني والثالث إيجاباً وسلباً إنما هو بواسطة الحد الأوسط، وليس نسبة الأكبر إلى الأصغر فيهما بواسطة الحد الأوسط على النحو المفهوم من توسطه في الشكل الأول من كون الأكبر محكوماً به على نفس الأوسط، والأوسط على نفس الأصغر، بل على وجه آخر إذا صدق أمكن أن يقال يصدق نسبة الأكبر إلى الأصغر، ولا يلزم من كون القياس إذ ذاك غير بين أن يكون ما نحن فيه غير بيّن، وعلى هذا فقد ظهر أيضاً فساد قول من قال إنما سمیناه كاملاً بالقياس إلى ما بعده من الأشكال لا مطلقاً، وعلى ما بيناه من معنى الكامل وغير الكامل، فلو كانت المقدمة المشروط إيجابها من القرينة في هذا الشكل ممكنة سالبة بالمعنى الخاص، فقد قلنا أن القرينة تكون منتجة لكون هذا السالب في حكم الموجب وقوته، لكن لا سبيل إلى الإنتاج إلا بقلبها إلى الإيجاب وإذ ذاك فالقياس يكون غير كامل ولا بيّن بنفسه، فإن دخول "ب" تحت "أ" بواسطة دخولها تحت "ج" ودخول "ج" تحت "أ" وهو غير بين، إلا مع النظر إلى هذا القلب وتذكر هذه المقدمة الكلية وهي أن كل ما هو ممكن أن لا يكون، وعند هذا القلب نتبين دخول "ب" تحت الحد الأوسط الذي هو "ج" وإلا فمع قطع النظر عن فهم أن كل ما هو ممكن أن لا يكون فهو ممكن أن يكون لا نتبين دخول "ب" تحت "ج" فتكون نسبة تذكر ما مثل هذه المقدمة، والقلب للمقدمة الصغرى في بيان إنتاج القرينة نازلاً منزلة العكس، أو الخلف، أو الافتراض في الشكل الثاني والثالث كما ستعلمه . وما قيل من أن الفيلسوف لا يطلب غير الدائم أو الأكثري، وهذه المقدمة الممكنة وإن كانت أكثرية فعند القلب إلى الإيجاب قد تعود أقلية، ويخرج عن الاقتران إذ ذاك عن الاستعمال، فخطأ من وجهين [الوجه الأول: إن كان المقصود إنما هو تحقق الإمكان فتكون مستعملة، وإنما لا تكون مستعمله ولا مطلوبة عندما لو كان طلب الفيلسوف الوجود، فبحثه إنما يكون في الدائم أو الأكثري، لكن بحث الفيلسوف ليس عن الوجود الدائم أو الأكثري فقط، بل عن الممكن المساوي أيضاً، بل والأقلي أيضاً، وهو وإن قدر أن نفعه نزر بالنسبة فليس كتاب القياس موضوعاً لبيان الأنفع فقط. الوجه الثاني إن كانت أكثرية قبل القلب وأقليّة بعده، فلا بد من قلب المطلوب بعد الإنتاج، وهو يعود أكثرياً. وأما إن كانت أقلية قبل القلب فهي وإن عادت أكثرية بعده فعند القلب المطلوب يعود أقليّاً فلا يندفع به ما استبعد كما قد ظن.
One of the terms under the other was not potentially in the judgment of the judge, but in the thing itself, and it does not follow from its being not evident when it enters potentially into the judgment of the judge that it is not evident when it enters actually into the judgment of the judge, even if it is potential in the thing itself also. For the judgment of the major term over the minor in the second and third figures, affirmative and negative, is only by means of the middle term, and the relation of the major to the minor in them is not by means of the middle term in the manner understood from its mediation in the first figure — that the major is predicated of the middle itself and the middle of the minor itself — but in another way such that if true, it can be said that the relation of the major to the minor is true. And it does not follow from the syllogism being not evident then that what we are dealing with is not evident. And upon this there also appears the corruption of the statement of one who said that we only call it complete relative to what comes after it of the figures, not absolutely — upon what we have explained of the meaning of complete and incomplete. So if the premise whose affirmation is conditional from the qarina in this figure is possible negative in the specific sense, we have said that the qarina is productive because this negative is in the judgment and force of the affirmative, but there is no way to production except by converting it to affirmation, and then the syllogism is incomplete and not evident by itself. For the subsumption of "B" under "A" by means of its subsumption under "J" and the subsumption of "J" under "A" is not evident, except with consideration of this conversion and recollection of this universal premise, namely that everything that is possible may not be, and upon this conversion we discern the subsumption of "B" under the middle term which is "J". Otherwise, with abstraction from understanding that everything that is possible may not be is possible may be, we do not discern the subsumption of "B" under "J", so it is a relation that is mentioned like this premise. And the conversion of the minor premise in explaining the production of the qarina takes the place of conversion (aks), or reduction (khulf), or assumption (iftirad) in the second and third figures, as you will learn. And what is said that the philosopher does not seek other than the perpetual or the majority, and this possible premise even if it is majority, then upon conversion to affirmation it may become minority, and it exits from conjunction (iqtiran) thus from use, is erroneous from two aspects. The first aspect: if the intention is only the verification of possibility, then it is used; it is only not used nor sought when the philosopher's pursuit is existence, so his investigation is only in the perpetual or the majority. But the philosopher's investigation is not only about perpetual or majority existence, but also about the equal possible, and even the minority possible. And even if it is granted that its benefit is small relatively, the book of syllogism is not composed to explain only the more beneficial. The second aspect: if it is majority before conversion and minority after it, then the conversion of the desired (matlub) after production is necessary, and it returns to majority. But if it is minority before conversion, then even if it returns to majority after it, upon conversion the desired returns to minority, so what is deemed improbable is not repelled, as is thought.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe examining the completeness of the first figure of categorical syllogism. The author begins by clarifying that a term's potentiality in the judgment of the judge (potentially known) does not imply it is not evident when actually known. He then explains that in the second and third figures, the major term is not related to the minor via the middle term in the same direct way as in the first figure (where the middle term is the subject of the major and the predicate of the minor); instead, the relation is indirect, and even if the syllogism is not self-evident, that does not mean the conclusion is not evident. Building on this, the author refutes a claim that the first figure is called 'complete' only relative to other figures: he argues that completeness is absolute, based on the direct mediation of the middle term. The discussion then turns to a special case: if a premise in this figure is a possible negative (mumkin salib), the qarina (a certain type of proposition) can still produce a conclusion because the negative has the force of an affirmative, but conversion to affirmation is required, making the syllogism incomplete and not self-evident. The author illustrates with variables: 'B' under 'A' via 'J', needing a universal premise 'everything possible may not be' to recognize the subsumption after conversion. This conversion is analogous to 'aks, khulf, or iftirad in other figures. Finally, the author addresses an objection: that the philosopher only seeks perpetual or majority cases, so converting a majority possible to a minority one renders it useless. He refutes this with two points: (1) the philosopher investigates all possibilities, not just the beneficial; (2) if the premise is majority before conversion, the desired conclusion after conversion can be converted back to majority; if minority, the desired conclusion may become minority, but that does not invalidate the syllogism's validity.

Key Terms
كامل
قلب
قرينة
ممكن

Checkpoint
According to the author, what makes the first figure 'complete' (kamil) compared to other figures?
A) It produces a conclusion without conversion of any premise.
B) The major term is predicated of the minor directly, without a middle term.
C) The middle term is the subject of the major and predicate of the minor, making the relation self-evident.
D) It is called complete only because it is the simplest form.

Key Takeaway
The first figure is complete because the middle term directly links major and minor, making the conclusion self-evident without conversion; conversion of a possible premise introduces incompleteness but does not undermine the figure's absolute status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism on a fiqh topic (e.g., prohibition of khamr) and explain why it is complete without conversion.
?
Identify a syllogism from al-Ghazali's 'Mi'yar al-Ilm' that uses a possible premise and discuss whether conversion is needed to reach the conclusion.

Mixed Premises: General and Temporal
Author classifies mixed premises and determines results for combinations of absolute and temporal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mixtures of absolute and temporal premises in categorical syllogisms and determine whether a conclusion follows and its modality.

Core Idea
In mixed premises, the major's modality rules, but a temporal major blocks conclusion.

In Islamic logic, syllogisms often mix premises of different types—absolute, temporal, existential. Understanding how these mixtures affect the conclusion is crucial for evaluating arguments in theology and jurisprudence. This lesson focuses on mixtures of general absolute propositions with temporal ones, a common source of fallacious reasoning if not properly analyzed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Determine whether each premise is general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma), existential (wujida), or temporal (waqtiyya). General absolute applies universally without time restriction; temporal applies only to present or past.
The passage also mentions modalized propositions, but we focus on absolute mixtures.
2
Determine minor and major premises
In a categorical syllogism, the minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion; the major contains the predicate. Identify which premise is the minor and which is the major.
The order in the text may not be standard; use term positions.
3
Apply the rule for general absolute minor and specific major
If the general absolute is the minor and the specific (existential or temporal) is the major, the conclusion's modality follows the major. This is based on the principle 'predicated of every' (al-qawl 'ala al-kull).
Example: if major is temporal, conclusion is temporal.
4
Apply the rule when general absolute is major
If the general absolute is the major, the conclusion is always general possible (mumkin 'amm), because the major could be necessary or possible—the common thread is possibility.
The author states this rule explicitly.
5
Identify invalid combinations
When the minor is general absolute and the major is temporal, no conclusion is produced. The temporal major is inherently particular, and a particular major in the first figure (where this applies) destroys syllogistic validity.
The example given: 'Every human is an animal' (general) and 'Every animal is a horse' (temporal) does not yield 'Every human is a horse'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت القرينة مختلطة فإما أن يكون الاختلاط من المطلقات بعضها مع بعض، أو من الموجهات أو من المطلقات والموجهات فإن كان الاختلاط من المطلقات فإما أن يكون من المطلقة العامة والوجودية، أو من العامة والوقتية، أو من الوجودية والوقتية، فإن كانت العامة صغرى والخاصة كبرى فجهة النتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى لما حققناه في فصل المقول على الكل، وإن كانت العامة كبرى فالنتيجة ممكنة عامة؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون الأكبر للأصغر ضرورياً، ويحتمل أن يكون ممكناً، والعام لهما هو الممكن العام. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقة والوقتية فإما أن تكون العامة صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت العامة صغرى والوقتية كبرى فلا إنتاج أصلاً؛ إذ الوقتية في نفس الأمر جزئية من جهة الحكم فيها في نفس الأمر إنما هو على بعض الحد الأوسط، "أ" تحقق له وجود في الحال أو الماضي دون الاستقبال على ما تحقق، ولا قياس في هذا الشكل عما كبراه جزئية؛ لجواز اختلاف الحد الأوسط، ولهذا يصدق قولنا: (بالإطلاق العام كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان فرس بالإطلاق الوقتي ولا تصدق النتيجة ك) إنسان (فرس وإن كانت العامة كبرى فالنتيجة ممكنة عامة لما عرف سابقاً. وإن كان الاختلاط من الوقتية والوجودية، فالحكم فيها كالحكم في اختلاط
And as for if the qarina is mixed, then either the mixture is from absolute propositions (mutlaqat) with each other, or from modalized propositions, or from absolute and modalized. If the mixture is from absolute propositions, then either from the general absolute and existential, or from general and temporal, or from existential and temporal. If the general is minor and the specific is major, then the modality of the conclusion follows the modality of the major, according to what we verified in the chapter on 'predicated of every'. And if the general is major, then the conclusion is general possible; because it is possible that the major is necessary for the minor, and possible that it is possible, and the common to them is the general possible. And as for if the mixture is from absolute and temporal, then either the general is minor or major. If the general is minor and the temporal is major, then there is no production at all; for the temporal in itself is particular insofar as the judgment in itself is only on some of the middle term, "A" has existence in the present or past but not future according to what is verified, and there is no syllogism in this figure concerning whose major is particular; because of the possibility of difference of the middle term. And for this reason our statement is true: (by absolute generality) 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is a horse by temporal absolute (waqti)', and the conclusion 'Every human is a horse' is not true. And if the general is major, then the conclusion is general possible, as known previously. And if the mixture is from temporal and existential, the judgment concerning it is like the judgment concerning the mixture
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise on mixed premises (mukhtalita) in syllogisms. The author systematically categorizes mixtures: from absolute propositions (mutlaqat) among themselves, from modalized propositions (muwajjahat), and from both. For absolute mixtures, three types: general absolute ('amma) and existential (wujida), general and temporal (waqtiyya), existential and temporal. The rule: if the general absolute is minor and the specific (e.g., existential or temporal) is major, the conclusion's modality follows the major; if the general is major, the conclusion is general possible (mumkin 'amm), because the major could be necessary or possible. The author then focuses on the mixture of absolute general and temporal. If the general is minor and temporal is major, no conclusion is produced because the temporal is inherently particular — its judgment applies only to some of the middle term (e.g., only to those existing in present/past, not future). A particular major premise invalidates the syllogism due to possible variation in the middle term. The example given: 'Every human is an animal' (general absolute) and 'Every animal is a horse' (temporal absolute) does not yield 'Every human is a horse'. If the general is major, the conclusion is general possible. The passage ends noting that mixture of temporal and existential is treated similarly, but the text cuts off at the page break.

Key Terms
مختلطة
مطلقة عامة
وقتية
وجودية

Checkpoint

B) The conclusion is temporal.
C) No conclusion is produced.
D) The conclusion is necessary.

Key Takeaway
In mixed-premise syllogisms, the type of the major premise governs the conclusion's modality, but when the major is temporal (particular), no conclusion is produced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism

Mixing Absolute Premises in the First Figure
Rules for mixing absolute premises in first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion when mixing general absolute, existential, and temporal absolute premises in the first figure.

Core Idea
The modality of the conclusion depends on which premise is major.

In the first figure of categorical syllogisms, absolute premises come in three types: general, existential, and temporal. When mixing these types, the modality of the conclusion is not always absolute; it depends on the position and type of the major premise. This lesson explores the rules for such mixing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of absolute premises
General absolute (المطلقة العامة) has no temporal restriction and is universal. Existential (الوجودية) asserts existence at some time. Temporal (الوقتية) asserts existence within a specific time interval.
These are all 'absolute' (non-modal) propositions, but differ in temporal specificity.
2
Mixing general and existential: determine conclusion
If the general is the minor and the existential is the major, the conclusion's modality follows the major (existential). If the general is the major, the conclusion is possible general (ممكنة عامة), because the major may be necessary or possible for the minor, and the common modality is possibility.
The principle refers to the 'maqul ala al-kull' chapter.
3
Mixing general and temporal: check for validity
If the general is the minor and the temporal is the major, there is no conclusion because the temporal major is effectively particular (it applies only to some instances of the middle term). If the general is the major, the conclusion is possible general.
Example: 'Every human is an animal' (general) and 'Every animal is a horse' (temporal) does not yield 'Every human is a horse'.
4
Mixing temporal and existential: apply analogous rule
The judgment for mixing temporal and existential is like mixing general and temporal. This means if the more specific (temporal) is major, no conclusion; if the more general (existential) is major, conclusion is possible general.
The passage explicitly states similarity, so you can infer the cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت القرينة مختلطة فإما أن يكون الاختلاط من المطلقات بعضها مع بعض، أو من الموجهات أو من المطلقات والموجهات فإن كان الاختلاط من المطلقات فإما أن يكون من المطلقة العامة والوجودية، أو من العامة والوقتية، أو من الوجودية والوقتية، فإن كانت العامة صغرى والخاصة كبرى فجهة النتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى لما حققناه في فصل المقول على الكل، وإن كانت العامة كبرى فالنتيجة ممكنة عامة؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون الأكبر للأصغر ضرورياً، ويحتمل أن يكون ممكناً، والعام لهما هو الممكن العام. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقة والوقتية فإما أن تكون العامة صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت العامة صغرى والوقتية كبرى فلا إنتاج أصلاً؛ إذ الوقتية في نفس الأمر جزئية من جهة الحكم فيها في نفس الأمر إنما هو على بعض الحد الأوسط، "أ" تحقق له وجود في الحال أو الماضي دون الاستقبال على ما تحقق، ولا قياس في هذا الشكل عما كبراه جزئية؛ لجواز اختلاف الحد الأوسط، ولهذا يصدق قولنا: بالإطلاق العام كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان فرس بالإطلاق الوقتي ولا تصدق النتيجة كل إنسان فرس. وإن كانت العامة كبرى فالنتيجة ممكنة عامة لما عرف سابقاً. وإن كان الاختلاط من الوقتية والوجودية، فالحكم فيها كالحكم في اختلاط المطلقة العامة والوقتية.
And if the premise is mixed, then the mixing is either from the absolute [premises] with each other, or from the modalized [premises], or from the absolute and the modalized. If the mixing is from the absolute, then it is either from the general absolute and the existential, or from the general and the temporal, or from the existential and the temporal. If the general [absolute] is the minor and the specific [existential/temporal] is the major, then the modality of the conclusion follows the modality of the major, according to what we verified in the chapter on 'al-maqul 'ala al-kull'. And if the general is the major, then the conclusion is possible general, because it is possible that the major belongs to the minor necessarily, and possible that it is possible, and the common [modality] between them is the general possible. And if the mixing is from the absolute and the temporal, then either the general is the minor or the major. If the general is the minor and the temporal is the major, then there is no conclusion at all, because the temporal in reality is particular, since the judgment in it in reality is only on some of the middle term, such that 'A' has existence in the present or past but not in the future, as verified, and there is no syllogism in this figure when its major is particular because of the possibility of the middle term differing; hence our saying: 'In the general absolute, every human is an animal, and every animal is a horse in the temporal absolute' is true, but the conclusion 'Every human is a horse' is not true. And if the general is the major, then the conclusion is possible general, as previously known. And if the mixing is from the temporal and the existential, then the judgment regarding it is like the judgment regarding the mixing of the general absolute and temporal.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage explains the rules for mixing different types of absolute premises (المطلقات) in the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first classifies absolute premises into three types: general absolute (المطلقة العامة), existential (الوجودية), and temporal (الوقتية). The general absolute is a universal proposition without temporal restriction; the existential asserts existence at some time; the temporal asserts existence in a specific time period. The discussion covers three combinations: (1) general and existential, (2) general and temporal, (3) temporal and existential. For (1), if the general is minor and the existential is major, the conclusion's modality follows the major (existential). If the general is major, the conclusion is 'possible general' (ممكنة عامة) because the major could be necessarily or possibly true of the minor, and the common modality is possibility. For (2), if the general is minor and the temporal is major, no conclusion is produced because temporal premises are effectively particular—they apply only to some instances of the middle term (e.g., only to individuals existing now or in the past). Since a syllogism in the first figure requires a universal major, a particular major invalidates the inference. The example given is: 'Every human is an animal' (general) and 'Every animal is a horse' (temporal). The temporal premise 'Every animal is a horse' is false in reality, but even if taken as true for a specific time, it does not yield a universal conclusion because the temporal restriction makes the major particular. If the general is major, the conclusion is possible general. For (3), mixing temporal and existential is analogous to mixing general and temporal, meaning the same rules apply. Throughout, the author references an earlier chapter 'al-maqul 'ala al-kull' for the principle that the modality of the conclusion follows the major when the minor is general and the major is specific.

الوجودية
الوقتية
القرينة
جهة النتيجة

Key Takeaway
When mixing absolute premises, the modality of the conclusion follows the major if the minor is general; if the major is temporal, no conclusion is possible due to its particular nature.

Mixed Modal Syllogisms: Necessary Absolute and Conditional in First Figure
Analyzes mixed modal first-figure syllogism with necessary absolute minor and conditional major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct modality of the conclusion when a necessary absolute minor premise is combined with a conditional major premise in the first figure of a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
When modalities diverge, the conclusion's modality is the common (general possible) modality, not automatically the minor's.

This passage tackles a subtle case in modal logic: what happens when you mix a necessary absolute premise with a conditional premise? The common view says the conclusion follows the minor's modality, but the author shows this fails because necessity can be absolute or conditional. Using the example of the sphere's change, he argues that the conclusion must be in the general possible modality to avoid contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism components
The author gives: minor premise 'Every B is C with absolute necessity' and major premise 'Everything that is C perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as it is C'. Recognize that the major is conditional — it holds only while the subject is C.
Perpetuity here means the condition holds for all times the subject exists.
2
State the common view
Specialists claim the conclusion is 'Every B is A necessarily' because B is C necessarily and the major says all perpetual C is A; since B is perpetually C, perpetuity in universals is necessary.
This relies on the idea that universal perpetuity entails necessity.
3
Understand the author's objection
The author distinguishes absolute necessity from conditional necessity. He gives the counterexample: 'Every sphere is perpetually changing' is true but not absolutely necessary — it depends on the sphere's motion. So if the major's necessity is conditional, the conclusion cannot be absolutely necessary.
The sphere example shows that perpetuity alone does not guarantee absolute necessity.
4
Derive the correct conclusion
If the necessity in the major is conditional, the conclusion's modality should follow the major, not the minor. But the minor is absolute and the major conditional — the common modality between them is general possibility (imkan 'amm). Hence the conclusion is 'Every B is A with general possibility'. The author shows that assuming otherwise leads to contradiction in the second figure.
General possibility includes both absolute and conditional necessity, so it is a safe intermediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الموجهات بعضها مع بعض فإما أن تكون من الضرورية المطلقة والمشروطة، أو من المطلقة والممكنة، أو من المشروطة والممكنة، أو من المشروطات بعضها مع بعض، أو الممكنات بعضها مع بعض، فإن كانت من الضرورية المطلقة والمشروطة فإما أن تكون المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع أو بشرط مع المحمول أو بوقت فإن كانت مشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع فإما أن يكون الشرط دائماً بدوام الموضوع والمحمول دائم بدوامه، أو أنه دائم بدوام الموضوع والمحمول غير دائم بدوامه، أو أنه غير دائم بدوام الموضوع والمحمول دائم بدوامه، وإما أن يكون ثابتاً للموضوع في بعض أوقات كونه مشروطاً لا ما دام مشروطاً، فإن كان الأول فإما أن تكون الضرورية المطلقة صغرى أو المشروطة كبرى أو بالعكس، فإن كانت المطلقة صغرى والمشروطة كبرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول بالضرورة المطلقة كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج") فالمشهور بين الخواص أن النتيجة ضرورية تابعة لجهة الصغرى مستثناة عما قيل من اتباعها الكبرى وذلك لأن "أ" دائماً لكل ما هو دائماً "ج" و"ب" "ج" دائماً لكونه "ج" ضرورياً لـ "ب" فكل "ب" دائماً "أ" والدوام في الكليات لا يكون إلا ضرورياً، فكل "ب" بالضرورة "أ". وفيه نظر فإن الدوام في الكليات وإن كان ضرورياً إلا أنا قد بينا أن الضروري أعم من الضروري المطلق؛ إذ هو قد يكون مطلقاً، وقد يكون مشروطاً، فإن أريد بالضرورة في دوام الكليات الضرورة المطلقة فهو منتقض بقولنا: (كل فلك كتغير دائماً فإنه حق، وليس التغير مع دوامه وكون القضية كلية ضرورياً مطلقاً بل مشروطاً بدوام كون الفلك متحركاً. وإن أريد به الضرورة المشروطة، فذلك مما لا يوجب اتباع النتيجة لجهة الصغرى، بل لجهة الكبرى، إذ هي مشروطة والصغرى مطلقة، وعند ذلك فنقول أمكن أن يكون "أ" ثابتاً لـ "ب" بالضرورة المطلقة) و(أمكن أن يكون مشروطاً بدوام "ج" لـ "ب") والعام لهما من الجهات هو الممكن العام، فإذن النتيجة كل "ب" "أ" بالإمكان العام وإلا فالصادق بالضرورة ليس كل "ب" "أ") وكان (كل "ج" "أ" ما دام "ج") ويلزمه على ما ستعلم في الشكل الثاني بالضرورة ليس كل "ب" "ج") وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف.
And as for the mixing of modalities with each other, it is either from the necessary absolute and the conditional, or from the absolute and the possible, or from the conditional and the possible, or from conditionals with each other, or from possibles with each other. If it is from the necessary absolute and the conditional, then the conditional may be with a condition on the subject, or with a condition on the predicate, or with a time. If it is conditional with a condition on the subject, then the condition may be perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject and the predicate is perpetual with its perpetuity, or it is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject and the predicate is not perpetual with its perpetuity, or it is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject and the predicate is perpetual with its perpetuity, or it may be affirmed of the subject in some times of its being conditional, not as long as it is conditional. If it is the first, then the necessary absolute may be minor or the conditional major, or the opposite. If the absolute is minor and the conditional is major, as we say in the first figure: 'by necessary absolute, every B is C,' and 'everything that is C perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as it is C,' then the common view among the specialists is that the conclusion is necessary, following the modality of the minor, except for what was said about its following the major, because A is perpetual for everything that is perpetually C, and B is C perpetually because C is necessary for B, so every B is perpetually A, and perpetuity in universals is only necessary, so every B is necessarily A. However, there is a consideration: perpetuity in universals, although it is necessary, we have shown that the necessary is more general than the absolute necessary; for it may be absolute, and it may be conditional. If what is meant by necessity in the perpetuity of universals is the absolute necessity, then it is refuted by our statement: 'Every sphere is perpetually changing' – this is true, yet the change with its perpetuity and the proposition being universal is not absolutely necessary but conditional on the perpetuity of the sphere being in motion. If what is meant is conditional necessity, then this does not entail the conclusion following the modality of the minor, but rather the modality of the major, since it is conditional and the minor is absolute. In that case, we say: it is possible that A is affirmed of B with absolute necessity, and it is possible that it is conditional on the perpetuity of C for B, and the common modality between them is the general possible. Therefore, the conclusion is: every B is A with general possibility. Otherwise, the true with necessity is not every B is A, and we had 'every C is A as long as it is C,' and it follows from it, as you will learn in the second figure, that by necessity not every B is C, and by necessity every B is C – and that is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of mixed modalities in categorical syllogisms, specifically in the first figure. The author begins by enumerating the possible mixtures of modal propositions: necessary absolute and conditional, absolute and possible, conditional and possible, conditionals with each other, and possibles with each other. He then focuses on the mixture of necessary absolute and conditional, and further subdivides the conditional based on what the condition is attached to: the subject, the predicate, or a time. For the case where the condition is on the subject, he lists four possibilities regarding the perpetuity of the condition and the predicate. He then takes the first possibility (the condition is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject and the predicate is perpetual with its perpetuity) and considers two arrangements: either the necessary absolute is the minor premise or the conditional is the minor. In the specific case where the necessary absolute is the minor and the conditional is the major, he presents the syllogism: 'Every B is C necessarily' and 'Everything that is C perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as it is C'. The common view claims the conclusion is necessary, following the minor premise's modality, because perpetuity in universal propositions entails necessity. However, the author raises an objection: perpetuity in universals can be either absolute necessity or conditional necessity. He gives the example of the sphere's change: 'Every sphere is perpetually changing' — this is true, but the change is not absolutely necessary; rather, it is conditional on the sphere's motion. If the intended necessity is absolute, the example refutes it; if it is conditional, then the conclusion's modality should follow the major (which is conditional), not the minor. Consequently, the conclusion cannot be necessary in either the absolute or the conditional sense; instead, the common modality between absolute and conditional is the general possible. Hence, the conclusion is 'Every B is A with general possibility'. He then argues that if the conclusion were otherwise (i.e., necessary), it would lead to a contradiction in the second figure, namely that some B is not C necessarily and all B is C necessarily, which is absurd. Thus, the correct conclusion is with general possibility.

Mixed Modalities: Necessary Absolute Major and Perpetual Conditional Minor
Author presents and defends the rule for mixed modalities in first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a first-figure categorical syllogism where the major is necessary absolute and the minor is a perpetual conditional, and refute the objection based on the modality of the middle term using the dictum de omni.

Core Idea
The conclusion follows the major's modality, and the dictum de omni ensures transfer of necessity.

In this passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, the author examines a specific mixed-modal syllogism: the major premise is necessary absolute while the minor is a conditional with a perpetual condition. Some might think the conclusion should be conditional because the middle term is only conditionally necessary for the subject. The author refutes this by invoking the dictum de omni principle, clarifying that the necessity of the major transfers directly. Understanding this rule is crucial for evaluating modal syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their modalities
From the example: Minor: 'كل ما هو ب دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو ج ما دام ب' (conditional, perpetual condition). Major: 'كل ج أ بالضرورة' (necessary absolute). Write them in standard form.
2
Apply the conclusion rule
The author states: 'the conclusion follows the modality of the major.' So the conclusion is necessary absolute: 'كل ب أ بالضرورة'.
The text mentions the conclusion is perpetual, but here it is necessary.
3
Examine the imagined objection
The objector reasons: Since C is not necessary absolute for B but only conditional, the attribution of A to B should also be conditional. Why is this wrong?
4
Understand the dictum de omni rebuttal
The dictum de omni states that whatever is affirmed of a universal subject distributively is affirmed of every individual that falls under it. Here, the minor affirms that every B that is perpetually B is C. So all such B are instances of C. The major affirms that every C is necessarily A. Therefore, every such B is necessarily A.
5
Test with a concrete Islamic example
Example: 'Every intoxicant is haram necessarily. Every mature nabidh that is intoxicating is intoxicant perpetually (as long as it is intoxicating). Therefore, every such nabidh is haram necessarily.' This shows the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمكن أن يقال بأن النتيجة دائمة لعموم الدوام للطرفين، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحال في ما بقي من الاقترانات. وأما إن كانت الضرورية المطلقة كبرى والمشروطة صغرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل "ج" "أ" بالضرورة) فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة لكبرى؛ لما عرفناه سابقاً. وربما تعجب من كون "أ" ضرورياً لـ "ب" وهو إنما ينسب إلى "ب" بواسطة "ج" و"ج" ليس لـ "ب" بالضرورة المطلقة بـل بالمشروطة، فكان من الواجب أن تكون جهة نسبته إلى "ب" هي جهة نسبته [إلى] الحد الأوسط إليها، ومن أحاط بما مهدناه في فصل المقول على الكل علم أن ما تخيله هذا المتخيل ليس بشيء. ثم إن كان هذا حقاً فَلِمَ لا كانت النتيجة ضرورية عند كون الأوسط ضرورياً للموضوع والأكبر للأوسط ممكناً. وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية المطلقة والمشروطة بالاعتبار الثاني فإما أن تكون الضرورية المطلقة صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى والمشروطة كبرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" في بعض أوقات كونه "ج" لا دائماً) فالنتيجة تابعة أيضاً لجهة الكبرى؛ لأن "أ" ثابت لـ "ج" الدائم بدوام الموضوع في بعض الأوقات لا دائماً، و"ج" ثابت لـ "ب" دائماً، فيكون "ب" داخلاً تحت حكم الكبرى. وإن كانت الضرورية المطلقة هي الكبرى أيضاً تابعة لجهتها كما علم، وإن استبعد ذلك بسبب كون الحد الأوسط مشروطاً بالنسبة إلى الأصغر، وظن أنه أن تكون جهة النتيجة جهة.
And it is possible to say that the conclusion is perpetual because of the generality of perpetuity for both extremes, and upon this, the situation is not hidden in the remaining figures. But if the necessary absolute is major and the conditional is minor, as we say in the first figure: 'Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is C as long as it is B' and 'Every C is A necessarily,' then the conclusion follows the modality of the major, according to what we have known previously. Perhaps one may wonder that A is necessary for B, while it is attributed to B through the mediation of C, and C is not for B with absolute necessity but with conditional necessity, so it would be necessary that the modality of its attribution to B is the modality of its attribution to the middle term. But he who is knowledgeable about what we have laid down in the chapter on the dictum de omni knows that what this imaginer imagines is nothing. Then, if this is true, why is the conclusion not necessary when the middle is necessary for the subject and the major is possible for the middle? As for the mixing between the necessary absolute and the conditional in the second consideration, then either the necessary absolute is minor or major. If it is minor and the conditional is major, as we say in the first figure: 'by necessity every B is C' and 'everything that is C perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A at some times of its being C, not perpetually,' then the conclusion also follows the modality of the major, because A is affirmed of C which is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject at some times not perpetually, and C is affirmed of B perpetually, so B falls under the judgment of the major. If the necessary absolute is the major, it also follows its modality, as known, even if this is deemed far-fetched because the middle term is conditional in relation to the minor, and one may think that the modality of the conclusion should be the modality of the middle.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of mixed modalities in the first figure. First, the author presents an alternative view that the conclusion is perpetual (universal) due to the generality of perpetuity for both extremes, suggesting that the situation in the remaining figures is analogous. Then he considers the opposite arrangement: the necessary absolute is the major and the conditional is the minor. The example is: 'Every B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is C as long as it is B' (conditional minor) and 'Every C is A necessarily' (necessary absolute major). Here, the conclusion follows the modality of the major, i.e., necessary. The author then addresses an imagined objection: someone might wonder how A can be necessary for B when the middle term C is only conditionally necessary for B. The objector argues that the modality of A's attribution to B should match the modality of C's attribution to B (conditional). The author dismisses this by referring to his earlier discussion of the dictum de omni (مقول على الكل), asserting that the major term's necessity for C directly transfers to B because C is perpetually affirmed of B. He then poses a rhetorical question: if this principle holds, why is the conclusion not necessary when the middle is necessary for the subject but the major is only possible for the middle? (This hints at a different scenario where the conclusion might not be necessary.) Next, he moves to the 'second consideration' of mixing necessary absolute and conditional. This likely refers to a different classification of conditional propositions, possibly where the condition is not perpetual or the predicate is not perpetual. He again considers two cases: necessary absolute minor and conditional major, and necessary absolute major and conditional minor. In the first case (minor necessary, major conditional with a temporal qualification: 'at some times of its being C, not perpetually'), the conclusion follows the modality of the major (conditional) because the affirmation of A to C is only temporal, and since B is perpetually C, B falls under that temporal judgment. In the second case (major necessary), the conclusion again follows the major, even though it might seem less intuitive because the middle term is conditional relative to the minor. The text ends abruptly, but the principle is consistent: the modality of the conclusion is determined by the major premise when it is necessary absolute, or by the major when it is conditional in these mixed cases.

Second Consideration: Necessary Absolute and Temporal Conditional
Author introduces the second consideration for mixing necessary absolute and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in first-figure mixed-modal syllogisms involving a temporal (non-perpetual) conditional premise, in both arrangements.

Core Idea
In the second consideration, the conclusion also follows the major's modality, even when the conditional has a temporal qualification.

This passage introduces a 'second consideration' of mixing necessary absolute and conditional, where the conditional premise does not have a perpetual predicate but is limited to some times. The author examines two cases: necessary absolute minor with conditional major, and necessary absolute major with conditional minor. In both, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise. Understanding this distinction helps in analyzing modal syllogisms with temporal conditions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second consideration conditional
In this consideration, the conditional major says: 'Everything that is C perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A at some times of its being C, not perpetually.' The predicate A is only affirmed temporally.
2
Case 1: Necessary absolute minor, temporal conditional major
Minor: by necessity every B is C. Major: every C perpetually with perpetuity of subject is A at some times not perpetually. Conclusion: Every B is A at some times (not perpetually). So conclusion follows major (conditional/temporal).
3
Case 2: Necessary absolute major, temporal conditional minor
Minor: conditional with perpetual condition (e.g., 'every B that is perpetually B is C'). Major: necessary absolute. Conclusion: necessary absolute, following the major. The author notes this may seem far-fetched but applies the same principle.
4
Why the rule holds
In case 1, the temporal affirmation of A to C transfers to B because C is perpetually affirmed of B. In case 2, the necessity of A for C applies to all B that are C perpetually, so the conclusion is necessary.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Case 1: 'Every intoxicant is haram at the time of intoxication (temporal). Every mature nabidh that is intoxicating is intoxicant perpetually. Therefore, every such nabidh is haram at the time of intoxication.' Case 2: 'Every intoxicant is haram necessarily. Every intoxicating drink that is kept is intoxicant perpetually. Therefore, every such drink is haram necessarily.'

وأمكن أن يقال بأن النتيجة دائمة لعموم الدوام للطرفين، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحال في ما بقي من الاقترانات. وأما إن كانت الضرورية المطلقة كبرى والمشروطة صغرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل "ج" "أ" بالضرورة) فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة لكبرى؛ لما عرفناه سابقاً. وربما تعجب من كون "أ" ضرورياً لـ "ب" وهو إنما ينسب إلى "ب" بواسطة "ج" و"ج" ليس لـ "ب" بالضرورة المطلقة بـل بالمشروطة، فكان من الواجب أن تكون جهة نسبته إلى "ب" هي جهة نسبته [إلى] الحد الأوسط إليها، ومن أحاط بما مهدناه في فصل المقول على الكل علم أن ما تخيله هذا المتخيل ليس بشيء. ثم إن كان هذا حقاً فَلِمَ لا كانت النتيجة ضرورية عند كون الأوسط ضرورياً للموضوع والأكبر للأوسط ممكناً. وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية المطلقة والمشروطة بالاعتبار الثاني فإما أن تكون الضرورية المطلقة صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى والمشروطة كبرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" في بعض أوقات كونه "ج" لا دائماً) فالنتيجة تابعة أيضاً لجهة الكبرى؛ لأن "أ" ثابت لـ "ج" الدائم بدوام الموضوع في بعض الأوقات لا دائماً، و"ج" ثابت لـ "ب" دائماً، فيكون "ب" داخلاً تحت حكم الكبرى. وإن كانت الضرورية المطلقة هي الكبرى أيضاً تابعة لجهتها كما علم، وإن استبعد ذلك بسبب كون الحد الأوسط مشروطاً بالنسبة إلى الأصغر، وظن أنه أن تكون جهة النتيجة جهة.
And it is possible to say that the conclusion is perpetual because of the generality of perpetuity for both extremes, and upon this, the situation is not hidden in the remaining figures. But if the necessary absolute is major and the conditional is minor, as we say in the first figure: 'Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is C as long as it is B' and 'Every C is A necessarily,' then the conclusion follows the modality of the major, according to what we have known previously. Perhaps one may wonder that A is necessary for B, while it is attributed to B through the mediation of C, and C is not for B with absolute necessity but with conditional necessity, so it would be necessary that the modality of its attribution to B is the modality of its attribution to the middle term. But he who is knowledgeable about what we have laid down in the chapter on the dictum de omni knows that what this imaginer imagines is nothing. Then, if this is true, why is the conclusion not necessary when the middle is necessary for the subject and the major is possible for the middle? As for the mixing between the necessary absolute and the conditional in the second consideration, then either the necessary absolute is minor or major. If it is minor and the conditional is major, as we say in the first figure: 'by necessity every B is C' and 'everything that is C perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A at some times of its being C, not perpetually,' then the conclusion also follows the modality of the major, because A is affirmed of C which is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject at some times not perpetually, and C is affirmed of B perpetually, so B falls under the judgment of the major. If the necessary absolute is the major, it also follows its modality, as known, even if this is deemed far-fetched because the middle term is conditional in relation to the minor, and one may think that the modality of the conclusion should be the modality of the middle.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of mixed modalities in the first figure. First, the author presents an alternative view that the conclusion is perpetual (universal) due to the generality of perpetuity for both extremes, suggesting that the situation in the remaining figures is analogous. Then he considers the opposite arrangement: the necessary absolute is the major and the conditional is the minor. The example is: 'Every B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is C as long as it is B' (conditional minor) and 'Every C is A necessarily' (necessary absolute major). Here, the conclusion follows the modality of the major, i.e., necessary. The author then addresses an imagined objection: someone might wonder how A can be necessary for B when the middle term C is only conditionally necessary for B. The objector argues that the modality of A's attribution to B should match the modality of C's attribution to B (conditional). The author dismisses this by referring to his earlier discussion of the dictum de omni (مقول على الكل), asserting that the major term's necessity for C directly transfers to B because C is perpetually affirmed of B. He then poses a rhetorical question: if this principle holds, why is the conclusion not necessary when the middle is necessary for the subject but the major is only possible for the middle? (This hints at a different scenario where the conclusion might not be necessary.) Next, he moves to the 'second consideration' of mixing necessary absolute and conditional. This likely refers to a different classification of conditional propositions, possibly where the condition is not perpetual or the predicate is not perpetual. He again considers two cases: necessary absolute minor and conditional major, and necessary absolute major and conditional minor. In the first case (minor necessary, major conditional with a temporal qualification: 'at some times of its being C, not perpetually'), the conclusion follows the modality of the major (conditional) because the affirmation of A to C is only temporal, and since B is perpetually C, B falls under that temporal judgment. In the second case (major necessary), the conclusion again follows the major, even though it might seem less intuitive because the middle term is conditional relative to the minor. The text ends abruptly, but the principle is consistent: the modality of the conclusion is determined by the major premise when it is necessary absolute, or by the major when it is conditional in these mixed cases.

The Syllogism

Mixing Absolute Necessary and Conditional Premises
Author examines mixing absolute necessary and conditional premises of the third type, analyzing cases of non-production.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze syllogisms mixing absolute necessary and conditional premises to determine whether they produce a conclusion and identify the condition under which they do.

Core Idea
Modality matters: a shared middle term is not enough if the mode of the propositions differs.

In modal logic, syllogisms require not only the middle term to appear in both premises but also uniformity in the mode of predication. This passage examines mixing absolute necessary and conditional premises under the 'third consideration' and shows when such combinations are valid or invalid, providing a deeper understanding of syllogistic rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types
Determine which premise is absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة) and which is conditional (مشروطة) based on the terms. Note the mode: necessary holds always, conditional holds only under a condition.
The passage uses three terms: B (ب), J (ج), A (أ).
2
Check middle term uniformity
Examine whether the middle term (ج) is used in the same way in both premises. In the first scenario when absolute necessary is minor, J is necessary and permanent for B, but in the major premise J is conditional and not permanent. This lack of uniformity prevents production.
Uniformity includes both term and modal status.
3
Evaluate the role of conditionality
When the conditional premise has 'not always' as part of the predicate, the premises may become contradictory. Analyze if the truth of one premise forces the falsity of the other, leading to no production from false premises.
The author distinguishes two subcases for conditional as major.
4
Determine productiveness
Apply the rules: if the conditional is minor and the necessary is major, the syllogism produces a conclusion following the mode of the major premise. If the necessary is minor and the conditional is major, no conclusion follows either due to middle term non-uniformity or mutual falsity.
The valid case requires the middle term to be such that the minor's conditionality does not undermine the major's necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية المطلقة والمشروطة بالاعتبار الثالث فإما أن تكون المطلقة صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى والمشروطة كبرى كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع، فهو "أ" ما دام [؟]) فلا إنتاج ؛ لعدم الاشتراك في الحد الأوسط، فإن "ج" المحكوم به على "ب" دائم بدوام "ب"؛ لكونه ضرورياً لها، و"ج" المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" مما لا دوام له بدوام الموضوع، هذا إن جعل لا دائماً جزءاً من الموضوع وإلا فإن ذكر على أنه جزء من المحمول مع كونه دائماً بدوام شرط الموضوع كقولنا : و(كل "ج" ما دام "ج" فهو "أ" لا دائماً) أي لا دائماً "أ" فلا إنتاج أيضاً، لا لعدم الاشتراك في الحد الأوسط، بل لما فيه من التكاذب بين المقدمة الصغرى والكبرى، فإنه إذا كان كل "ج" ما دام "ج" فهو "أ") فـ ("ب" "أ" لكونها "ج") فإذا لم يكن "أ" دائماً لما قيل له "ج" فليس إلا العدم دوام "ج" له، وقد قيل إن "ج" دائماً لـ "ب" ويلزم من صدق إحدى المقدمتين كذب الأخرى، ولا إنتاج عن المقدمات الكاذبة، كما أنه لا إنتاج عـن الصورة الفاسدة، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا كانت المشروطة هي الصغرى والضرورية هي الكبرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل "ج" "أ" بالضرورة) فإن الاقتران يكون منتجاً، والنتيجة فيه تابعة لجهة الكبرى لما بيناه، وإن احتمل الاستثناء بسبب أن الحد الأوسط غير ضروري للأصغر. فالوجه في حله ما عرف
And as for if the mixing is between the absolute necessary and conditional according to the third consideration, then either the absolute is the minor or the major. If it is the minor and the conditional is the major, like our saying: 'By necessity every B is J' and 'Everything that is J always with the permanence of the subject, it is A as long as [?]', then there is no production due to lack of sharing in the middle term. For J predicated of B is permanent with the permanence of B because it is necessary for it, while J predicated of A has no permanence with the permanence of the subject. This if we make 'not always' a part of the subject; otherwise if it is mentioned as a part of the predicate while being always with the condition of the subject, like our saying: 'Every J as long as it is J, it is A not always', i.e., not always A, then there is no production as well, not due to lack of sharing in the middle term, but because of mutual falsity between the minor and major premises. For if every J as long as it is J is A, then B is A because it is J. So if it is not always A, then it is not said to be J, so nothing remains except the non-permanence of J for it. And it was said that J is always for B, and the truth of one premise necessitates the falsity of the other. There is no production from false premises, just as there is no production from an invalid form. This is contrary to the case where the conditional is the minor and the necessary is the major, like our saying: 'Everything that is B not always with the permanence of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Every J is A by necessity'. For the combination produces, and the conclusion follows the mode of the major as we explained, although it may admit exception because the middle term is not necessary for the minor. The solution for this is what you have known.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage examines the mixing of absolute necessary and conditional premises under the third consideration. The author considers two scenarios based on which premise is minor and which is major. When the absolute necessary is minor and the conditional is major, no conclusion follows because the middle term 'J' is used differently: in the minor it is necessary and permanent for B, but in the major it is not permanent for the subject. This lack of uniformitiy in the middle term prevents a valid syllogism. Alternatively, if the conditional's 'not always' is part of the predicate, the premises become contradictory, leading to mutual falsity and thus no production. However, when the conditional is minor and the necessary is major, a valid conclusion does follow, and it takes the mode of the major premise, though an exception is possible if the middle term is not necessary for the minor. This analysis demonstrates the subtle conditions required for valid syllogisms with modal premises.

Key Terms

مشروطة (mashruta)
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat)

Proof by Contraposition in Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author introduces khulf as a proof method for mixed modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of contraposition (khulf) to verify the conclusion of a mixed modal syllogism involving the fourth consideration.

Core Idea
Khulf: assume the opposite of the conclusion, derive a contradiction of a true premise, and the original conclusion is proven.

When mixing necessary absolute and conditional premises under the fourth consideration, the conclusion's validity depends on which premise is major. The author provides a universal method to prove the conclusion: contraposition (khulf). This technique is essential for verifying arguments in Islamic logic and kalam, where modal distinctions matter. Let us walk through the steps of khulf and apply it to a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's premises and conclusion
Locate the two premises and the conclusion in the argument. Note which premise is major (containing the predicate of the conclusion) and which is minor (containing the subject).
In the passage, the example uses variables B, J, A.
2
Determine if the necessary premise is major
For the fourth consideration, a valid conclusion follows only if the necessary (absolute) premise is major. Check this condition.
If the necessary is minor and conditional is major, no conclusion follows.
3
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
To prove the conclusion by khulf, begin by assuming its contradictory (negation). This is your starting point.
Take natiq al-natija (contradictory of the conclusion).
4
Combine the contradictory with one premise
Combine the assumed contradictory with one of the premises (usually the one that does not already contain it) to form a syllogism.
The passage says 'combine it with one of the two premises'.
5
Derive a contradiction of the other premise
From the combination, deduce something that contradicts the other true premise. This shows that the assumption (contradictory conclusion) is false, so the original conclusion must be true.
The derived proposition 'falsifies the other true premise'.

وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحال في ما بقي من الاقترانات. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضرورية المطلقة والمشروطة بالاعتبار الرابع فإما أن تكون المطلقة صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى والمشروطة كبرى كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما هو "ج" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" في بعض أوقات كونه "ج" لا دائماً) فلا إنتاج أيضاً لما عرف في الاختلاط الذي قبله. وإن كانت الضرورية كبرى والمشروطة صغرى، فالكلام في صحة الإنتاج واتباع النتيجة لجهة الكبرى، واحتمال الاستثناء، وحل الإشكال فيه، فعلى ما تقدم في الاختلاط الذي قبله. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضرورية المطلقة والمشروطة بشرط مع المحمول فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى أيضاً على ما سلف. وكذلك الحكم في ما إذا كان الاختلاط من الضرورية والمشروطة بشرط وقت، وسواء كان الوقت معيناً أو غير معين. وإن شئت أن تبين جهة النتيجة في جميع هذه الاقترانات بالخلف فخذ نقيض النتيجة وافرنه بما يتفق من مقدمتي الاقتران فإنه ينتج ما يكذب الأخرى الصادقة فيكون المطلوب الأول وهو الحق، وعليك أن تعتبر ذلك في الكل، فإنك تجده على ما وصفناه.
And on this basis, the situation for the remaining conjunctions is not hidden. And as for if the mixing is from the absolute necessary and conditional according to the fourth consideration, then either the absolute is minor or major. If it is minor and the conditional is major, like our saying: 'By necessity every B is J' and 'Everything that is J not always with the permanence of the subject is A in some times of its being J, not always', then there is also no production, as known in the previous mixing. And if the necessary is major and the conditional is minor, then the discussion is about the validity of production and the following of the conclusion to the mode of the major, and the possibility of exception, and the solution of the difficulty in it, as mentioned in the previous mixing. And as for if the mixing is from the absolute necessary and conditional with a condition on the predicate, then the conclusion follows the mode of the major as well, as previously. And the same judgment applies when the mixing is from the necessary and conditional with a condition of time, whether the time is specified or unspecified. Rule for Modal Conclusion in Mixed Syllogisms
Author discusses mixing necessary and possible premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a categorical syllogism when premises mix necessary and possible modes, and resolve apparent counterexamples by checking term alignment.

Core Idea
In mixed premises, the conclusion's modality always matches the major premise.

When premises in a categorical syllogism have different modalities—one necessary, one possible—how do we determine the modality of the conclusion? The classical logicians agreed that the conclusion follows the mode of the major premise, regardless of which is which. This lesson unpacks that rule through a reductio proof and a tricky example involving sperm and human, showing that the subject of the major premise must be understood in the same actual mode as the minor's predicate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their modalities
Read the syllogism and label each premise as necessary (ضرورية) or possible (ممكنة). The author distinguishes three types of possibility: general (ʻāmma), specific (khāṣṣa), and more specific (akhaṣṣ). For this rule, all types work the same.
2
Locate the major premise
The major premise is the one that contains the predicate of the conclusion. In a standard first‑figure syllogism, the major premise is the one with the larger term (محصول الكبرى). Verify which premise is major.
3
Apply the agreement rule
The conclusion takes the modality of the major premise. If the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if the major is possible, the conclusion is possible. This is stated by consensus (بالاتفاق).
4
Check the reductio argument
The author provides a proof: Suppose the necessary is major and the possible is minor, but the conclusion is not necessary. Then some of the minor would be true with general possibility, leading to a contradiction (خلف). This shows the conclusion must be necessary.
The reductio uses the second figure to derive 'some B is not J necessarily', which contradicts the original premises.
5
Resolve the sperm example
The example: 'Human sperm in the womb is possibly human' (ممكنة) and 'Every human is necessarily an animal' (ضرورية). If we naively conclude 'Sperm is necessarily an animal', that seems false. The resolution: the predicate of the minor ('human') refers to a future stage, not the present sperm. The major's subject ('human') refers to an actual human. The terms are not aligned in mode, so the rule does not apply.
Always check that the middle term is used in the same sense in both premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضرورية المطلقة والممكنة، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى بالاتفاق، وسواء كانت الضرورية هي الكبرى أو الصغرى، وسواء كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص لما حققناه قبل؛ ولأنه إذا كانت الضرورية هي الكبرى والممكنة هي الصغرى كما في قولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل) "ج" (بالإمكان) وإن شئت (بالإمكان العام أو الخاص) أو (الأخص) و(كل "ج" "أ" بالضرورة لو لم تكن النتيجة بالضرورة كل "ب" "أ") وإلا صدق (بعض "ب" "أ" بالإمكان العام وكان) كل "ج" "أ" بالضرورة) فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج" كما تعلمه في الثاني وهو خلف، وإن استبعد كون الأكبر ضرورياً للأصغر؛ لكون الأوسط غير ضروري للأصغر. فالوجه في حله ما سلف ولا يلزم على ما ذكرناه قول القائل (نطفة الإنسان في الرحم ممكن أن تكون إنساناً) و(كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة) فإنه يلزم منه صدق قولنا: (نطفة الإنسان في الرحم حيوان بالضرورة مع صدق المقدمتين؛ لأنا نقول المحمول على النطفة إنما هو تكونها في المستقبل إنساناً بجهة الإمكان، لا نفس الكون في الحال، والمحكوم عليه في الكبرى بالحيوان إنما هو الإنسان الكائن
And as for if the mixing is from the absolute necessary and the possible, the conclusion follows the mode of the major by agreement, whether the necessary is the major or the minor, and whether the possible is in the general sense, the specific, or the more specific, as we established before. Because if the necessary is the major and the possible is the minor, as in our saying in the first conjunction: 'Every J is possible' (whether general, specific, or more specific) and 'Every J is A by necessity', if the conclusion were not 'Every B is A by necessity', then 'Some B is A by general possibility' would be true, and 'Every J is A by necessity' would be false, so 'It is necessary that some B is not J' as you know from the second [figure], which is a contradiction. And even if it is improbable that the major is necessary for the minor because the middle is not necessary for the minor, the solution is as previously mentioned. And it is not necessary, based on what we have said, the statement of one who says: 'The human sperm in the womb is possibly a human' and 'Every human is necessarily an animal', for it would entail the truth of our saying: 'The human sperm in the womb is necessarily an animal' with the truth of the premises. Because we say: the predicate on the sperm is only its future becoming a human with possibility, not its actual existence in the present, and the subject of the animal predicate in the major is only the human being
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage examines the mixing of necessary absolute and possible premises. The author asserts that, by consensus, the conclusion follows the mode of the major premise, regardless of which premise is necessary and which is possible. This holds for all types of possibility (general, specific, more specific). A reductio argument is given: if the necessary is major and the possible is minor, and we suppose the conclusion is not necessary, a contradiction results. However, an objection is considered: it seems improbable that the major is necessary for the minor if the middle term is not necessary for the minor. The author dismisses this objection, referencing previous solutions. Finally, an example is analyzed: the statement 'Human sperm in the womb is possibly a human' and 'Every human is necessarily an animal' does not lead to 'The sperm is necessarily an animal' as a logical consequence, because the predicate on the sperm refers to its future possibility, not its immediate actuality. The major's subject is the actual human, not the sperm. Thus, the syllogism's terms must be properly aligned.

Key Terms
ممكنة (mumkina): A possible proposition, which may be in the general sense (al-mumkina al-'amma: not impossible), specific (al-mumkina al-khassa: contingent but not necessary), or more specific (al-mumkina al-akhass: with additional qualification).
ضرورية (daruriyya): A necessary proposition, expressing absolute necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mixing of necessary absolute and possible premises, what determines the mode of the conclusion?
A) The mode of the minor premise
B) The mode of the major premise
C) The mode of the middle term
D) It is always necessary

Key Takeaway
When premises have mixed modalities, the conclusion's modality is always that of the major premise, provided the terms are properly aligned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism in the first figure where the minor premise is possible (in the general sense) and the major premise is necessary. State the conclusion and explain why its modality is necessary, using the rule from this lesson.
?
Analyze the following argument using the sperm example's logic: 'Every fetus is potentially a human (possible). Every human is a rational animal (necessary). Therefore, every fetus is a rational animal (necessary).' Is this conclusion valid? Explain why or why not, referencing the need for term alignment.

General Rule for Mixing Necessary and Possible Propositions
General rule and reductio proof for mixing necessary and possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that the conclusion follows the major's modality when mixing necessary absolute and possible propositions and demonstrate its proof by reductio.

Core Idea
In mixed modal syllogisms, the major's modality rules the conclusion.

In this part of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq, the author establishes a fundamental rule for mixing necessary absolute (الضرورية المطلقة) and possible (الممكنة) propositions in categorical syllogisms. He states that the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise, regardless of which premise is necessary or possible, and regardless of the type of possible proposition. He then provides a rigorous proof using reductio ad absurdum (خلف). This lesson walks you through the rule and its logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The conclusion's modality follows the major premise when mixing necessary and possible; note the author's phrase 'فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى بالاتفاق'.
This rule holds whether necessary is major or minor.
2
Understand the scope
The rule applies for any type of possible (general, specific, most specific) as per 'سواء كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص'.
3
Examine the reductio proof
For the case where necessary is major and possible is minor, assume the opposite conclusion and derive a contradiction: if 'some ب is possibly أ' and 'every ج is necessarily أ' then 'some ب is not ج' contradicts given.
The author uses Arabic variables ج, ب, أ for terms.
4
Formalize the syllogism with variables
Write the syllogism: Minor: 'Every ج is ب (possibly)'; Major: 'Every ج is أ (necessarily)'; Conclusion: 'Every ب is أ (necessarily)'.
This is a third-figure syllogism.
5
Verify the reductio steps
Follow the author's logic: if the conclusion is not necessary, then the opposite (some ب is possibly not أ) leads to a contradiction using the premises.
The contradiction proves the conclusion must be necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضرورية المطلقة والممكنة، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى بالاتفاق، وسواء كانت الضرورية هي الكبرى أو الصغرى، وسواء كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص لما حققناه قبل؛ ولأنه إذا كانت الضرورية هي الكبرى والممكنة هي الصغرى كما في قولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل) "ج" (بالإمكان) وإن شئت (بالإمكان العام أو الخاص) أو (الأخص) و(كل "ج" "أ" بالضرورة لو لم تكن النتيجة بالضرورة كل "ب" "أ") وإلا صدق (بعض "ب" "أ" بالإمكان العام وكان) كل "ج" "أ" بالضرورة) فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج" كما تعلمه في الثاني وهو خلف، وإن استبعد كون الأكبر ضرورياً للأصغر؛ لكون الأوسط غير ضروري للأصغر. فالوجه في حله ما سلف ولا يلزم على ما ذكرناه قول القائل (نطفة الإنسان في الرحم ممكن أن تكون إنساناً) و(كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة) فإنه يلزم منه صدق قولنا: (نطفة الإنسان في الرحم حيوان بالضرورة مع صدق المقدمتين؛ لأنا نقول المحمول على النطفة إنما هو تكونها في المستقبل إنساناً بجهة الإمكان، لا نفس الكون في الحال، والمحكوم عليه في الكبرى بالحيوان إنما هو الإنسان الكائن فلا إنتاج؛ لاختلاف الحد الأوسط، حتى لو حكم في الكبرى بالحيوان بجهة الضرورة على ما هو ممكن أن يصير إنسانا وهو الحد الأوسط، كانت الكبرى كاذبة، وعلى كل تقدير فلا إنتاج، إما لاختلاف الحد الأوسط، وإما لكذب الكبرى. وأما إذا كانت المطلقة الضرورية هي الصغرى والممكنة هي الكبرى فلا خلاف في أن النتيجة تكون ممكنة بالمعنى الذي كانت الكبرى به ممكنة؛ لما حققناه وعليك باعتبار الخلف في كل ذلك بنفسك. وربما ظن أنه إذا كانت الكبرى ممكنة خاصة وهي سالبة أن النتيجة تكون مطلقة عامة، ولا حاصل له ولا الفرق بينها وبين الموجبة لما حققناه، وعلى هذا يكون الحال في ما بقي من الاقترانات
As for if the mixing is from the necessary absolute and the possible, the conclusion follows the modality of the major by agreement, whether the necessary is the major or the minor, and whether the possible is in the general sense or the specific or the most specific, due to what we verified before; and because if the necessary is the major and the possible is the minor, as in our saying in the first syllogism 'Every ج is possibly (or generally possible or specific or most specific) and Every ج is أ necessarily, if the conclusion is not necessarily every ب is أ, otherwise it would be true that some ب is أ in the general possible, and every ج is أ necessarily, so necessarily some ب is not ج as you know in the second, and that is a reductio; and if it is deemed unlikely that the major is necessary for the minor because the middle is not necessary for the minor, then the way to resolve it is as before. And what we mentioned does not entail the saying of one who says: 'The human sperm in the womb is possibly human, and every human is necessarily an animal', for it would entail the truth of our saying 'The human sperm in the womb is necessarily an animal' while both premises are true; for we say: The predicate on the sperm is only its becoming human in the future in the mode of possibility, not the actual existence at present, and what is judged in the major as animal is only the existing human, so there is no production due to the difference of the middle term; even if one judged in the major with animal necessarily on that which is possible to become human, and it is the middle term, then the major would be false. In any case, there is no production, either because of difference of the middle term or because of falsehood of the major. As for when the absolute necessary is the minor and the possible is the major, there is no disagreement that the conclusion is possible in the sense that the major was possible, due to what we verified, and you must consider the reductio in all that yourself. And perhaps one thought that if the major is specific possible and negative, the conclusion would be general absolute, but there is no basis for it, and there is no difference between it and the affirmative due to what we verified. And thus is the case in the remaining syllogisms.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for mixing necessary absolute (الضرورية المطلقة) and possible (الممكنة) propositions in categorical syllogisms. The author states the general principle: the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise, regardless of which premise is necessary or possible, and regardless of the particular type of possible (general, specific, or most specific). He then proves this using a reductio ad absurdum (خلف). Specifically, if the necessary is major and possible is minor, and we assume the opposite conclusion (i.e., not necessary), we derive a contradiction. The author addresses a potential objection: the example of human sperm (نطفة الإنسان) being possibly human, and every human necessarily being an animal, which would seem to imply the sperm is necessarily an animal. He resolves this by pointing out that the predicate in the minor is about future possibility, not actual present existence, while the major predicate 'animal' applies only to actual humans; thus the middle term differs, preventing a valid syllogism. He further clarifies that even if one tried to adjust the major to apply to the possible, the major would become false. For the case where the necessary is minor and possible is major, the conclusion is simply possible in the same sense as the major. Finally, he dismisses a mistaken claim that a negative specific possible major yields a general absolute conclusion, asserting there is no difference between affirmative and negative in this respect.

Key Terms
الضرورية المطلقة
الممكنة
الخلف
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The minor premise
B) The major premise
C) The modal type of the minor
D) Neither premise

Key Takeaway
In mixed modal syllogisms, the conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise, as proven by reductio.

?
