Resolving Apparent Counterexample and Remaining Rules
Resolving an apparent counterexample and stating the remaining rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of differing middle terms in modal syllogisms and apply the rule that when the necessary is minor and the possible is major, the conclusion is possible.

Core Idea
A valid modal syllogism requires a univocal middle term; apparent counterexamples often hide a shift in meaning.

In this continuation, the author defends his rule against an apparent counterexample involving human sperm and animality. He shows that the apparent inference fails because the middle term 'human' is used in different senses (potential vs actual). He then states the rule for the case when the necessary is minor and the possible is major, and dismisses a mistaken claim about negative specific possible majors. This lesson sharpens your ability to diagnose invalidity due to equivocation in modal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Present the apparent counterexample
The sperm example: 'The sperm is possibly human; every human is necessarily an animal; therefore the sperm is necessarily an animal.' This seems to violate the rule.
2
Identify the source of invalidity
The middle term 'human' differs in meaning: in the minor it refers to potential future human, in the major to an actual human. Thus the middle term is not identical.
The author says: 'لاختلاف الحد الأوسط'.
3
Check alternative repairs
Even if the major is adjusted to apply to 'what is possibly human', it becomes false because not every possible human is actually an animal.
Thus no valid syllogism possible.
4
State the rule for necessary minor and possible major
When the necessary is minor and possible is major, the conclusion is possible in the same sense as the major.
The author says: 'فلا خلاف في أن النتيجة تكون ممكنة بالمعنى الذي كانت الكبرى به ممكنة'.
5
Note the dismissal of a mistaken claim
Some thought a negative specific possible major yields a general absolute conclusion, but the author rejects this, affirming no difference between affirmative and negative.
This applies to the remaining syllogisms as well.

As for if the mixing is from the necessary absolute and the possible, the conclusion follows the modality of the major by agreement, whether the necessary is the major or the minor, and whether the possible is in the general sense or the specific or the most specific, due to what we verified before; and because if the necessary is the major and the possible is the minor, as in our saying in the first syllogism 'Every ج is possibly (or generally possible or specific or most specific) and Every ج is أ necessarily, if the conclusion is not necessarily every ب is أ, otherwise it would be true that some ب is أ in the general possible, and every ج is أ necessarily, so necessarily some ب is not ج as you know in the second, and that is a reductio; and if it is deemed unlikely that the major is necessary for the minor because the middle is not necessary for the minor, then the way to resolve it is as before. Mixing Necessary Conditional and Possible Propositions
Author classifies types of mixing necessary conditional with possible and shows non-productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a modal syllogism mixing a necessary conditional major with a possible minor is productive by checking the sharing of the middle term.

Core Idea
Possibility does not guarantee perpetuity: the middle term may not be shared.

In modal syllogistics, mixing a necessary conditional proposition with a possible proposition requires careful attention to the condition of perpetuity. The author examines the case where the necessary conditional has a condition with the subject, and the possible is general. This lesson will show why such a syllogism is non-productive, and why a proposed alternative conclusion fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Extract the two premises from the Arabic example: minor: 'Every ب is ج in the general possible'; major: 'Every what is ج always with the perpetuity of the subject is أ as long as it is ج'.
The example uses the first syllogism (first figure) with two universal affirmatives.
2
Determine the modal qualifications
Note the modality: the minor is 'general possible' (إمكان عام), while the major is a 'necessary conditional' (ضرورية مشروطة) with the condition 'always with the perpetuity of the subject' (دائماً بدوام الموضوع).
The major's predicate holds necessarily as long as the subject exists.
3
Find the middle term
Identify the term common to both premises: in this case, ج is the middle term. The minor says ب is ج possibly; the major says anything that is ج necessarily is أ.
The middle term is ج.
4
Check whether the middle term is shared
The major requires ج to be always (دائماً) for the subject of the major (which is implicitly ب in the context of the first figure). However, the minor only asserts that ج is possible for ب — not that it holds always. Therefore, the middle term is not shared in the same mode across both premises.
The author argues: it does not follow that protože ج is possible for ب in every respect that it is perpetual for ب.
5
Conclude on productivity
Since the middle term is not shared, the syllogism is non-productive (غير منتج). The author also refutes the claim that it yields a general possible conclusion, because possibility of existence does not imply possibility of permanence.
The counterexample: a heavy object moving from point B to point C — it is possible for it to be at C at some time, but not necessarily always.

وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضرورية المشروطة والممكنة فإما أن تكون الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى العام فالضرورة المشروطة إما أن تكون مشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع أو المحمول أو مشروطة بوقت، فإن كان الشرط مع الموضوع فإما أن يكون من القسم الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع فإن كان من القسم الأول فإما أن تكون الممكنة صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل "ب" "ج" بالإمكان العام) و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج" فهو غير منتج لجواز أن يكون "ج" الثابت لـ "ب" بالإمكان غير دائم بدوام "ب" فإنه لا يلزم من كون ("ج" ممكناً لـ "ب") بكل اعتبار أن يكون دائماً لـ "ب" وما حكم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو موصوف بـ دائما به ج بدوام الموضوع، فلا يكون الحد الأوسط مشتركاً ، وإذا لم يكن مشتركاً فلا إنتاج. وقد قيل : أنه ينتج ممكنة عامة؛ لأن "ج" وإن لم يثبت بالفعل دوامه لـ "ب" لكنه ممكن فأمكن، واحتمل أن يكون ذلك ضرورياً، والعام لهما هو الممكن العام. وليس بحق فإنه كما لم يثبت كون "ج" دائماً لـ "ب" بالفعل، لم يثبت إمكان دوامه لـ "ب" بل جاز في نفس الأمر أن لا يكون دوامه ممكناً لـ "ب" وإن كان "ج" لـ "ب" ممكناً، كما لو قيل (كل) ثقيل فإنه يتحرك من نقطة "ب" إلى نقطة "ج")
And as for if the mixing is from the necessary conditional and the possible, then the possible is either in the general sense or the specific or the most specific. If it is in the general sense, then the necessary conditional is either conditional with a condition with the subject or the predicate or conditional with a time. If the condition is with the subject, then it is either from the first division or the second or the third or the fourth. If it is from the first division, then the possible is either minor or major. If it is minor, as in our saying in the first syllogism: 'Every ب is ج in the general possible' and 'Every what is ج always with the perpetuity of the subject is أ as long as it is ج' then it is non-productive because it is possible that the ج which is affirmed of ب in the possible mode is not perpetual with the perpetuity of ب; for it does not follow from the fact that ج is possible for ب in every respect that it is perpetual for ب, and what is judged as أ is only described as always being ج with the perpetuity of the subject, so the middle term is not shared. And when it is not shared, there is no production. And it has been said that it produces a general possible, because ج even if its perpetuity for ب is not actually established, yet it is possible, so it is possible, and it is possible that it is necessary, and the common between them is the general possible. But it is not correct, because just as the perpetuity of ج for ب is not actually established, the possibility of its perpetuity for ب is also not established; rather, it is possible in the matter itself that its perpetuity is not possible for ب, even if ج is possible for ب, as if it is said: 'Every heavy thing moves from point ب to point ج.'
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage examines the mixing of necessary conditional (الضرورية المشروطة) and possible (الممكنة) propositions. The author breaks down the types: first, the possible may be general, specific, or most specific; the necessary conditional may have a condition with the subject, with the predicate, or with a time. He then focuses on the case where the condition is with the subject and falls under the first division, and the possible is the minor premise. He provides an example: 'Every ب is ج in the general possible' and 'Everything that is ج always as long as the subject persists is أ as long as it is ج'. He argues that this syllogism is non-productive because the middle term (ج) may not be constant: the possible ج for ب does not guarantee that ج persists for ب in the same way required by the major. Thus the middle term is not shared. He then refutes a claim that such a syllogism yields a general possible conclusion, because one cannot assume the possibility of permanence simply from the possibility of existence; there may be no possibility of permanence. He gives an example: a heavy object moving from point B to point C — it is possible for it to be at C at some time, but not necessarily always. The passage is cut off but illustrates the argument.

Key Terms
الضرورية المشروطة (al-daruriyya al-mashruta): A necessary proposition qualified by a condition, e.g., with the subject or predicate or time. [Note: This is a specialized modal notion; not all logicians treat it as a distinct type.]
دائماً بدوام الموضوع (da'iman bi-dawam al-mawdu'): Always with the perpetuity of the subject — a condition that the predicate holds as long as the subject exists. [Note: A technical phrase in modal syllogistics; stable in meaning.]
غير منتج (ghayr muntij): Non-productive; does not yield a valid conclusion. [Note: Standard term; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the syllogism with a necessary conditional major (condition with subject) and a general possible minor fail?
A) The minor is false.
B) The major is false.
C) The middle term may not be constant or shared.
D) The conclusion is a contradiction.

Key Takeaway
A possible minor premise does not guarantee the permanence required by a necessary conditional major, so the middle term is not shared and the syllogism is non-productive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every rational being (عاقل) is morally accountable (مكلف) by necessity of the subject's existence (i.e., always as long as they exist). Every human (إنسان) is rational in the general possible sense.' Determine whether this syllogism is productive. Explain your reasoning by checking the sharing of the middle term, following the author's method from the passage.
?
Choose a different combination: the possible is specific (خاص) instead of general. Thinking about the possibilities, would the syllogism be productive? Why or why not? (Note: the passage does not cover this fully, but you can reason based on the principle.)

Mixed Modality: Necessary Minor and Possible Major
Rule for conclusion modality when minor is necessary and major is possible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a first-figure syllogism when the minor is absolutely necessary and the major is possible.

Core Idea
When minor is necessary and major is possible, the conclusion follows the major's mode.

In this lesson, we examine a fundamental rule for mixed modal syllogisms in the first figure. The passage from our text states clearly that when the minor premise is absolutely necessary and the major is possible, the conclusion is possible, taking the same modality as the major. This rule is crucial for evaluating arguments in theological and legal reasoning where premises may have different modal strengths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal status of each premise
Look at the minor premise: is it absolutely necessary (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa)? Look at the major: is it possible (mumkina)? The passage specifies the case where the minor is absolutely necessary and the major is possible (general, specific, or most specific).
2
Apply the rule for mixed modality
If the minor is absolutely necessary and the major is possible, the conclusion will be possible in the same sense as the major. Do not be misled by quantifiers or negation; the modality of the conclusion depends solely on the major's modality.
The text refutes a mistaken view that a particular possible major with negation yields an absolute general conclusion.
3
Verify with a concrete Islamic example
Construct an example: Minor: 'Every human is necessarily a living being' (necessary). Major: 'Every living being is possibly mortal' (possible). Conclusion: 'Every human is possibly mortal' (possible). Check that the conclusion is indeed possible.
The minor's necessity does not transfer; the conclusion is possible.

الكبرى. وأما إذا كانت المطلقة الضرورية هي الصغرى والممكنة هي الكبرى فلا خلاف في أن النتيجة تكون ممكنة بالمعنى الذي كانت الكبرى به ممكنة؛ لما حققناه وعليك باعتبار الخلف في كل ذلك بنفسك. وربما ظن أنه فإما أن يكون من القسم الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع فإن كان من القسم الأول فإما أن تكون الممكنة صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل "ب" "ج" بالإمكان العام) و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج" فهو غير منتج لجواز أن يكون "ج" الثابت لـ "ب" بالإمكان غير دائم بدوام "ب" فإنه لا يلزم من كون ("ج" ممكناً لـ "ب") بكل اعتبار أن يكون دائماً لـ "ب" وما حكم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو موصوف بـ دائما به ج بدوام الموضوع، فلا يكون الحد الأوسط مشتركاً، وإذا لم يكن مشتركاً فلا إنتاج. وقد قيل: أنه ينتج ممكنة عامة؛ لأن "ج" وإن لم يثبت بالفعل دوامه لـ "ب" لكنه ممكن فأمكن، واحتمل أن يكون ذلك ضرورياً، والعام لهما هو الممكن العام. وليس بحق فإنه كما لم يثبت كون "ج" دائماً لـ "ب" بالفعل، لم يثبت إمكان دوامه لـ "ب" بل جاز في نفس الأمر أن لا يكون دوامه ممكناً لـ "ب" وإن كان "ج" لـ "ب" ممكناً، كما لو قيل (كل) ثقيل فإنه يتحرك من نقطة "ب" إلى نقطة "ج" وليس دوام حركته من نقطة "ب" إلى نقطة "ج" ممكناً ضرورة التناهي، وعلى هذا يكون الحكم وإن كانت الضرورية المشروطة بالاعتبار الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع الثاني فلكون المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو ما هو موصوف بـ "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع، وإن لم يكن دائماً بدوامه. وأما الثالث والرابع فلكون المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو ما هو موصوف بـ "ج" لا ما دام الموضوع موجوداً، وإذ ذاك فالحد الأوسط لا يلزم أن يكون مشتركاً، لجواز أن يكون "ج" الثابت لـ "ب" وإن كان بجهة الإمكان العام دائماً لـ "ب" فلا يكون داخلاً تحت الكبرى، وعلى هذا يكون الحكم إذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة خاصة أو أخصية. وأما إن كانت الممكنة كبرى أو المشروطة صغرى - ولا تبال بأي اعتبار كانت المشروطة أو الممكنة - فالإنتاج واقع، والنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى؛ لما حققناه قبل، وعليك أن تعتبر أمثلة جميع هذه الأقسام بنفسك، وكذلك البيان بالخلف. وأما إن كانت المشروطة مشروطة بشرط مأخوذ مع المحمول أو بشرط وقت، وسواء كان معيناً أو مبهماً، وسواء كانت المشروطة كبرى أو صغرى، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى. وكذلك الحكم في ما إذا كان الاختلاط من الممكنات بعضها مع بعض أيضاً؛ لما حققناه، وعليك باعتبار أمثلة ذلك بنفسك. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضروريات المشروطة بعضها مع بعض، فلنفرض الكلام أولاً في الاختلاط من القسم الأول من الضروريات المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع، ومن كل قسم من أقسام المشروطة، ثم كذلك على الترتيب. فنقول: إذا كان الاختلاط بين القسم الأول والثاني من المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع فإما أن يكون القسم الأول صغرى أو كبرى فإن كان صغرى فهو كقولنا في الاقتران الأول كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" لا ما دام "ج")، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة
Thus there is no production; due to the difference of the middle term, even if in the major premise one judges 'animal' with the mode of necessity upon what is possible to become a human, which is the middle term, the major would be false. And in any case, there is no production: either due to the difference of the middle term, or due to the falsity of the major. As for when the absolute necessary is the minor and the possible is the major, there is no disagreement that the conclusion will be possible in the sense that the major was possible; due to what we have verified, and you must consider the reduction (khalaf) in all that yourself. And perhaps one might think that if the major is particularly possible and negative, the conclusion would be absolute general, but there is no benefit to that, nor the distinction between it and the affirmative due to what we have verified. And thus is the case for the remaining conjunctions. As for if the mixing is of conditional necessary and possible, then the possible is either general, specific, or most specific. If it is general, then the conditional necessary is either conditional upon a condition with the subject, the predicate, or conditional upon a time. If the condition is with the subject, then it is either of the first, second, third, or fourth division. If it is of the first division, then either the possible is minor or major. If it is minor, as we say in the first conjunction: 'Every B is J with general possibility' and 'Everything that is J always with the permanence of the subject is A as long as it is J' – then it is unproductive because it is possible that the J that is established for B with possibility is not permanent with the permanence of B. For it does not follow from the fact that J is possible for B in every respect that it is permanent for B. And what is judged with A is only that which is described as always J with the permanence of the subject. Hence, the middle term is not common, and if it is not common, there is no production. And it was said that it produces a general possible because J, even if its permanence for B is not actually established, yet it is possible, so it is possible, and it is possible that that is necessary, and what is common to both is the general possible. But that is not correct, because just as the permanence of J for B is not actually established, so its possibility of permanence for B is not established. Rather, it is possible in the very matter that its permanence is not possible for B even though J is possible for B, as if it is said: every heavy thing moves from point B to point J, and the permanence of its motion from point B to point J is not possible due to the necessity of finitude. And thus is the judgment even if the conditional necessary is of the second, third, or fourth [division] – the second because what is judged with A is only what is described as J always with the permanence of the subject, even if not always with its permanence. As for the third and fourth, because what is judged with A is only what is described as J, not as long as the subject exists. And then the middle term need not be common, because it is possible that J, even if established for B with the mode of general possibility, is always for B, and thus it does not fall under the major. And thus is the judgment if the minor is specific or most specific possibility. As for if the possible is major or the conditional necessary is minor – and do not mind by which consideration the conditional or the possible is – then production occurs, and the conclusion follows the mode of the major; due to what we have verified before. And you must consider examples of all these divisions yourself, and likewise the proof by reduction (khalaf). As for if the conditional necessary is conditional upon a condition taken with the predicate or upon a condition of time, whether definite or indefinite, and whether the conditional is major or minor, the conclusion follows the mode of the major. And likewise the judgment in the case when the mixing is of possibles with each other; due to what we have verified, and you must consider examples of that yourself. As for if the mixing is of conditional necessary premises with each other, let us first posit the discussion on the mixing of the first division of conditional necessary with a condition with the subject, and from each division of the conditional, then likewise in order. So we say: if the mixing is between the first and second division of the conditional with a condition with the subject, then either the first division is minor or major. If it is minor, it is like our saying in the first conjunction: 'Everything that is B always with the permanence of the subject is J as long as it is B' and 'Everything that is J always with the permanence of the subject is A not as long as it is J' – so the conclusion follows the mode
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of mixed modality syllogisms in the first figure (al-iqtiran al-awwal) of categorical syllogisms. The author, likely a commentator on the logic tradition, explains the conditions under which a conclusion follows when premises have different modal qualifications: necessary (ḍarūriyya) and possible (mumkina), including absolute necessary (muṭlaqa ḍarūriyya) and conditional necessary (ḍarūriyya mashrūṭa). The opening line discusses a case where mixing necessary and possible leads to no conclusion (lā intāj) because the middle term differs or the major premise becomes false. Then the author clarifies that if the minor is absolutely necessary and the major is possible, the conclusion is possible, following the mode of the major. He refutes a mistaken view that a negative particular possible major yields an absolute general conclusion. Next, he delves into the more complex mixing of conditional necessary (e.g., 'always with the permanence of the subject') with possible (general, specific, or most specific). He analyzes various combinations: when the possible is minor and the conditional necessary is major in the first division, he shows why no conclusion is produced because the middle term is not common. He gives an example using heavy objects and motion to illustrate that possibility of permanence does not entail actual permanence. He then discusses cases where the possible is major (or conditional necessary is minor) and states that production occurs, with the conclusion following the mode of the major. He also covers conditional necessary with conditions on the predicate or time, and mixing possibles with each other. Finally, he begins to analyze mixing among different divisions of conditional necessary (first and second) with conditions on the subject. Throughout, the author emphasizes that the conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise when the minor is possible or when the conditional necessary is minor, but when the conditional necessary is major and the possible is minor in certain divisions, the middle term may not be common, preventing a conclusion. The commentary encourages the reader to test examples and use proof by reduction (khalaf). This passage is a classic exercise in modal syllogistic, illustrating the precision required in evaluating arguments with temporal and modal conditions.

Conditional Necessary Major and Possible Minor: When Production Fails
Why a conditional necessary major with a possible minor in the first division does not produce a conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a mixed modal syllogism with a conditional necessary major and a possible minor in the first division is unproductive due to lack of middle term commonality.

Core Idea
A possible minor does not guarantee the permanence required by a conditional necessary major.

This lesson tackles a more complex scenario: a conditional necessary premise (e.g., 'always with the permanence of the subject') combined with a possible premise. The author demonstrates that when the possible is minor and the conditional necessary is major, the syllogism fails because the middle term is not truly common. We will walk through the reasoning and refute a counterproposal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their types
The minor is a general possible proposition: 'Every B is J with general possibility'. The major is a conditional necessary: 'Everything that is J always with the permanence of the subject is A as long as it is J'. Note the condition in the major: it applies only to J that is permanent with the subject.
2
Check if the middle term is common
The middle term is 'J'. In the minor, J is only possibly true for B; it does not assert permanence. In the major, J is required to be permanent with the subject. Therefore, the J in the minor may not satisfy the condition of the major, so the middle term is not truly common. Hence, no conclusion follows.
The author gives an example: a heavy object moving from point B to point J; the permanence of that motion is impossible due to finitude, even though the motion itself is possible.
3
Evaluate the mistaken view
Some claimed that a general possible conclusion could be drawn because J is possible, so its permanence could be possible. But the author refutes this: possibility of J does not entail possibility of its permanence, as shown by the heavy object example.
This highlights the need to distinguish between possibility of a thing and possibility of its permanence.

دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج" فهو غير منتج لجواز أن يكون "ج" الثابت لـ "ب" بالإمكان غير دائم بدوام "ب" فإنه لا يلزم من كون ("ج" ممكناً لـ "ب") بكل اعتبار أن يكون دائماً لـ "ب" وما حكم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو موصوف بـ دائما به ج بدوام الموضوع، فلا يكون الحد الأوسط مشتركاً، وإذا لم يكن مشتركاً فلا إنتاج. وقد قيل: أنه ينتج ممكنة عامة؛ لأن "ج" وإن لم يثبت بالفعل دوامه لـ "ب" لكنه ممكن فأمكن، واحتمل أن يكون ذلك ضرورياً، والعام لهما هو الممكن العام. وليس بحق فإنه كما لم يثبت كون "ج" دائماً لـ "ب" بالفعل، لم يثبت إمكان دوامه لـ "ب" بل جاز في نفس الأمر أن لا يكون دوامه ممكناً لـ "ب" وإن كان "ج" لـ "ب" ممكناً، كما لو قيل (كل) ثقيل فإنه يتحرك من نقطة "ب" إلى نقطة "ج" وليس دوام حركته من نقطة "ب" إلى نقطة "ج" ممكناً ضرورة التناهي، وعلى هذا يكون الحكم وإن كانت الضرورية المشروطة بالاعتبار الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع الثاني فلكون المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو ما هو موصوف بـ "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع، وإن لم يكن دائماً بدوامه. وأما الثالث والرابع فلكون المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو ما هو موصوف بـ "ج" لا ما دام الموضوع موجوداً، وإذ ذاك فالحد الأوسط لا يلزم أن يكون مشتركاً، لجواز أن يكون "ج" الثابت لـ "ب" وإن كان بجهة الإمكان العام دائماً لـ "ب" فلا يكون داخلاً تحت الكبرى، وعلى هذا يكون الحكم إذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة خاصة أو أخصية. وأما إن كانت الممكنة كبرى أو المشروطة صغرى - ولا تبال بأي اعتبار كانت المشروطة أو الممكنة - فالإنتاج واقع، والنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى؛ لما حققناه قبل، وعليك أن تعتبر أمثلة جميع هذه الأقسام بنفسك، وكذلك البيان بالخلف. وأما إن كانت المشروطة مشروطة بشرط مأخوذ مع المحمول أو بشرط وقت، وسواء كان معيناً أو مبهماً، وسواء كانت المشروطة كبرى أو صغرى، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى. وكذلك الحكم في ما إذا كان الاختلاط من الممكنات بعضها مع بعض أيضاً؛ لما حققناه، وعليك باعتبار أمثلة ذلك بنفسك. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضروريات المشروطة بعضها مع بعض، فلنفرض الكلام أولاً في الاختلاط من القسم الأول من الضروريات المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع، ومن كل قسم من أقسام المشروطة، ثم كذلك على الترتيب. فنقول: إذا كان الاختلاط بين القسم الأول والثاني من المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع فإما أن يكون القسم الأول صغرى أو كبرى فإن كان صغرى فهو كقولنا في الاقتران الأول كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" لا ما دام "ج")، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة
Thus there is no production; due to the difference of the middle term, even if in the major premise one judges 'animal' with the mode of necessity upon what is possible to become a human, which is the middle term, the major would be false. And in any case, there is no production: either due to the difference of the middle term, or due to the falsity of the major. As for when the absolute necessary is the minor and the possible is the major, there is no disagreement that the conclusion will be possible in the sense that the major was possible; due to what we have verified, and you must consider the reduction (khalaf) in all that yourself. And perhaps one might think that if the major is particularly possible and negative, the conclusion would be absolute general, but there is no benefit to that, nor the distinction between it and the affirmative due to what we have verified. And thus is the case for the remaining conjunctions. As for if the mixing is of conditional necessary and possible, then the possible is either general, specific, or most specific. If it is general, then the conditional necessary is either conditional upon a condition with the subject, the predicate, or conditional upon a time. If the condition is with the subject, then it is either of the first, second, third, or fourth division. If it is of the first division, then either the possible is minor or major. If it is minor, as we say in the first conjunction: 'Every B is J with general possibility' and 'Everything that is J always with the permanence of the subject is A as long as it is J' – then it is unproductive because it is possible that the J that is established for B with possibility is not permanent with the permanence of B. For it does not follow from the fact that J is possible for B in every respect that it is permanent for B. And what is judged with A is only that which is described as always J with the permanence of the subject. Hence, the middle term is not common, and if it is not common, there is no production. And it was said that it produces a general possible because J, even if its permanence for B is not actually established, yet it is possible, so it is possible, and it is possible that that is necessary, and what is common to both is the general possible. But that is not correct, because just as the permanence of J for B is not actually established, so its possibility of permanence for B is not established. Rather, it is possible in the very matter that its permanence is not possible for B even though J is possible for B, as if it is said: every heavy thing moves from point B to point J, and the permanence of its motion from point B to point J is not possible due to the necessity of finitude. And thus is the judgment even if the conditional necessary is of the second, third, or fourth [division] – the second because what is judged with A is only what is described as J always with the permanence of the subject, even if not always with its permanence. As for the third and fourth, because what is judged with A is only what is described as J, not as long as the subject exists. And then the middle term need not be common, because it is possible that J, even if established for B with the mode of general possibility, is always for B, and thus it does not fall under the major. And thus is the judgment if the minor is specific or most specific possibility. As for if the possible is major or the conditional necessary is minor – and do not mind by which consideration the conditional or the possible is – then production occurs, and the conclusion follows the mode of the major; due to what we have verified before. And you must consider examples of all these divisions yourself, and likewise the proof by reduction (khalaf). As for if the conditional necessary is conditional upon a condition taken with the predicate or upon a condition of time, whether definite or indefinite, and whether the conditional is major or minor, the conclusion follows the mode of the major. And likewise the judgment in the case when the mixing is of possibles with each other; due to what we have verified, and you must consider examples of that yourself. As for if the mixing is of conditional necessary premises with each other, let us first posit the discussion on the mixing of the first division of conditional necessary with a condition with the subject, and from each division of the conditional, then likewise in order. So we say: if the mixing is between the first and second division of the conditional with a condition with the subject, then either the first division is minor or major. If it is minor, it is like our saying in the first conjunction: 'Everything that is B always with the permanence of the subject is J as long as it is B' and 'Everything that is J always with the permanence of the subject is A not as long as it is J' – so the conclusion follows the mode
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of mixed modality syllogisms in the first figure (al-iqtiran al-awwal) of categorical syllogisms. The author, likely a commentator on the logic tradition, explains the conditions under which a conclusion follows when premises have different modal qualifications: necessary (ḍarūriyya) and possible (mumkina), including absolute necessary (muṭlaqa ḍarūriyya) and conditional necessary (ḍarūriyya mashrūṭa). The opening line discusses a case where mixing necessary and possible leads to no conclusion (lā intāj) because the middle term differs or the major premise becomes false. Then the author clarifies that if the minor is absolutely necessary and the major is possible, the conclusion is possible, following the mode of the major. He refutes a mistaken view that a negative particular possible major yields an absolute general conclusion. Next, he delves into the more complex mixing of conditional necessary (e.g., 'always with the permanence of the subject') with possible (general, specific, or most specific). He analyzes various combinations: when the possible is minor and the conditional necessary is major in the first division, he shows why no conclusion is produced because the middle term is not common. He gives an example using heavy objects and motion to illustrate that possibility of permanence does not entail actual permanence. He then discusses cases where the possible is major (or conditional necessary is minor) and states that production occurs, with the conclusion following the mode of the major. He also covers conditional necessary with conditions on the predicate or time, and mixing possibles with each other. Finally, he begins to analyze mixing among different divisions of conditional necessary (first and second) with conditions on the subject. Throughout, the author emphasizes that the conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise when the minor is possible or when the conditional necessary is minor, but when the conditional necessary is major and the possible is minor in certain divisions, the middle term may not be common, preventing a conclusion. The commentary encourages the reader to test examples and use proof by reduction (khalaf). This passage is a classic exercise in modal syllogistic, illustrating the precision required in evaluating arguments with temporal and modal conditions.

Checkpoint
Why is the syllogism 'Every B is possibly J' and 'Everything that is J always with permanence is A' unproductive?
A) The minor is false
B) The major is false
C) The middle term is not common
D) The conclusion would be necessary

Key Takeaway
A possible minor cannot be combined with a conditional necessary major that requires permanence, because the middle term lacks commonality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: Minor: 'Every new financial transaction is possibly a form of riba' (possible). Major: 'Every form of riba is necessarily forbidden with permanence as riba' (conditional necessary). Is this a productive syllogism? Explain why or why not using the concept of middle term commonality.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
When Possible Major or Conditional Necessary Minor: Production and Modality
Rule for production and conclusion modality when possible is major or conditional necessary is minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that in a mixed modal syllogism, when the possible premise is the major or the conditional necessary is the minor, production occurs and the conclusion follows the major's modality.

Core Idea
In production, the conclusion's modality always matches the major premise.

After analyzing cases where production fails, the author now presents the positive rules: when the possible is the major premise or when the conditional necessary is the minor, the syllogism is productive and the conclusion takes the modality of the major. This holds even when the conditional necessary has conditions on the predicate or time. Let's understand this general principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify which premise is major and which is minor
In the first figure, the major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion. Determine whether the major is possible or conditional necessary, and similarly for the minor.
2
Apply the production rule
If the major is possible (any kind) or if the minor is conditional necessary (any kind), the syllogism is productive. The conclusion's modality follows the major's modality.
The author says: 'do not mind by which consideration the conditional or the possible is'.
3
Consider special conditions on the conditional necessary
The rule applies whether the conditional necessary has a condition with the subject, predicate, or time. The conclusion still follows the major's modality.
This covers the remaining cases discussed in the passage.

General Rule: Conclusion Follows the Major's Modality
General rule for mixing conditional and possible premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the general rule for mixing conditional and possible premises in a first-figure categorical syllogism to determine if a conclusion is produced and its modality.

Core Idea
When mixing, if the major is possible or the minor conditional, the conclusion mirrors the major's modality.

The author establishes a foundational principle: in the first figure, whenever the major premise is a possible proposition or the minor premise is a conditional (regardless of its subtype), the syllogism produces a conclusion whose modality follows that of the major premise. This rule simplifies modal reasoning by tying the conclusion's force to the major premise's modal character.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal types of the premises
Read the premises and determine whether each is a possible (mumkin) or conditional (mashrūṭah) proposition. The conditional may have various conditions (e.g., time, subject perpetuity).
The passage uses 'mumkinah' for possible and 'mashrūṭah' for conditional.
2
Check which premise is conditional or possible
Apply the rule: if the major premise is possible, or the minor premise is conditional (even if the major is not possible), then the syllogism will produce a conclusion.
The author states: 'if the possible is major or the conditional is minor – and do not mind which consideration the conditional or possible is – then production occurs.'
3
Determine the conclusion's modality
When production occurs, the conclusion's modality is the same as the major premise's modality. For example, if the major is a necessary conditional, the conclusion will be necessary conditional as well.
The text says: 'the conclusion follows the mode of the major' (النتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى).
4
Verify with a concrete example
Construct an example from Islamic law: Major: 'Every intoxicant is haram as long as it intoxicates' (conditional). Minor: 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' (non-modal universal). According to the rule, production occurs and the conclusion is 'Every nabidh is haram as long as it intoxicates', matching the major's modality.
The author encourages the reader to consider examples and provide proof by reduction (al-bayān bi-l-khalf).

وليس دوام حركته من نقطة "ب" إلى نقطة "ج" ممكناً ضرورة التناهي، وعلى هذا يكون الحكم وإن كانت الضرورية المشروطة بالاعتبار الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع فلكون المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو ما هو موصوف بـ "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع، وإن لم يكن دائماً بدوامه.
وأما الثالث والرابع فلكون المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" إنما هو ما هو موصوف بـ "أ" لا ما دام الموضوع موجوداً، وإذ ذاك فالحد الأوسط لا يلزم أن يكون مشتركاً، لجواز أن يكون "ج" الثابت لـ "ب" وإن كان بجهة الإمكان العام دائماً لـ "ب" فلا يكون داخلاً تحت الكبرى، وعلى هذا يكون الحكم إذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة خاصة أو أخصية.
وأما إن كانت الممكنة كبرى أو المشروطة صغرى - ولا تبال بأي اعتبار كانت المشروطة أو الممكنة - فالإنتاج واقع، والنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى؛ لما حققناه قبل، وعليك أن تعتبر أمثلة جميع هذه الأقسام بنفسك، وكذلك البيان بالخلف. وأما إن كانت المشروطة مشروطة بشرط مأخوذ مع المحمول أو بشرط وقت، وسواء كان معيناً أو مبهماً، وسواء كانت المشروطة كبرى أو صغرى، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى.
وكذلك الحكم في ما إذا كان الاختلاط من الممكنات بعضها مع بعض أيضاً؛ لما حققناه، وعليك باعتبار أمثلة ذلك بنفسك.
وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضروريات المشروطة بعضها مع بعض، فلنفرض الكلام أولاً في الاختلاط من القسم الأول من الضروريات المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع، ومن كل قسم من أقسام المشروطة، ثم كذلك على الترتيب.
فنقول: إذا كان الاختلاط بين القسم الأول والثاني من المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع فإما أن يكون القسم الأول صغرى أو كبرى فإن كان صغرى فهو كقولنا في الاقتران الأول كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" لا ما دام "ج")، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى وهي (كل "ب" "أ" لا دائماً لضرورة أنه محكوم به على كل ما هو "ج" دائماً لا ما دام "ج" و "ب" "ج" دائماً فكان "ب" "أ" لا دائماً).
وأما إن كان الأمر بالعكس أي أن القسم الأول كبرى والثاني صغرى كقولنا: (كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" لا دائماً) و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج") فلا إنتاج ; لعدم الاشتراك في الحد الأوسط، إذ المحكوم عليه بـ "أ" هو ("ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع) و"ج" المحكوم به على "ب" غير دائم بدوامها.
وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين القسم الأول والثالث من الضرورة المشروطة فإما أن يكون القسم الأول صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كان صغرى فمثاله من الاقتران الأول (كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل ما هو "ج" لا ما دام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج" فما هو من "ج" موضوع الكبرى غير دائم بدوام الموضوع وما هو منه محمول في الصغرى دائم بدوام الموضوع، فالحد الأوسط غير مشترك فلا إنتاج.
وإن كان القسم الأول هو الكبرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج") فلا إنتاج أيضاً؛ لعدم تحقق الاشتراك في الحد الأوسط إذ الموضوع من "ج" في المقدمة الكبرى هو ما دائم بدوام الموضوع والمحمول منه في المقدمة الصغرى، هو ما لا دوام له بدوام الموضوع، وعلى هذا فلا إنتاج من الاختلاط الكائن من القسم الأول والرابع فهو إما "ج" فما هو من "ج" موضوع الكبرى، غير دائم بدوام الموضوع، وما موضوعه محمول في الصغرى دائم بدوام الموضوع فالحد الأوسط غير مشترك، فلا إنتاج.
وإن كان القسم الأول هو الكبرى، كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" لا دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب" وكل ما هو "ج" دائم بدوام الموضوع، فهو ما دام "ج" فلا إنتاج أيضاً، لعدم تحقق الاشتراك في الحد الأوسط، إذ الموضوع من "ج"
And the continuity of its motion from point 'B' to point 'J' is not possible due to the necessity of finitude, and accordingly the judgment, even if the necessary conditional [premise] is in the second, third, or fourth consideration, because the subject ruled by 'A' is only that which is described by 'J' perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject, even if not perpetually with its perpetuity. As for the third and fourth, because the subject ruled by 'A' is only that which is described by 'A' not as long as the subject exists, and then the middle term does not necessarily have to be shared, because it is possible that 'J' which is affirmed of 'B', even if it is in the mode of general possibility always for 'B', is not included under the major premise, and accordingly the judgment is when the minor is particular possible or more specific. And if the possible is major or the conditional is minor—and do not mind which consideration the conditional or possible is—then production occurs, and the conclusion follows the mode of the major; as we have verified before, and you must consider examples of all these sections yourself, and likewise the proof by reduction. And if the conditional is conditional with a condition taken with the predicate or with a condition of time, whether determinate or indeterminate, and whether the conditional is major or minor, then the conclusion follows the mode of the major. And likewise the judgment when the mixture is of possibilities with each other also; as we have verified, and you must consider examples of that yourself. And if the mixture is of necessary conditionals with each other, let us first assume the discussion in the mixture of the first division of necessary conditionals with a condition with the subject, and from each division of the divisions of conditionals, then likewise in order. So we say: if the mixture is between the first and second divisions of conditionals with a condition with the subject, then either the first division is minor or major. If it is minor, then it is like our saying in the first figure: 'Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Everything that is J perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A not as long as J', then the conclusion follows the mode of the major, which is 'Every B is A not perpetually' because it is necessary that it is ruled of everything that is J perpetually not as long as J, and B is J perpetually, so B is A not perpetually. And if the case is reversed, i.e., the first division is major and the second is minor, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J not perpetually' and 'Everything that is J perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as J', then no production; due to lack of commonality in the middle term, since what is ruled by 'A' is 'J perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject' and 'J' ruled of 'B' is not perpetual with its perpetuity. And if the mixture is between the first and third divisions of necessary conditionals, then either the first division is minor or major. If it is minor, then its example from the first figure: 'Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Everything that is J not as long as the subject is A as long as J', so what is from 'J' as subject of the major is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject and what is from it as predicate in the minor is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, so the middle term is not shared, so no production. And if the first division is the major, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B not perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Everything that is J perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as J', then no production either; due to lack of commonality in the middle term, since the subject from 'J' in the major premise is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject and the predicate from it in the minor premise is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject. And accordingly, there is no production from the mixture occurring between the first and fourth divisions. So it is either 'J': what is from 'J' as subject of the major is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, and what is subject of it as predicate in the minor is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, so the middle term is not shared, so no production. And if the first division is the major, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Everything that is J perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as J', then no production either, due to lack of commonality in the middle term, since the subject from 'J'

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical discussion of modal syllogisms, specifically concerning the mixing of different types of conditional premises in the first figure. The author, likely following the tradition of Ibn Sina or later logicians, examines how the modality of premises affects the production of a conclusion. The passage begins with an illustration using motion from point B to J to explain a point about necessity and perpetuity. Then it states that for the third and fourth types of conditionals, the middle term may not be shared because the subject of the major premise is described differently from the predicate of the minor. The author gives a rule: if the minor premise is a particular possible or more specific, the judgment is such. Then a key general rule is given: whenever the major premise is a possible conditional or the minor is a conditional (regardless of its subtype), a conclusion is produced and its modality follows the major premise. Similarly, if the conditional has a condition with the predicate or a time condition, the conclusion follows the major. The same holds when mixing possibilities with each other. The author then turns to mixing necessary conditionals with each other, focusing first on mixtures between different divisions. For the mixture of the first and second divisions: if the first is minor, production occurs and the conclusion follows the major's modality (non-perpetual). If reversed, no production due to lack of shared middle term. For first and third: no production in either case because the middle term is not shared. For first and fourth: similarly no production. The reasoning in each case hinges on whether the middle term (J) is the same in both premises in terms of its modality (perpetual with subject vs. not perpetual). This exemplifies the rigor of modal logic in the Islamic tradition, where the exact temporal conditions of predication must match for a valid syllogism.

Key Terms
المشروطة (al-mashrūṭah): A conditional premise where the predicate is affirmed or denied with a condition, such as 'as long as the subject exists' or with a time condition. [Note: In earlier logic, 'mashrūṭah' often refers specifically to conditional sentences (qadhiyya shartiyya) in the sense of hypotheticals, not modal conditionals. Here it is a type of modal proposition.]
الممكنة (al-mumkinah): A possible proposition, often meaning contingent or non-necessary. [Note: May contrast with necessary (ḍarūriyyah) in modal syllogistic.]
الإنتاج (al-intāj): The production of a valid conclusion from the premises. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
النتيجة تابع لجهة الكبرى (al-natījah tābi' li jihah al-kubrā): The conclusion follows the modality of the major premise. [Note: Key rule in this passage.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the major premise is a possible proposition and the minor premise is a conditional, what is the outcome?
A) No conclusion is produced because the middle term is not shared.
B) A conclusion is produced, and its modality follows the minor premise.
C) A conclusion is produced, and its modality follows the major premise.
D) A conclusion is produced only if the minor is also possible.

Key Takeaway
In modal syllogisms of the first figure, when the major is possible or the minor is conditional, the conclusion's modality is always that of the major premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism with a possible major premise and a conditional minor premise on a topic from Islamic law (e.g., purity of water). Use the author's rule to determine if a conclusion is produced and state its modality.
?
Take the author's example: 'If the major is possible or the minor is conditional, then production occurs and the conclusion follows the mode of the major.' Test this with a concrete example from kalam: 'The world is possible (major: possible) and everything possible is created (minor: universal affirmative non-modal).' Does the conclusion 'The world is created' follow? What would be its modality according to the rule?

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Mixing Necessary Conditionals: Middle Term Modality Matters
Detailed examination of mixing first three divisions of necessary conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a first-figure categorical syllogism mixing first and second/third/fourth divisions of necessary conditionals produces a valid conclusion, based on the modal identity of the middle term.

Core Idea
A valid conclusion requires the middle term to be shared in the same modal sense; otherwise production fails.

The author delves into mixtures of different divisions of necessary conditionals. He demonstrates that even when the terms are identical, if the middle term's modality (perpetuity with subject vs. not) differs between premises, the syllogism is invalid. This highlights the importance of precise modal matching in the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divisions of necessary conditionals
The passage refers to 'first division' (Ā: perpetual with subject, e.g., 'B is J perpetually with the perpetuity of B') and 'second division' (similar but with different temporal qualifiers: 'J is A not as long as J', i.e., not perpetual with subject). Third and fourth divisions are also mentioned.
These divisions stem from post-Avicennan modal classifications. The first division: perpetual with subject; second: perpetual with subject but not with predicate; etc.
2
Write the premise forms using the author's variables
For first division minor and second division major: Minor: 'Every B is J as long as B' (perpetual with B). Major: 'Every J is A not as long as J' (perpetual with J but not perpetual with A). Check if the middle term 'J' is used in the same modal sense in both.
The passage uses 'B', 'J', 'A' with qualifiers like 'perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject' (دائماً بدوام الموضوع) vs. 'as long as' (ما دام).
3
Check the middle term's modal identity
Compare the modality of 'J' in the minor (as predicate: 'B is J perpetually with B') and in the major (as subject: 'J is A perpetually with J? or not?'). If the modality differs (e.g., one is perpetual with subject, the other is not), the middle term is not shared modally.
The author says: 'the middle term does not necessarily have to be shared' (الحد الأوسط لا يلزم أن يكون مشتركاً) when the modalities differ.
4
Determine production based on middle term sharing
If the middle term is shared in the same modal sense, production occurs and the conclusion follows the major's modality. If not, no production. For first minor + second major: middle term is shared (both 'J' are perpetual with subject), so production occurs. For first major + second minor: middle term differs (major's 'J' is perpetual with subject, minor's 'J' is 'J not perpetually'? Actually the minor says 'B is J not perpetually'? Wait, need to read carefully. The passage says if first is major and second minor, then no production because 'J' in major is perpetual with subject, but 'J' in minor is not perpetual with subject. So mismatch.
Refer to the specific examples in the passage for each pair.
5
Extract the general principle
The key is not just the identity of the term (J) but its modal character. The middle term must be the same entity under the same temporal condition for the syllogism to be valid. This principle underlies the author's rejection of combinations where the first division is major and the second is minor, or any mixture where the J in one premise is perpetual with subject and in the other not.
The passage ends with a note about the first and fourth divisions, also failing.

And if the conditional is conditional with a condition taken with the predicate or with a condition of time, whether determinate or indeterminate, and whether the conditional is major or minor, then the conclusion follows the mode of the major. And likewise the judgment when the mixture is of possibilities with each other also; as we have verified, and you must consider examples of that yourself. And if the mixture is of necessary conditionals with each other, let us first assume the discussion in the mixture of the first division of necessary conditionals with a condition with the subject, and from each division of the divisions of conditionals, then likewise in order. So we say: if the mixture is between the first and second divisions of conditionals with a condition with the subject, then either the first division is minor or major. If it is minor, then it is like our saying in the first figure: 'Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Everything that is J perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A not as long as J', then the conclusion follows the mode of the major, which is 'Every B is A not perpetually' because it is necessary that it is ruled of everything that is J perpetually not as long as J, and B is J perpetually, so B is A not perpetually. And if the case is reversed, i.e., the first division is major and the second is minor, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J not perpetually' and 'Everything that is J perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as J', then no production; due to lack of commonality in the middle term, since what is ruled by 'A' is 'J perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject' and 'J' ruled of 'B' is not perpetual with its perpetuity. And if the mixture is between the first and third divisions of necessary conditionals, then either the first division is minor or major. If it is minor, then its example from the first figure: 'Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Everything that is J not as long as the subject is A as long as J', so what is from 'J' as subject of the major is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject and what is from it as predicate in the minor is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, so the middle term is not shared, so no production. And if the first division is the major, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B not perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Everything that is J perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as J', then no production either; due to lack of commonality in the middle term, since the subject from 'J' in the major premise is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject and the predicate from it in the minor premise is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject. And accordingly, there is no production from the mixture occurring between the first and fourth divisions. So it is either 'J': what is from 'J' as subject of the major is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, and what is subject of it as predicate in the minor is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, so the middle term is not shared, so no production. And if the first division is the major, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject is J as long as B' and 'Everything that is J perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject is A as long as J', then no production either, due to lack of commonality in the middle term, since the subject from 'J'
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical discussion of modal syllogisms, specifically concerning the mixing of different types of conditional premises in the first figure. The author, likely following the tradition of Ibn Sina or later logicians, examines how the modality of premises affects the production of a conclusion. The passage begins with an illustration using motion from point B to J to explain a point about necessity and perpetuity. Then it states that for the third and fourth types of conditionals, the middle term may not be shared because the subject of the major premise is described differently from the predicate of the minor. The author gives a rule: if the minor premise is a particular possible or more specific, the judgment is such. Then a key general rule is given: whenever the major premise is a possible conditional or the minor is a conditional (regardless of its subtype), a conclusion is produced and its modality follows the major premise. Similarly, if the conditional has a condition with the predicate or a time condition, the conclusion follows the major. The same holds when mixing possibilities with each other. The author then turns to mixing necessary conditionals with each other, focusing first on mixtures between different divisions. For the mixture of the first and second divisions: if the first is minor, production occurs and the conclusion follows the major's modality (non-perpetual). If reversed, no production due to lack of shared middle term. For first and third: no production in either case because the middle term is not shared. For first and fourth: similarly no production. The reasoning in each case hinges on whether the middle term (J) is the same in both premises in terms of its modality (perpetual with subject vs. not perpetual). This exemplifies the rigor of modal logic in the Islamic tradition, where the exact temporal conditions of predication must match for a valid syllogism.

Key Terms
الضرورية المشروطة (al-ḍarūriyyah al-mashrūṭah): A necessary conditional proposition, where the necessity is conditional on the existence of the subject or some other condition. [Note: This term is specific to later modal logic; earlier texts might use 'ḍarūriyyah' alone for absolute necessity.]
الاختلاط (al-ikhtilāṭ): The mixing of premises of different modalities in a syllogism. [Note: Standard term in modal syllogistic.]
الحد الأوسط (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ): The middle term, which must be shared between the two premises for a valid syllogism. [Note: Stable term.]
بدوام الموضوع (bi-dawām al-mawḍūʿ): With the perpetuity of the subject, meaning the predicate holds as long as the subject exists. [Note: Key modal qualifier in this passage.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No production because the middle term is not shared.
B) A conclusion is produced and follows the modality of the major premise.
C) A conclusion is produced and follows the modality of the minor premise.
D) A conclusion is produced only if the major is necessary.

Key Takeaway
In mixtures of necessary conditionals, the middle term must be modally identical for a valid syllogism; otherwise no conclusion follows.

Mixing Conditioned Necessity Premises: When No Conclusion Follows
Author enumerates rules for mixing different divisions of conditioned necessity premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether mixing two premises from different divisions of conditioned necessity yields a valid conclusion by checking if the middle term is shared in the same temporal modality.

Core Idea
In conditioned necessity syllogisms, the middle term must be identical in temporal modality for a conclusion to follow.

This passage explores a subtle but critical point in modal logic: when the premises of a syllogism come from different divisions of 'conditioned necessity' (الضرورة المشروطة), the conclusion may fail even though the terms appear to match. The key is whether the middle term is qualified with 'always in the duration of the subject' (دائم بدوام الموضوع) or 'not always but as long as the subject exists' (لا دائم بدوام الموضوع). Only if these qualifications are the same across both premises can the middle term truly be shared.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divisions of conditioned necessity
Locate which of the four divisions (qisms) of conditioned necessity each premise belongs to, especially noting whether it is the first (always as long as subject exists), second, third (not always but as long as subject exists), or fourth.
The passage focuses on first, third, and fourth divisions.
2
Extract the middle term from both premises
Find the term that appears in both premises as subject in one and predicate in the other. Note its full description including the temporal qualification (دائم or لا دائم).
Example: In the first case, the middle term is 'C', but in the minor premise it is qualified as perpetual, in the major as non-perpetual.
3
Compare the temporal qualifications of the middle term
Check if the middle term's temporal modality (دائم بدوام الموضوع vs. لا دائم بدوام الموضوع) is the same in both occurrences. If they differ, the term is not shared in the same sense.
This is the central rule: sameness of subject term alone is insufficient; the temporal persistence must match.
4
Determine productivity
If the middle term is not shared (due to different temporal modalities), the syllogism is unproductive (لا إنتاج). If they match, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise.
The author also notes cases where the conclusion does follow (e.g., second division as minor, third as major).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين القسم الأول والثالث من الضرورة المشروطة فإما أن يكون القسم الأول صغرى أو كبرى، فإن كان صغرى فمثاله من الاقتران الأول (كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل ما هو "ج" لا ما دام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج" فما هو من "ج" موضوع الكبرى غير دائم بدوام الموضوع وما هو منه محمول في الصغرى دائم بدوام الموضوع، فالحد الأوسط غير مشترك فلا إنتاج. وإن كان القسم الأول هو الكبرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و(كل ما هو "ج" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ج") فلا إنتاج أيضاً؛ لعدم تحقق الاشتراك في الحد الأوسط إذ الموضوع من "ج" في المقدمة الكبرى هو ما دائم بدوام الموضوع والمحمول منه في المقدمة الصغرى، هو ما لا دوام له بدوام الموضوع، وعلى هذا فلا إنتاج من الاختلاط الكائن من القسم الأول والرابع ... وأما إن كان الاختلاط من القسم الثاني من أقسام الضرورة المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع، وبين القسم الثالث من أقسامها فإما أن يكون كبرى أو صغرى فإن كان القسم الثاني هو الصغرى أو الثالث هو الكبرى، كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو ما دام "ج" فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى لما عرف وكذا إن كان صغرى للقسم الرابع من الضرورة المشروطة بشرط الموضوع، وإن كان كبرى لهما فلا إنتاج؛ لاختلاف الحد الأوسط، واعتبره بنفسك.
As for mixing between the first and third divisions of conditioned necessity: either the first division is the minor premise or the major. If it is the minor, its example from the first figure: (Every B is always in the duration of the subject, so it is C as long as it is B) and (Every C is not always in the duration of the subject, so it is A as long as it is C). Then what is from C as the subject of the major is not perpetual in the duration of the subject, and what from it is predicated in the minor is perpetual in the duration of the subject, so the middle term is not shared, so no production. And if the first division is the major, as our saying: (Every B is not always in the duration of the subject, so it is C as long as it is B) and (Every C is always in the duration of the subject, so it is A as long as it is C), then no production either; due to lack of sharing in the middle term. For the subject of C in the major premise is perpetual in the duration of the subject, and the predicate from it in the minor premise is not perpetual in the duration of the subject. And thus there is no production from the mixing between the first and fourth divisions... And as for mixing between the second division of conditioned necessity with the condition of the subject, and the third division of its divisions: either it is major or minor. If the second division is the minor and the third is the major, as our saying: (Every B is always in the duration of the subject, so it is A as long as it is C), then the conclusion follows the direction of the major, as known. Similarly if it is the minor for the fourth division of conditioned necessity with the condition of the subject. And if it is the major for both, then no production due to difference in the middle term, and you consider it yourself.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of rules for mixing premises from different divisions of conditioned necessity (الضرورة المشروطة) in categorical syllogisms. The author examines several cases: 1) Mixing first and third divisions: when the first division is minor, the middle term differs because C in the major is non-perpetual but in the minor is perpetual, so no conclusion. Similarly when first is major, the middle term again differs (C in major is perpetual, in minor non-perpetual), so no production. 2) Mixing first and fourth divisions: the same reasoning applies — no sharing of middle term, no conclusion. 3) Mixing second and third divisions: if second is minor and third is major, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise (the third division). But if the second is major for both (i.e., used as major premise with two different minor premises from third and fourth), there is no production because the middle term differs. The author emphasizes that the student should work out examples themselves. The key principle is that for a syllogism to be productive, the middle term must be shared in the same sense; if the temporal qualification (دائم بدوام الموضوع vs. لا دائم بدوام الموضوع) differs between the occurrences of the middle term, there is no common term and thus no inference.

A) A valid conclusion with the modality of the major.
B) No conclusion because the middle term is not shared.
C) A valid conclusion with the modality of the minor.
D) A valid conclusion with a non-modal conclusion.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism with conditioned necessity premises is productive only if the middle term's temporal modality is identical in both premises; otherwise no conclusion follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The General Rule: Conclusion Follows the Modality of the Major
Author states the general rule of modal mixing in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that when mixing modal propositions, the conclusion's modality follows that of the major premise, given that the middle term is shared.

Core Idea
Modal mixing: the conclusion's modality mirrors the major premise, not the minor.

This passage introduces a key principle in modal syllogistics: when premises of different necessity-types are combined, the modality of the conclusion is determined by the major premise. The author explains this with reference to the four divisions of 'necessary conditioned by a condition with the subject' and other modal types. We will dissect the first set of rules and see how to apply them to real Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor premises in the mixing scenario
The author states: 'In the major premise, what is permanent with the permanence of the subject, and its predicate in the minor is what has no permanence with the permanence of the subject.' This defines the modal content of each premise. Notice that the major deals with permanent subject-necessity, while the minor lacks that permanence.
The major premise is the one containing the predicate of the conclusion, but here it is identified by its modal characteristic.
2
Apply the rule for first and fourth divisions and other conditioned propositions
The author says: 'There is no production from what belongs to the first and fourth divisions of the necessary conditioned by a condition with the subject, and from the conditioned by a condition of the predicate, or by a condition of time, whichever is major or minor. So the conclusion follows the modality of the major.' This means that in these combinations, the conclusion takes the modality of the major premise, regardless of which division appears where.
The 'conditioned by a condition with the subject' refers to necessity as long as the subject exists, etc.
3
Generalize the pattern for other combinations
The author repeatedly states 'the conclusion follows the modality of the major' for many cases (second with third, etc.). The principle is consistent: unless the middle term is not shared, the major's modality dictates the conclusion's modality.

Mixing: In the major premise, what is permanent with the permanence of the subject, and its predicate in the minor premise is what has no permanence with the permanence of the subject. If the mixing is from the third division of the divisions of the necessary conditioned by a condition with the subject, and whether the first division is minor or major, because there is no commonality between them in the middle term; consider it yourself. Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the rules for mixing modalized propositions in categorical syllogisms, specifically focusing on the different divisions of 'necessary conditioned by a condition with the subject' (الضرورة المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع). The author lists various combinations and states when a conclusion follows and what modality it has. The key principle is that the conclusion's modality generally follows the major premise, except when the middle term is not shared, in which case no conclusion is produced. The passage uses variables ب, ج, أ for terms. The author urges the reader to consider the remaining cases on their own, indicating that the rules are systematic and can be generalized. For example, when the second division (something necessary as long as the subject exists) is minor and the third division (something necessary as long as the predicate exists) is major, the conclusion follows the modality of the major. But if the second division is major and the fourth is minor, the middle term differs and no inference is valid. This mirrors patterns in Islamic modal logic where the temporal persistence of the subject or predicate matters.

Identifying When No Conclusion Follows (لا إنتاج)
Author specifies when modal mixing yields no conclusion due to middle term mismatch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases in modal mixing where no conclusion is produced due to a mismatch in the middle term, as explained by the author.

Core Idea
When the middle term is not shared, the syllogism yields no conclusion, regardless of modality.

The passage also specifies conditions under which mixing modal propositions fails to produce a valid inference. The key reason is a difference in the middle term (الحد الأوسط), which breaks the syllogistic connection. The author illustrates this with specific combinations, such as when the second division is major and the fourth division is minor, or when the third division mixes with the first. We will examine these exceptions to prevent invalid reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the condition for no production
The author explicitly says: 'If it is major for both, then there is no production due to the difference of the middle term.' This occurs when a specific division (e.g., second) serves as major in relation to another (e.g., fourth) in a certain way. Identify the exact phrase 'فلا إنتاج' and note the reason given: 'لاختلاف الحد الأوسط'.
The middle term must be identical in meaning and scope in both premises; if one premise uses a term with a condition that changes its identity, it is not the same middle term.
2
Understand why the middle term differs
In the example 'second division as major, fourth as minor', the second division's necessity is 'as long as the subject exists', while the fourth division may have a different temporal scope or condition. This alters the term's sense, making it not shared.
The author uses 'the middle term differs' literally, meaning the term is not the same in both premises due to differing modal conditions.
3
Apply the rule to other cases
The author also mentions: 'If the mixing is from the third division ... and whether the first division is minor or major, because there is no commonality between them in the middle term' – again no production. So whenever the modal conditions change the meaning of the middle term, inference fails.

Mixing: In the major premise, what is permanent with the permanence of the subject, and its predicate in the minor premise is what has no permanence with the permanence of the subject. Accordingly, there is no production from what belongs to the first and fourth divisions of the necessary conditioned by a condition with the subject, and from the conditioned by a condition of the predicate, or by a condition of time, whichever is major or minor. So the conclusion follows the modality of the major, as we have verified, and you must consider the rest of the conjunctions. Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the rules for mixing modalized propositions in categorical syllogisms, specifically focusing on the different divisions of 'necessary conditioned by a condition with the subject' (الضرورة المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع). The author lists various combinations and states when a conclusion follows and what modality it has. The key principle is that the conclusion's modality generally follows the major premise, except when the middle term is not shared, in which case no conclusion is produced. The passage uses variables ب, ج, أ for terms. The author urges the reader to consider the remaining cases on their own, indicating that the rules are systematic and can be generalized. For example, when the second division (something necessary as long as the subject exists) is minor and the third division (something necessary as long as the predicate exists) is major, the conclusion follows the modality of the major. But if the second division is major and the fourth is minor, the middle term differs and no inference is valid. This mirrors patterns in Islamic modal logic where the temporal persistence of the subject or predicate matters.

Key Takeaway
When mixing modal propositions, always verify that the middle term is the same in both premises; if the modal conditions alter its identity, no valid syllogism follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are the middle terms the same? Why or why not?
