Mixing Absolute and Modalized Propositions
Rules for mixing absolute with modalized premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion when mixing general absolute, specific absolute, and modalized premises in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The conclusion's modality depends on the major premise—except when the minor is possible and the major is absolute general.

In modal logic, absolute propositions (without modality) are divided into general, specific, and temporal. When mixed with necessary or possible premises, the conclusion usually follows the modality of the major premise. But there is an important exception when the minor is a possible proposition and the major is a general absolute—then by consensus the conclusion is general possible. This lesson walks you through the key rules from the passage so you can apply them correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of absolute propositions
The passage divides absolute into three: general (مطلقة عامة), specific (مطلقة خاصة), and temporal (مطلقة وقتية). General absolute has no modality and applies to all individuals; specific absolute asserts actual existence (existential); temporal absolute is bound by time.
The temporal absolute is not further discussed in this fragment.
2
Check the mixing case for general absolute with necessary
If the general absolute mixes with a necessary premise, the necessary may be absolute (unmodalized) or conditioned. If the necessary is absolute, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise (if the major is necessary, conclusion necessary; if major is general absolute, conclusion general possible).
The phrase 'لما سلف في اختلاطها' refers to earlier rules.
3
Apply the rule for general absolute with possible
When mixing general absolute with possible (whether general, specific, or most specific possible), the conclusion normally follows the major premise's modality. But there is a key exception: if the minor is possible (any type) and the major is general absolute, then the conclusion is general possible by consensus of the majority.
The exception is explicitly stated: 'اللهم إلا أن تكون الصغرى ممكنة...'
4
Handle the specific absolute as minor
If the specific absolute (which is existential) serves as the minor premise, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise, regardless of whether the major is necessary or possible. The passage affirms this based on what was previously established.
The passage cuts off here, but the rule is clear.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقات والموجهات، فالمطلقة إما أن تكون عامة أو خاصة أو وقتية فإن كانت عامة فإما أن يكون اختلاطها مع الضرورية أو الممكنة، فإن كان اختلاطها مع الضرورية فالضرورية إما أن تكون مطلقة أو مشروطة، فإن كانت مطلقة فالنتيجة تكون تابعة لجهة الكبرى إن كانت ضرورية وإن كانت مطلقة عامة فالنتيجة ممكنة عامة؛ لما سلف في اختلاطها وأما إن كانت الضرورية مشروطة فالحكم في الاختلاط الكائن من أقسامها ومن المطلقة العامة حكم اختلاطها مع الممكنة وقد عرف. وإن كان الاختلاط من المطلقة العامة والممكنة فالحكم أيضاً في اتباع النتيجة للكبرى على ما عرف في باقي الاختلاطات، وسواء كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص. اللهم إلا أن تكون الصغرى ممكنة بأي اعتبار قدر و الكبرى مطلقة عامة، فإن النتيجة تكون ممكنة عامة باتفاق الجمهور؛ لما سلف في اختلاطها بالمطلقة الخاصة. وأما المطلقة الخاصة وجودية وهي المطلقة الخاصة، فإن كانت صغرى فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى، وسواء كانت الكبرى ضرورية أو ممكنة؛ لما أسلفناه في تحقيق
If the mixing is from absolute (مطلقات) and modalized (موجهات) propositions, then the absolute is either general, specific, or temporal. If it is general, then either its mixing is with necessary or possible. If its mixing is with necessary, then the necessary is either absolute or conditioned. If it is absolute, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major if it is necessary; and if it is absolute general, then the conclusion is general possible; because of what preceded about its mixing. If the necessary is conditioned, then the judgment about the mixing from its divisions and from the general absolute is the same as its mixing with the possible, as known. If the mixing is from the general absolute and the possible, then the judgment also is that the conclusion follows the major according to what is known in the rest of the mixings, whether the possible is in the general sense, the specific, or the most specific. Except if the minor is possible in any consideration it is taken and the major is general absolute, then the conclusion is general possible by agreement of the majority; because of what preceded about its mixing with the specific absolute. As for the specific absolute, it is existential, and it is the specific absolute: if it is minor, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major, whether the major is necessary or possible; because of what we have stated earlier in the verification...
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of modal syllogisms, now focusing on absolute (unmodalized) propositions. The author first divides absolute propositions into three types: general (مطلقة عامة), specific (مطلقة خاصة), and temporal (مطلقة وقتية). For the general absolute, the rules depend on whether it is mixed with necessary or possible premises. When mixed with a necessary that is itself absolute (i.e., unmodalized), the conclusion follows the modality of the major (the necessary one) if it is major; if the absolute general is major and the minor is absolute general too, the conclusion is general possible. When mixed with a conditioned necessary, the rule is the same as mixing with the possible. When mixing general absolute with possible, the conclusion normally follows the major, except when the minor is possible and the major is general absolute; then by consensus the conclusion is general possible. For the specific absolute (مطلقة خاصة), which is existential, if it is the minor, the conclusion follows the modality of the major regardless of whether the major is necessary or possible. The passage cuts off, but the pattern is that absolute propositions behave predictably in modal mixings.

Key Takeaway
When mixing absolute general with possible premises, the conclusion normally follows the major, but if the minor is possible and the major is absolute general, the conclusion is general possible by consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism where the minor is a possible proposition (e.g., 'It is possible that some knowledge is acquired') and the major is a general absolute (e.g., 'Every acquired thing is temporal'). Determine the modality of the conclusion using the exception rule from this lesson.
?
Identify a real example from Usul al-Fiqh: When proving that an analogy (qiyas) yields a ruling, the major premise is a general absolute (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden') and the minor may be a possible (e.g., 'Nabidh might be an intoxicant'). Apply the rule to say what conclusion is justified.

General Rule: Conclusion Follows Major's Modality
Author explains rules for mixed modal syllogisms with conditional and necessary premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that in mixed modal syllogisms involving conditional or necessary premises, the conclusion takes the modality of the major premise.

Core Idea
The major premise's modality dictates the conclusion's modality.

This lesson introduces the foundational rule for mixed modal syllogisms. When premises have different modalities, the conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise, unless special cases apply. We examine the first set of rules from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixing type
Determine if the mixing involves a conditional (mashruta) premise or specific divisions of necessary premises.
The text discusses conditional with predicate or time condition, and third/fourth divisions of necessary conditional.
2
Check if the mixed premise is the major for both
If the mixed premise is the major for both premises, no conclusion is produced because the middle term differs.
This is a special case that blocks inference.
3
Apply the main rule for conditional and necessary mixing
In all other cases of mixing with conditional or necessary premises, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise, whether it is major or minor.
The author emphasizes this is consistent across various divisions.
4
Verify with an example
Construct a syllogism using Islamic concepts, e.g., 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause (necessary). If the world is temporally originated, it has a cause (conditional).' The conclusion is necessary, following the major.
Ensure the example satisfies the rule.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عامة باتفاق الجمهور؛ لما سلف في اختلاطها بالمطلقة الخاصة. وأما المطلقة الخاصة وجودية وهي المطلقة الخاصة، فإن كانت صغرى فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى، وسواء كانت الكبرى ضرورية أو ممكنة؛ لما أسلفناه في And if it (the mixed premise) is the major for both, then there is no production (of a conclusion) because of the difference of the middle term, and consider it yourself. And as for if its mixing is with the conditional (al-mashruta) with the condition of the predicate or time, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major in all, whether it is major or minor, and consider it [yourself]. And as for if the mixing is from the third division of the necessary (al-daruriyya) conditional with the condition of the subject and from the fourth division thereof, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major, due to what we have verified, whether it is major or minor. And similarly the rule in the case where the mixing is from the fourth division thereof and from the conditional with the condition of the predicate or with the condition of time, or the mixing is from the fourth division of the conditional with the condition of the subject and the conditional with the condition of the predicate, or with the condition of time, or the mixing is from the conditional with the condition of the predicate and the conditional with the condition of time, whether determined or ambiguous, and you must consider all yourself, for it is easy. And as for if the mixing is from the absolute (al-mutlaqat) and the modalized (al-muwajjhat), then the absolute is either general or specific or temporal. If it is general, then either its mixing is with the necessary or the possible. If its mixing is with the necessary, then the necessary is either absolute or conditional. If it is absolute, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major if it is necessary; and if it is absolute general, then the conclusion is possible general, due to what preceded in its mixing. And if the necessary is conditional, then the rule in the mixing that occurs from its divisions and from the absolute general is the rule of its mixing with the possible, and it has been known. And if the mixing is from the absolute general and the possible, then the rule also regarding the conclusion following the major is as known in the remaining mixings, whether the possible is in the general sense or the specific or the most specific. Except if the minor is possible in any consideration and the major is absolute general, then the conclusion is possible general by consensus of the majority, due to what preceded in its mixing with the absolute specific. And as for the absolute specific, it is existential (wujudiyya), and it is the absolute specific. If it is minor, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major, whether the major is necessary or possible, due to what we have previously stated in the verification of the universal predication (al-maqul 'ala al-kull). And if it is major and the minor is possible in any consideration, then the conclusion according to the majority is possible specific, because the absolute specific encompasses the divisions of the conditionals at a time without perpetuity, and at that time the major term is established for the middle subject to its being 'j' in actuality, and it is possible that 'j' is not established for the minor in actuality but by possibility, so the major belongs to the minor by possibility not actuality, in the manner it was established for the middle. This is contrary to the case where the minor is necessary, because necessarily the middle term in it is essential for the minor in actuality, not merely by possibility, and you must demonstrate that through reductio (bi al-khalf) if you wish, and verify it with examples. And as for if the absolute is temporal, its rule in mixing with the modalized is the rule of its mixing with the absolute general and specific, and we have explained it, so do not prolong it.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of rules for mixed modal categorical syllogisms. The author discusses various cases where premises have different modalities (necessary, possible, absolute, conditional, temporal) and states the modality of the conclusion. The overall principle is that the conclusion's modality follows the major premise's modality in many cases, with exceptions especially when the minor is possible. The author urges the reader to verify the rules themselves, indicating a pedagogical approach. For example, in the rule about absolute general and possible, the conclusion is possible general except when the minor is possible and the major is absolute general, then the conclusion is possible general by consensus. The passage ends with the case of temporal absolute, which follows the same rule as general and specific. The text uses logical variables like 'j' (ج) to denote terms. Practically, in Islamic law, one might apply these rules to evaluate arguments about divine attributes or legal rulings.

Key Terms

النتيجة
جهة الكبرى

D) The figure of the syllogism

Key Takeaway
In mixed modal syllogisms with conditional or necessary premises, the conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise.

Mixing Absolute General with Necessary and Possible
Author details rules for mixing absolute general premises with necessary and possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion's modality when mixing absolute general premises with necessary or possible premises, distinguishing between necessary absolute and necessary conditional.

Core Idea
Absolute general as major yields possible general conclusion; as minor, conclusion follows major.

When one premise is absolute general (mutlaqa 'amma), the conclusion's modality depends on the other premise and its own modality. This lesson covers the rules for mixing absolute general with necessary and possible premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absolute premise as general
Recognize that the absolute premise is mutlaqa 'amma, which is unrestricted and universal.
It can be general, specific, or temporal; here we focus on general.
2
Check if mixing with necessary or possible
If mixing with necessary, further distinguish if the necessary is absolute or conditional.
The text gives separate rules for each subcase.
3
Apply rule for mixing with necessary absolute
If the necessary is absolute (mutila), the conclusion follows the major's modality. If the major is necessary, conclusion is necessary; if the major is absolute general, conclusion is possible general.
The text gives this explicitly.
4
Apply rule for mixing with necessary conditional
If the necessary is conditional (mashruta), the rule is the same as mixing with possible: the conclusion follows the major's modality.
The text says 'the rule is the rule of its mixing with the possible'.

من القسم الرابع منه ومن المشروطة بشرط مع المحمول أو بشرط وقت، أو كان الاختلاط من القسم الرابع من المشروط بشرط مع الموضوع والمشروط بشرط مع المحمول، أو بشرط الوقت أو كان الاختلاط من المشروط بشرط المحمول عامة باتفاق الجمهور؛ لما سلف في اختلاطها بالمطلقة الخاصة. وأما المطلقة الخاصة وجودية وهي المطلقة الخاصة، فإن كانت صغرى فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى، وسواء كانت الكبرى ضرورية أو ممكنة؛ لما أسلفناه في Exception: Possible Minor with Absolute General Major
Author states exception for possible minor and absolute general major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exception when the minor premise is possible and the major is absolute general, stating that the conclusion is possible general by consensus.

Core Idea
Exception: possible minor + absolute general major = possible general conclusion.

The author notes an important exception to the general rule, which is agreed upon by the majority. When the minor premise is possible (in any sense) and the major premise is absolute general, the conclusion is always possible general.

Guided Path
1
Check if the minor premise is possible
The possibility can be in the general, specific, or most specific sense.
The text says 'possible in any consideration'.
2
Check if the major premise is absolute general
The major must be mutlaqa 'amma, i.e., unrestricted and universal.
3
Apply the exception
If both conditions hold, the conclusion is possible general (mumkina 'amma).
The text says 'by consensus of the majority'.
4
Understand the reason
The possible minor does not guarantee actuality of the middle term for the minor, so the conclusion can only be possible.
This is similar to mixing with absolute specific.

Mixing with Absolute Specific Premises
Author explains rules for mixing absolute specific premises with necessary and possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion's modality when mixing absolute specific premises with necessary or possible premises, depending on whether the absolute specific is minor or major.

Core Idea
Absolute specific as minor: conclusion follows major. Absolute specific as major with possible minor: conclusion possible specific.

The absolute specific (mutlaqa khassa) is an existential proposition true at a specific time but not perpetually. This lesson covers the rules for mixing it with modal premises, as explained in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absolute premise as specific
It is existential (wujudiyya) and time-bound without perpetuity.
The text calls it 'المطلقة الخاصة وجودية'.
2
Determine if it is minor or major
The rule differs based on its position.
3
If it is minor, apply rule
The conclusion follows the modality of the major, whether the major is necessary or possible.
The text says 'فإن كانت صغرى فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى'.
4
If it is major and the minor is possible
The conclusion is possible specific (mumkina khassa).
The text provides a reasoning: the major term is not actually present in the minor.
5
If the minor is necessary
Then the conclusion does not follow the possible-specific rule; the middle term is essential in actuality.
The text says this is contrary to the previous case.

وإن كان كبرى لهما فلا إنتاج؛ لاختلاف الحد الأوسط، واعتبره بنفسك. وأما إن كان اختلاطه مع المشروطة بشرط المحمول أو الوقت، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى في الكل، وسواء كان هو كبرى أو صغرى واعتبره [بنفسك]. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من القسم الثالث من أقسام الضرورية المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع ومن القسم الرابع منها، فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى؛ لما حققناه وسواء كان كبرى أو صغرى، وكذا الحكم في ما إذا كان الاختلاط من القسم الرابع منه ومن المشروطة بشرط مع المحمول أو بشرط وقت، أو كان الاختلاط من القسم الرابع من المشروط بشرط مع الموضوع والمشروط بشرط مع المحمول، أو بشرط الوقت أو كان الاختلاط من المشروط بشرط المحمول والمشروط بشرط الوقت، وسواء كان معيناً أو مبهماً، وعليك أن تعتبر الكل بنفسك، فإنه سهل. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقات والموجهات، فالمطلقة إما أن تكون عامة أو خاصة أو وقتية فإن كانت عامة فإما أن يكون اختلاطها مع الضرورية أو الممكنة، فإن كان اختلاطها مع الضرورية فالضرورية إما أن تكون مطلقة أو مشروطة، فإن كانت مطلقة فالنتيجة تكون تابعة لجهة الكبرى إن كانت ضرورية وإن كانت مطلقة عامة فالنتيجة ممكنة عامة؛ لما سلف في اختلاطها وأما إن كانت الضرورية مشروطة فالحكم في الاختلاط الكائن من أقسامها ومن المطلقة العامة حكم اختلاطها مع الممكنة وقد عرف. وإن كان الاختلاط من المطلقة العامة والممكنة فالحكم أيضاً في اتباع النتيجة للكبرى على ما عرف في باقي الاختلاطات، وسواء كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص. اللهم إلا أن تكون الصغرى ممكنة بأي اعتبار قدر و الكبرى مطلقة عامة، فإن النتيجة تكون ممكنة عامة باتفاق الجمهور؛ لما سلف في اختلاطها بالمطلقة الخاصة. وأما المطلقة الخاصة وجودية وهي المطلقة الخاصة، فإن كانت صغرى فالنتيجة تابعة لجهة الكبرى، وسواء كانت الكبرى ضرورية أو ممكنة؛ لما أسلفناه في تحقيق المقول على الكل، وإن كانت كبرى والصغرى ممكنة بأي اعتبار قدر فالنتيجة عند الجمهور ممكنة خاصة، وذلك لأن المطلقة الخاصة ما تتناول أقسام المشروطات في وقت من غير دوام وعند ذلك أن يكون الحد الأكبر ثابتاً للأوسط مشروطاً بكونه "ج" بالفعل، ويحتمل أن لا يكون "ج" ثابتاً للأصغر بالفعل بل بالإمكان فيكون الأكبر للأصغر بالإمكان لا بالفعل على نحو ما كان ثابتاً للأوسط، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية؛ ضرورة أن الحد الأوسط فيها لا بُدّي للأصغر بالفعل، لا لمحض الإمكان، وعليك بيان ذلك بالخلف إن شئت، وتحقيقه بأمثلته. وأما إن كانت المطلقة وقتية فحكمها في الاختلاط مع الموجهات حكم اختلاطها مع المطلقات العامة والخاصة، وقد بيناه فلا تطول .به.
And if it (the mixed premise) is the major for both, then there is no production (of a conclusion) because of the difference of the middle term, and consider it yourself. And as for if its mixing is with the conditional (al-mashruta) with the condition of the predicate or time, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major in all, whether it is major or minor, and consider it [yourself]. And as for if the mixing is from the third division of the necessary (al-daruriyya) conditional with the condition of the subject and from the fourth division thereof, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major, due to what we have verified, whether it is major or minor. And similarly the rule in the case where the mixing is from the fourth division thereof and from the conditional with the condition of the predicate or with the condition of time, or the mixing is from the fourth division of the conditional with the condition of the subject and the conditional with the condition of the predicate, or with the condition of time, or the mixing is from the conditional with the condition of the predicate and the conditional with the condition of time, whether determined or ambiguous, and you must consider all yourself, for it is easy. And as for if the mixing is from the absolute (al-mutlaqat) and the modalized (al-muwajjhat), then the absolute is either general or specific or temporal. If it is general, then either its mixing is with the necessary or the possible. If its mixing is with the necessary, then the necessary is either absolute or conditional. If it is absolute, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major if it is necessary; and if it is absolute general, then the conclusion is possible general, due to what preceded in its mixing. And if the necessary is conditional, then the rule in the mixing that occurs from its divisions and from the absolute general is the rule of its mixing with the possible, and it has been known. And if the mixing is from the absolute general and the possible, then the rule also regarding the conclusion following the major is as known in the remaining mixings, whether the possible is in the general sense or the specific or the most specific. Except if the minor is possible in any consideration and the major is absolute general, then the conclusion is possible general by consensus of the majority, due to what preceded in its mixing with the absolute specific. And as for the absolute specific, it is existential (wujudiyya), and it is the absolute specific. If it is minor, then the conclusion follows the modality of the major, whether the major is necessary or possible, due to what we have previously stated in the verification of the universal predication (al-maqul 'ala al-kull). And if it is major and the minor is possible in any consideration, then the conclusion according to the majority is possible specific, because the absolute specific encompasses the divisions of the conditionals at a time without perpetuity, and at that time the major term is established for the middle subject to its being 'j' in actuality, and it is possible that 'j' is not established for the minor in actuality but by possibility, so the major belongs to the minor by possibility not actuality, in the manner it was established for the middle. This is contrary to the case where the minor is necessary, because necessarily the middle term in it is essential for the minor in actuality, not merely by possibility, and you must demonstrate that through reductio (bi al-khalf) if you wish, and verify it with examples. And as for if the absolute is temporal, its rule in mixing with the modalized is the rule of its mixing with the absolute general and specific, and we have explained it, so do not prolong it.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of rules for mixed modal categorical syllogisms. The author discusses various cases where premises have different modalities (necessary, possible, absolute, conditional, temporal) and states the modality of the conclusion. The overall principle is that the conclusion's modality follows the major premise's modality in many cases, with exceptions especially when the minor is possible. The author urges the reader to verify the rules themselves, indicating a pedagogical approach. For example, in the rule about absolute general and possible, the conclusion is possible general except when the minor is possible and the major is absolute general, then the conclusion is possible general by consensus. The passage ends with the case of temporal absolute, which follows the same rule as general and specific. The text uses logical variables like 'j' (ج) to denote terms. Practically, in Islamic law, one might apply these rules to evaluate arguments about divine attributes or legal rulings.

Temporal Absolute: Same Rules as General and Specific
Author states that temporal absolute follows the same rules as absolute general and specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that temporal absolute premises follow the same rules as absolute general and specific in mixed modal syllogisms.

Core Idea
Temporal absolute is not a new category; its rules are identical.

The author concludes the section by noting that temporal absolute (waqtiyya) behaves exactly like absolute general and specific. This lesson consolidates the rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise as temporal absolute
It is an absolute proposition bound by a specific time.
2
Apply the rules for absolute general or specific
The same conditions and conclusions apply depending on the case. No new rules needed.
The text says 'حكم اختلاطها مع المطلقات العامة والخاصة'.

Mixed Modal Syllogisms: Universal Affirmative Major and Possible Minor
Author explains conclusion when major is universal affirmative and minor is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion mode (specifically possible) when a universal affirmative major is combined with a possible minor in a categorical syllogism, and explain the contrast with a necessary minor.

Core Idea
When major is necessary-like but minor is possible, the conclusion follows possibility, not actuality.

In modal syllogisms, the modality of the premises determines the modality of the conclusion. This passage explores a specific case: when the major premise is a universal affirmative (like a necessary proposition) and the minor premise is a possible proposition. The author shows that the conclusion is 'specifically possible' rather than necessary, because the minor only possibly instantiates the middle term. This contrasts sharply with when the minor is necessary, where the conclusion becomes necessary. Understanding this rule is crucial for constructing valid modal arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
The major is a universal affirmative (al-maqūl ʿalā al-kull), which carries a strong modality (often necessary or absolute). The minor is a possible proposition (mumkina), meaning the predicate may or may not hold for the subject.
In the text, the major is taken as universal affirmative, not necessarily necessary, but the reasoning treats it as conditionally actual.
2
Understand the 'special absolute' (al-muṭlaqa al-khāṣṣa)
The author describes the conclusion as 'specifically possible' (mumkina khāṣṣa). This is related to the 'special absolute' proposition, which includes conditional possibilities without permanence. Recognise that this modal concept is specific to Avicennan logic.
Not all later systems use this term.
3
Reason about actuality vs. possibility
The major term (A) is established for the middle term (B) only conditionally: it is actual when B is actually J. But the minor term (C) may have B only possibly (not actually J). Therefore, A applies to C only by possibility, not actuality.
The variable 'ج' (jīm) stands for the condition (e.g., being actually in a certain state).
4
Contrast with a necessary minor
If the minor were necessary (ḍarūriyya), the middle term would be actual for the minor, forcing the major term to be actual for the minor as well, yielding a necessary conclusion. The author invites you to prove this by reduction (burhān khalfī) and examples.
This highlights the critical role of the minor's modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقول على الكل، وإن كانت كبرى والصغرى ممكنة بأي اعتبار قدر فالنتيجة عند الجمهور ممكنة خاصة، وذلك لأن المطلقة الخاصة ما تتناول أقسام المشروطات في وقت من غير دوام وعند ذلك أن يكون الحد الأكبر ثابتاً للأوسط مشروطاً بكونه "ج" بالفعل، ويحتمل أن لا يكون "ج" ثابتاً للأصغر بالفعل بل بالإمكان فيكون الأكبر للأصغر بالإمكان لا بالفعل على نحو ما كان ثابتاً للأوسط، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية؛ ضرورة أن الحد الأوسط فيها لا بُدّي للأصغر بالفعل، لا لمحض الإمكان، وعليك بيان ذلك بالخلف إن شئت، وتحقيقه بأمثلته.
وأما إن كانت المطلقة وقتية فحكمها في الاختلاط مع الموجهات حكم اختلاطها مع المطلقات العامة والخاصة، وقد بيناه فلا تطول به.
The universal affirmative [premise], and if the major [premise] is [universal affirmative] and the minor is possible in any consideration taken, then the conclusion according to the majority is specifically possible. That is because the special absolute [proposition] comprises the divisions of conditional things in a time without permanence, and then the major term is established for the middle term conditionally upon its being 'J' in actuality, and it is possible that 'J' is not established for the minor term in actuality but by possibility, so the major [term] is for the minor [term] by possibility, not by actuality, in the manner it was established for the middle. And this is contrary to when the minor is necessary, because the necessity is that the middle term in it is necessarily for the minor in actuality, not merely by possibility. And it is upon you to demonstrate that by reduction if you wish, and verify it with its examples. And as for if the absolute is temporal, its ruling in mixing with modals is the ruling of its mixing with general and special absolute propositions, and we have explained it, so do not lengthen with it.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage explains a specific rule in mixed modal syllogisms: when the major premise is a universal affirmative (al-maqūl ʿalā al-kull) and the minor premise is a possible proposition (mumkina), the conclusion is 'specifically possible' (mumkina khāṣṣa). The reasoning is that the major term is only conditionally attached to the middle term in actuality (i.e., when the middle term actually has the attribute), but the minor term may only possibly have the middle term. Thus the major term applies to the minor only by possibility, not actuality. This contrasts with when the minor is necessary (ḍarūriyya), where the middle term is actual for the minor, so the conclusion becomes necessary. The author encourages verifying this by reduction (burhān khalfī) and examples. Finally, it notes that if the absolute premise is temporal (waqtiyya), its mixing rule is already covered in earlier discussions on general and special absolutes.

المطلقة الخاصة
ممكنة
ضرورية

Checkpoint

A) necessarily affirmative
B) specifically possible
C) impossible
D) necessary negative

Key Takeaway
When the major premise is universal affirmative and the minor is possible, the conclusion is specifically possible, not necessary, because the middle term may not be actual for the minor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism with a universal affirmative major (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden') and a possible minor (e.g., 'Nabidh could be an intoxicant'), then determine the modal type of the conclusion according to the passage, explaining your reasoning.
?

Definition and Precedence of the Second Figure
Definition and comparison of figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure by its middle term position and explain why it is considered more useful than the third and fourth figures for scientific demonstration.

Core Idea
The second figure's universal negative conclusions make it more valuable than particulars.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms has a distinctive structure: its middle term is the predicate in both premises. The author explains why this figure takes precedence over the third and fourth figures, emphasizing that it produces universal negatives — the most useful type of proposition in the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term
In the second figure, the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) is the predicate of both premises. Identify this in the definition given by the author.
This contrasts with the first figure where the middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other.
2
Compare with the third figure
The third figure produces affirmative conclusions, but those are particular, not universal. The second figure, though limited to negatives, can yield universal negatives, which are more powerful for scientific proof.
Why are universal negatives more useful? They exclude entire categories.
3
Compare with the fourth figure
The fourth figure can produce both universal negatives and particular affirmatives, but it is less clear and natural than the second. Moreover, all fourth-figure arguments reduce to the second figure.
The author claims the second is 'closer to natures' (aqrab ilā al-ṭibāʿ).
4
Summarize the precedence
The second figure is prioritized because it yields universal negative conclusions, which are more beneficial in the sciences, and its arguments are more intuitive than those of the fourth figure.

وهو الفصل الرابع في تحقيق الشكل الثاني وقرائنه. ما الحد الأوسط فيه محمول على الطرفين وإنما كان تلواً للأول ومقدماً على ما بعده، أما بالنسبة إلى الثالث فلإنتاجه الكلي، والثالث وإن أنتج الموجبة فنتيجته لا تكون جزئية، وهذا وإن لم ينتج غير السلب فهو قد ينتج الكلي، والكلي السالب في العلوم أنفع من الجزئي الموجب، وأما بالنسبة إلى الرابع - وإن كان الرابع قد ينتج الكلي السالب والجزئي الموجب - فلأن الثاني أبين وأقرب إلى الطباع من الرابع وإنتاج الرابع، ففي كل اقتراناته إنما هو يعود إلى الثاني، وله في إنتاجه خاصة ومشروط، أما خاصيته فإنه لا ينتج غير السالب، وأما شروطه فكليّة كبراه، واختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف، إن كانت الجهة فيها واحدة، أو متداخلة أي أن جهة المقدمتين أعم من الأخرى، فلا إنتاج فيه مما كبراه جزئية، ولا من موجبتين ولا سالبتين إن كانت الجهة فيهما واحدة أو متداخلة على ما ذكرناه، أما اشتراط كليّة كبراه فلأنه إذا كانت الكبرى جزئية، فإما أن تكون موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فالصغرى إما أن تكون كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت كلية موجبة كقولنا: (كل "ج" "ب" وبعض "أ" "ج") فليس يلزم نسبة "أ" إلى "ب" لا بنفي ولا إثبات، بل قد تكون النسبة تارة بالإيجاب، وأخرى بالسلب أما الأول فكما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الجسم حيوان فإن النسبة هاهنا بين الإنسان والجسم بالإيجاب لا بالسلب. وأما الثاني فكما في قولنا: (كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الفرس حيوان) فالنسبة هاهنا بين الإنسان والفرس بالسلب لا بالإيجاب. وإن كانت الصغرى كلية سالبة كقولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "ج" وبعض "أ" "ج") فالنسبة أيضاً بين "أ" و"ب" قد تكون تارة بالإيجاب وأخرى بالسلب، أما الأول فكما في قولنا: (لا شيء من الإنسان حجر وبعض الجسم حجر) فالنسبة بين
And it is the fourth chapter on the verification of the second figure and its conditions. The middle term in it is predicated of both extremes, and it is a follower of the first and precedes what comes after it. As for compared to the third, because of its universal productivity: and although the third produces the affirmative, its conclusion is not particular, and this [second] although it does not produce anything other than the negative, it may produce the universal negative, and the universal negative is more useful in the sciences than the particular affirmative. And as for compared to the fourth — although the fourth may produce the universal negative and the particular affirmative — because the second is clearer and closer to natures than the fourth and its production. So in all its conjunctions, it returns to the second. And it has in its production a special property and a condition. As for its special property, it does not produce other than the negative. And as for its conditions: the universality of its major premise, and the difference of its two premises in quality, if the modality in them is one, or overlapping, meaning that the modality of the two premises is more general than the other. So there is no production from that whose major is particular, nor from two affirmatives nor two negatives if the modality in them is one or overlapping as we mentioned. As for the condition of the universality of its major: because if the major is particular, then either it is affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the minor is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, as in our saying: (Every 'J' is 'B' and some 'A' is 'J'), then the relation of 'A' to 'B' is not necessitated, neither by negation nor affirmation; rather, the relation may sometimes be by affirmation and [other times] by negation. As for the first, as in our saying: Every human is an animal and some body is an animal, then the relation here between human and body is by affirmation, not by negation. And as for the second, as in our saying: (Every human is an animal and some horse is an animal), then the relation here between human and horse is by negation, not by affirmation. And if the minor is universal negative, as in our saying: (Nothing of 'B' is 'J' and some 'A' is 'J'), then the relation also between 'A' and 'B' may sometimes be by affirmation and [other times] by negation. As for the first, as in our saying: (Nothing of human is stone and some body is stone), then the relation between
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is from the fourth chapter on the second figure (al-shakl al-thānī) of categorical syllogisms. The author begins by defining the second figure: its middle term is predicated of both extremes (i.e., it is the predicate in both premises). He explains its precedence over the third and fourth figures: the second produces universal negatives, which are more useful in sciences than particular affirmatives; also the fourth figure's arguments reduce to the second. Then he states the special property of the second figure: it only produces negative conclusions. The conditions for a valid syllogism in this figure are: (1) the major premise must be universal, and (2) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) when their modality is the same or when one modality includes the other. The author justifies the condition of universal major by showing that if the major is particular, no conclusion can be drawn. For example, with a universal affirmative major and a particular affirmative minor, the relation between the terms can be either affirmative or negative, as shown in the examples: 'Every human is an animal and some body is an animal' yields an affirmative relation between human and body, while 'Every human is an animal and some horse is an animal' yields a negative relation. Similarly, with a universal negative minor, the relation can vary. Thus, no necessary conclusion follows when the major is particular. The text cuts off mid-sentence, continuing into the next page.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني
الحد الأوسط
الكلى السالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It produces affirmative conclusions
C) It is the first figure
D) It produces particular affirmatives

Key Takeaway
The second figure is distinguished by its middle term being the predicate in both premises and is valued for its universal negative conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following argument: 'No angel is corporeal; every human is corporeal; therefore no human is an angel.' Identify the figure, the middle term, and explain why this conclusion is universal negative. Then compare its usefulness to a particular affirmative conclusion like 'Some human is not an angel.'

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Special Property and Conditions of the Second Figure
Statement of property and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the special property and two conditions for a valid second-figure syllogism and verify them in given syllogisms.

Core Idea
Second figure only yields negatives; major must be universal and premises must differ in quality.

After establishing the definition and precedence of the second figure, the author turns to its internal logic. He first declares its special property: it never produces an affirmative conclusion. Then he lays down two conditions for a productive second-figure syllogism: the major premise must be universal, and the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), given the same or overlapping modality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special property
The author states that the second figure 'does not produce other than the negative.' That means any valid second-figure syllogism must have a negative conclusion. Check this property in examples.
This is a strict rule; if a conclusion is affirmative, the syllogism cannot be second figure validly.
2
Check the first condition: major must be universal
The major premise (the one containing the major term, which is the predicate of the conclusion) must be universal (either universal affirmative or universal negative). If it is particular, no necessary conclusion can be drawn.
The author will later demonstrate why this condition is necessary.
3
Check the second condition: premises must differ in quality
The two premises cannot both be affirmative or both negative. One must be affirmative and the other negative. This applies when the modality of the premises is the same or when one modality is more general (overlapping).
If both are affirmative or both negative, the syllogism does not produce a conclusion.
4
Test a syllogism against the conditions
Take an example: 'No necessary thing is contingent; the world is contingent; therefore the world is not necessary.' Here major is universal negative, minor is universal affirmative, conclusion is universal negative. Check: major universal (yes), premises differ in quality (yes, one negative, one affirmative). So the conditions are satisfied.

The Syllogism

10 min
Justification for Universal Major in the Second Figure
Justification of the universal major condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a particular major premise prevents a necessary conclusion in the second figure, using counterexamples.

Core Idea
A particular major leaves the relation between extremes undetermined; both affirmation and negation are possible.

The author now provides the logical justification for why the major premise must be universal in the second figure. He systematically considers cases where the major is particular and shows that no necessary conclusion follows. Through concrete examples using variables and Islamic instances, he demonstrates that both affirmative and negative relations can hold between the subject and predicate of the intended conclusion, thus no deduction is forced.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
When the major premise is particular, the link between the terms is too weak to force a single conclusion. The author will show that both affirmative and negative relations are possible between the extremes, so no necessary inference exists.
This is a classic demonstration of invalidity through counterexample.
2
Case 1: Major is particular affirmative
Assume major: 'Some A is J' (particular affirmative). Then the minor may be universal or particular. The author examines when minor is universal: if minor is universal affirmative ('Every B is J'), then the relation between B and A can be either affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is animal' and 'some body is animal' gives 'Every human is body'? Actually the example shows 'human and body' are affirmative) or negative (e.g., 'Every human is animal' and 'some horse is animal' gives 'No human is horse'). Thus, no fixed conclusion.
The variable J stands for the middle term, B for the minor term, A for the major term.
3
Case 2: Minor is universal negative
If the minor is universal negative ('No B is J') and major is particular affirmative ('Some A is J'), again the relation between A and B can be affirmative or negative. Example: 'No human is stone' and 'some body is stone' yields some human is not body? Actually the example is cut off, but the logic is similar.
The pattern continues on the next page; the key insight is that particular major never necessitates one relation.
4
Generalize the condition
From these cases, the author concludes that for a necessary conclusion, the major must be universal. Only then can the premises force a single, determinate relation between the extremes, which in the second figure must be negative.

C) The relation between the extremes can be either affirmative or negative
D) No conclusion is possible only when the minor is particular

Key Takeaway
A particular major in the second figure always leaves the relation between the extremes undetermined, necessitating the condition that the major must be universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a counterexample for a second-figure syllogism with a particular negative major and a universal affirmative minor. Show that both a negative and an affirmative relation between the extremes are possible, using Islamic terms (e.g., angel, human, animal).

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Second Figure: Conditions for Validity
Author explains the second figure's properties and its two validity conditions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a valid second-figure categorical syllogism and test whether a given syllogism satisfies them.

Core Idea
The second figure only yields a valid negative conclusion when the major premise is universal and the premises differ in quality.

We have seen that the first figure is the most powerful. Now the author turns to the second figure, which has a special usefulness in the sciences because it produces universal negatives. However, it is more restrictive: it only works under two precise conditions. The author demonstrates these conditions through an exhaustive analysis of every possible violation, using concrete examples from familiar categories like 'human', 'animal', and 'stone'. This lesson will walk you through that analysis so that you can apply the conditions yourself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Check that the middle term is the predicate in both premises. That is the defining feature of the second figure.
In the first figure, the middle is subject in the major and predicate in the minor; in the third, subject in both; in the fourth, predicate in the major and subject in the minor.
2
Check major premise universality
Ensure the major premise (the one containing the major term) is universal (either affirmative or negative). The author demonstrates that if the major is particular, the conclusion does not necessarily follow, using exhaustive examples.
The author shows that with a particular major, the relation can be either affirmative or negative, so no necessary conclusion.
3
Check quality difference
Ensure the two premises differ in quality: one affirmative, one negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, no conclusion follows, provided modality conditions are met.
Modality condition: the jiha (necessity, possibility, etc.) must be identical or one more general; otherwise further complications arise.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Apply the conditions to a concrete second-figure syllogism from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'No permissible is prohibited; every khamr is prohibited; therefore no khamr is permissible.' Verify that major is universal (negative), premises differ in quality (major negative, minor affirmative), so conclusion follows.
This is a valid Cesare mood (EAE) in the second figure.

This is the fourth chapter: On the verification of the second figure and its conditions. The middle term there is predicated of both extremes, and it is the successor to the first figure and prior to what comes after it. Regarding the third figure, because it produces a universal conclusion; and the third figure, though it produces an affirmative, its conclusion is never particular. This figure (second), though it produces nothing but a negative, it may produce a universal, and the universal negative is more useful in the sciences than the particular affirmative. Regarding the fourth figure – although the fourth may produce a universal negative and a particular affirmative – because the second is clearer and closer to nature than the fourth and the production of the fourth; for in all its conjunctions it returns to the second. It has a peculiarity and a condition in its production: as for its peculiarity, it produces nothing but a negative; as for its conditions: the universality of its major premise, and the difference of its two premises in quality, if the modality in them is identical or overlapping, i.e., the modality of the two premises is more general than the other. So there is no production in it when its major premise is particular, nor from two affirmatives or two negatives if the modality in them is identical or overlapping as we mentioned. As for the requirement of the universality of its major premise: because if the major is particular, either it is affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the minor is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, as in our statement: 'Every J is B, and some A is J', then the relation of A to B does not necessarily follow, neither by negation nor affirmation, but rather the relation may sometimes be affirmative and sometimes negative. As for the first, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and some body is an animal' – here the relation between human and body is affirmative, not negative. As for the second, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and some horse is an animal' – here the relation between human and horse is negative, not affirmative. If the minor is universal negative, as in our statement: 'No B is J, and some A is J', then the relation between A and B may also be sometimes affirmative and sometimes negative. As for the first, as in our statement: 'No human is a stone, and some body is a stone' – the relation between human and body is affirmative, not negative; as for the second, as in our statement: 'No human is a stone, and some inanimate is a stone' – the relation between human and inanimate is negative, not affirmative. And if the minor is particular, either affirmative or negative. If affirmative as in our statement: 'Some B is J, and some B is J'? Actually it says: (some B is J) and (some B is J) – the relation between B and A may also sometimes be affirmative, as in our statement: 'Some human is an animal, and some rational is an animal'; similarly if negative as in our statement: 'Some B is not J, and some A is J' – the relation between B and A may sometimes be affirmative as in our statement: 'Some human is not a stone, and some body is a stone'; and sometimes negative as in our statement: 'Some human is not a stone, and some inanimate is a stone'. And in this manner if the major is particular negative, regardless of whether the minor is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative, it may happen that the major term is related to the minor term sometimes affirmatively and sometimes negatively. Consider when the minor is universal affirmative, as in our statement: 'Every B is J, and some A is not J'. If we say: 'Every human is an animal' or 'Some body is not an animal' – that would be true, and the relation between human and body is affirmative. If we say: 'Every human is an animal, and some stone is not an animal' – that would also be true, and the relation between human and stone is negative, not affirmative. If the minor is universal negative, then also the relation between the major and minor terms may sometimes be affirmative, as in our statement: 'No human is a horse, and some animal is not a horse' – the relation between human and animal is affirmative; and sometimes negative, as in our statement: 'No human is a stone, and some horse is not a stone' – the relation between human and horse is negative, not affirmative. From all this it is not hidden that conclusion does not necessarily follow even if the minor is particular affirmative or negative. And as for the requirement of the difference of the two premises in quality when the modality is identical or overlapping, because if the quality is identical when the modality is identical, then the quality would be either affirmative or negative. And in both cases, it does not follow that from affirming something of two things the relation between the two things is affirmative, without...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the second figure of categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-thani), its properties, and its necessary conditions for valid production of a conclusion. The author begins by positioning the second figure relative to the other figures: it comes after the first figure (which produces all four types of conclusions) and before the third and fourth. The author argues that the second figure is more useful than the third because it can produce a universal negative (which is valuable in sciences), and it is clearer and more natural than the fourth figure, since all fourth-figure moods can be reduced to the second. Then the author states two essential characteristics: (1) the second figure only produces negative conclusions (its peculiarity), and (2) it has two conditions: (a) the major premise must be universal, and (b) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) provided that their modality is either identical or overlapping (i.e., one modality is more general). The author then proves the necessity of the first condition (major universal) by exhaustive case analysis. He systematically examines all possible combinations when the major is particular: first when the major is affirmative, then negative; for each, he considers the possible forms of the minor (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). For each combination, he provides an example using the well-known terms 'human', 'animal', 'stone', 'body', 'horse', 'rational', 'inanimate' etc., to show that no necessary conclusion follows because the relation between the major and minor terms can sometimes be affirmative and sometimes negative. He continues this analysis through multiple scenarios, all demonstrating that when the major is particular, no valid inference is possible. The passage then begins to address the second condition (difference in quality), but cuts off mid-sentence. The reasoning for the second condition would likely be similar: if both premises are affirmative or both negative, no conclusion follows. The author's method is rigorous and demonstrates the need for the specified conditions through exhaustive counterexamples.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thani): The second figure of categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the predicate in both premises. [Note: No significant shift; stable across Arabic logic tradition.]
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat): The middle term, which appears in both premises and mediates the inference. [Note: No significant shift.]
الكبرى (al-kubra): The major premise containing the major term and middle term; here it must be universal. [Note: Stable term.]
الصغرى (al-sughra): The minor premise containing the minor term and middle term. [Note: Stable.]
الجهة (al-jiha): The modality of a proposition (necessity, impossibility, possibility). The condition for validity includes that modalities are identical or overlapping. [Note: In later works, discussions of modality become more complex; here it is a simple condition.]
الإنتاج (al-intaj): The production of a necessary conclusion from the premises. [Note: Stable.]
الحد الأكبر (al-hadd al-akbar): The major term, which is the predicate of the conclusion. [Note: Stable.]
الحد الأصغر (al-hadd al-asghar): The minor term, which is the subject of the conclusion. [Note: Stable.]

The second figure requires a universal major premise and differing qualities (one affirmative, one negative) to produce a valid negative conclusion.

Conditions for Production with Particular Minor Premise in Second Figure
Author examines cases of particular minor premises in second figure, showing production depends on material.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a second-figure syllogism with a particular minor premise yields a valid conclusion by analyzing the material relation between the major and minor terms.

Core Idea
In the second figure, particular premises break formal necessity—material determines the conclusion.

In this passage, the author investigates a boundary case of the second figure: when the minor premise is particular. He shows that the logical form alone is insufficient to guarantee a conclusion; the actual content (material) of the terms determines whether the relation is affirmative or negative. This lesson walks you through his reasoning and its implications for evaluating syllogistic validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
Recognize that the syllogism is in the second figure (both premises share a common term as subject of major and predicate of minor). Note that the minor premise is particular (i.e., 'Some B is C' or 'Some B is not C').
The author considers both affirmative and negative particular minors.
2
Extract the possible relations
For each case, list the possible relations (nisba) between the major term (A) and minor term (B). The author shows that the relation can be either affirmative or negative depending on the specific terms used.
Example: with 'Some human is animal' and 'Some body is animal', the relation between human and body is affirmative.
3
Test with concrete examples
Plug in real-world terms (e.g., human, animal, stone, body, horse) to see if the same form can yield both affirmative and negative relations between A and B.
The author provides multiple examples to demonstrate variability.
4
Assess validity
Conclude that no valid conclusion necessarily follows because the relation is not fixed by form; it depends on the material content. Hence, the syllogism is non-productive (ghayr muntij).
This does not mean the conclusion is false, but that it is not forced.

If the minor premise is particular, it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, as in our saying: (Some B is C) and (Some B is C), then the relation between B and A may sometimes be affirmative, as in our saying: (Some human is animal) and (Some rational is animal); likewise if it is negative, as in our saying: (Some B is not C) and (Some A is C), then the relation between B and A may sometimes be affirmative, as in our saying: (Some human is not stone) and (Some body is stone); and sometimes it may be negative, as in our saying: (Some human is not stone) and (Some inanimate is stone). And in this manner, if the major premise is particular negative, whether the minor premise is universal affirmative, or universal negative, or particular affirmative, or particular negative, it is possible that the major term relates to the minor term sometimes affirmatively and sometimes negatively. As for when the minor premise is universal affirmative, as in our saying: (Every B is C) and (Some A is not C): if we say 'Every human is animal' or 'Some body is not animal', it is true, and the relation between human and body is affirmative; and if we say 'Every human is animal' and 'Some stone is not animal', it is also true, and the relation between human and stone is negative, not affirmative. And if the minor premise is universal negative, the relation between the major and minor terms may also sometimes be affirmative, as in our saying: (No human is horse) and (Some animal is not horse), so the relation between human and animal is affirmative; and sometimes negative, as in our saying: (No human is stone) and (Some horse is not stone), then the relation between human and horse is negative, not affirmative. Therefore, it is clear that production does not necessarily follow, even if the minor premise is also particular affirmative or negative.

Commentary
This passage examines the conditions for a valid syllogism in the second figure when the minor premise is particular. The author is showing that even though the form of the premises might seem to allow a conclusion, the actual material relation between the major and minor terms can be either affirmative or negative, depending on the specific terms used. The author first considers cases where the minor premise is particular affirmative or negative, and then extends the discussion to cases where the minor premise is universal but the major is particular negative. Through a series of examples using terms like human, animal, stone, body, horse, horse, etc., the author demonstrates that the same formal arrangement can yield either an affirmative or a negative relation, meaning that the conclusion is not forced. This underscores the necessity of material consideration in syllogistic logic beyond mere form.

Why Premises Must Differ in Quality When Modality Is Same
Author argues premises must differ in quality when modality is identical, and refutes an objection about lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism's premises violate the condition of differing quality and explain why no conclusion follows.

Core Idea
Two premises with same modality and same quality yield no necessary conclusion—only varying material relations.

The author establishes a key condition for syllogistic validity: when premises share the same modality (e.g., both necessary), they must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Without this difference, the relation between the major and minor terms remains indeterminate. He illustrates with examples from necessary propositions and refutes a geometric objection involving lines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Locate where the author states that when modality is the same or overlapping, the two premises must differ in quality (affirmative vs. negative).
This is a condition for certain figures, especially the second figure.
2
Analyze why same quality fails
If both premises are affirmative or both negative, the actual relation (equality, subsumption, or separation) between the major and minor terms varies with the material. No unique conclusion is forced.
The author says 'it does not necessarily follow' that the relation is always affirmative or always negative.
3
Work through the examples
Examine the three cases with necessary premises: (1) equal terms (human, rational, animal), (2) subsumption (human, body, animal), (3) separation (human, horse, animal). Note that in each, the conclusion depends on material, not form.
The middle term is animal in all, but the major and minor differ in relation.
4
Examine the objection about lines
Someone argues that from 'Line B goes from center to circumference' and 'Line C goes from center to circumference' one can conclude B equals C, both premises affirmative. This seems to challenge the condition.
The objector thinks two affirmative premises can produce a conclusion.
5
Understand the author's response
The author points out that the conclusion does not follow from those two premises alone. An additional universal premise is needed: 'Every two lines from center to circumference are equal.' With that, the argument becomes a first-figure syllogism, not a counterexample to the second-figure condition.
Thus, the original two-premise argument is incomplete; the condition for second figure (differing quality) remains intact.

وأما اشتراط اختلاف المقدمتين في الكيف عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها، فلأنه إذا اتحدت الكيفية عند اتحاد الجهة فإما أن تكون الكيفية إيجاباً أو سلباً، وعلى كلا التقديرين فليس يلزم من إيجاب شيء لشيئين أن تكون النسبة بالشيئين بالإيجاب دون السلب أو بالسلب دون الإيجاب، وكذا إن كان مسلوباً عنهما، بل قد تكون النسبة الحد الأكبر والأصغر باعتبار خصوص المادة، تارة بالإيجاب و[أخرى بالسلب أما إذا كانت إيجابية كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فإنه إن كان الحد الأصغر والأكبر متساويين، أو أن الأصغر داخل تحت الأكبر في نفس الأمر كانت النسبة بينهما بالإيجاب، وإن كان الأصغر في نفس الأمر مبايناً للأكبر ، وإن كان الحد الأوسط أعم منهما فالنسبة بينهما بالسلب مثال الأول، أما إذا كان الحد الأصغر والأكبر متساويين كقولنا: بالضرورة كل إنسان (حيوان) و بالضرورة كل ناطق حيوان، وأما إذا كان الأصغر داخلاً تحت الأكبر كقولنا كل إنسان (جسم) وكل جسم حيوان (جسم) وأما إن كان الحد الأصغر مبايناً في نفس الأمر للأكبر كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان وكل فرس (حيوان)، وعلى هذا إن كانت الكيفية سلباً كسلب الحجر في المثال الأول عن الإنسان والناطق، والإنسان والحيوان، وفي المثال الثاني عن الإنسان والفرس، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى عدم الإنتاج اتحاد الكيفية وتداخل الجهة، وعلى أنا سنوضحه عن قريب، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا اختلفت الكيفية وكانت الكبرى كليّة فإنه يلزم من ذلك التباين بين ذلك التباين بين الأصغر والأكبر كما ستعلمه ولا يلزم على ما ذكرناه قول القائل (خط "ب" خرج من المركز إلى المحيط) و (خط "ج" خرج من المركز إلى المحيط) فإنه يلزم عنه خط "ب" مساو لخط "ج") والمقدمتان ،موجبتان والحد الأوسط محمول فيهما؛ لأنا نقول الحد الأصغر هاهنا إنما هو "ب" و"ج" والمحمول عليهما وهو الحد الأوسط خروجهما من المركز إلى المحيط، والنتيجة غير لازمة عن ذلك فقط دون مقدمة كبرى تضاف إلى ما ذكر وهي و(كل خارجين من المركز إلى المحيط فهما متساويان) ولازمه (خط "ب" و"ج" متساويان) وهو على نمط الشكل الأول دون الثاني.
As for the condition that the two premises differ in quality when the modality is the same or overlapping, that is because if the quality is united while the modality is united, then the quality is either affirmative or negative. In both cases, it does not necessarily follow from the affirmation of one thing to two things that the relation between the two things is affirmative without negative, or negative without affirmative. Likewise if it is denied of both. Rather, the relation between the major and minor terms, considering the particular matter, may sometimes be affirmative and sometimes negative. If it is affirmative, as in our saying: (Necessarily every B is C) and (Necessarily every A is C), then if the minor and major terms are equal, or if the minor is subsumed under the major in reality, the relation between them is affirmative. If the minor is in reality separate from the major, and the middle term is more general than both, then the relation between them is negative. Example of the first: if the minor and major are equal, as in our saying: Necessarily every human is animal and Necessarily every rational is animal. And if the minor is subsumed under the major, as in our saying: Every human is body and Every body is animal. And if the minor is in reality separate from the major, as in our saying: Every human is animal and Every horse is animal. Likewise, if the quality is negative, such as the negation of stone from human and rational, and from human and animal, and in the second example from human and horse. Therefore, it is clear that there is no production given unity of quality and overlapping of modality. And we will clarify this shortly. This is contrary to the case when the quality differs and the major premise is universal, for then the separation between the minor and major necessarily follows, as you will learn. And what we have mentioned is not affected by the saying of someone: (Line B goes out from the center to the circumference) and (Line C goes out from the center to the circumference), for it would imply that Line B is equal to Line C, and the two premises are affirmative and the middle term is predicated in both. For we say: The minor term here is B and C, and what is predicated of them, i.e., the middle term, is their going out from the center to the circumference. The conclusion does not follow from that alone without a major premise added to what was mentioned, namely: (Every two lines going out from the center to the circumference are equal). Its necessary conclusion is (Line B and Line C are equal). And it is in the manner of the first figure, not the second.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage discusses another condition for a valid syllogism: when the modality (e.g., necessity) of the premises is the same, the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The author explains that if both are affirmative or both negative, the actual material relation between the major and minor terms can vary—they may be equal, subsumed, or separate—so no unique conclusion is forced. He provides examples with necessary premises using terms like human, animal, rational, body, horse, and stone. He then addresses a potential objection: from the premises 'Line B goes from center to circumference' and 'Line C goes from center to circumference', one might think that B and C are equal. The author responds that this reasoning requires an additional universal premise ('Every two lines from center to circumference are equal'), which transforms the argument into a first figure syllogism, not a second figure one. This illustrates the importance of the formal conditions in the second figure.

When premises share the same modality, they must differ in quality; otherwise, the conclusion is not forced by the form alone—it depends on the material terms.

The Syllogism

Condition for Second-Figure Productivity and Refuting a Counterexample
Author states condition when quality differs and major is universal, refutes a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a productive second-figure syllogism (differing quality with universal major) and distinguish a genuine second-figure argument from a first-figure argument disguised as second.

Core Idea
Second figure yields mutual exclusion only when premises differ in quality and major is universal.

The second figure of categorical syllogism has specific conditions for productivity. The author states that when the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise is universal, the minor and major terms are mutually exclusive. He then refutes a counterexample that appears to produce a conclusion from two affirmative premises, but in fact belongs to the first figure. This lesson unpacks both the condition and the method of refuting such objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Note the two requirements: the two premises must differ in quality, and the major premise must be universal. When these hold, the conclusion is mutual exclusion (tabayun) between the minor and major terms.
Quality refers to affirmative/negative; major premise is the one containing the major term as subject.
2
Examine the consequence
Mutual exclusion means the minor and major terms are wholly separate; the conclusion is a universal negative (e.g., No S is P). This is the only valid result in the second figure under this condition.
3
Analyze the counterexample
The objector claims: Line B and Line J both issue from center to circumference, so they are equal. The premises are both affirmative and share the same middle term.
This seems like a second figure because middle term is predicate of both premises.
4
See why it fails
The conclusion does not follow from the given premises alone. To deduce equality, a universal major premise is needed: 'Every two lines issuing from center to circumference are equal'. With that, the argument becomes first figure (Barbara), not second.
Thus it does not violate the stated condition; it is simply a misidentified figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا بخلاف ما إذا اختلفت الكيفية وكانت الكبرى كليّة فإنه يلزم من ذلك التباين بين الأصغر والأكبر كما ستعلمه. ولا يلزم على ما ذكرناه قول القائل (خط "ب" خرج من المركز إلى المحيط) و (خط "ج" خرج من المركز إلى المحيط) فإنه يلزم عنه خط "ب" مساو لخط "ج") والمقدمتان موجبتان والحد الأوسط محمول فيهما؛ لأنا نقول الحد الأصغر هاهنا إنما هو "ب" و"ج" والمحمول عليهما وهو الحد الأوسط خروجهما من المركز إلى المحيط، والنتيجة غير لازمة عن ذلك فقط دون مقدمة كبرى تضاف إلى ما ذكر وهي (كل خارجين من المركز إلى المحيط فهما متساويان) ولازمه (خط "ب" و"ج" متساويان) وهو على نمط الشكل الأول دون الثاني.
This is contrary to the case when the quality differs and the major premise is universal, for then it necessarily follows that there is mutual exclusion between the minor and major terms, as you will learn. And what we have said is not refuted by someone saying: 'Line B issues from the center to the circumference, and line J issues from the center to the circumference, so it follows that line B equals line J' — yet the two premises are affirmative and the middle term is predicated in both. For we say: The minor terms here are B and J, and what is predicated of them, which is the middle term, is 'issuing from the center to the circumference'. The conclusion does not follow from that alone without an additional major premise, namely: 'Every two lines issuing from the center to the circumference are equal'. Its necessary consequent is 'Lines B and J are equal', and this is in the manner of the first figure, not the second.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage clarifies a condition for productivity in the second figure of categorical syllogism and defends it against a counterexample. The author states that when the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise (which contains the middle term and the major term) is universal, then the minor and major terms must be in a relation of mutual exclusion (tabayun). This is an established rule. He then considers a potential objection: one might think that from two affirmative premises with the same middle term predicated of both subjects, a conclusion follows (e.g., line B equals line J). But the author responds that this is not a valid second-figure argument because the premises actually yield a first-figure syllogism when the missing major premise is supplied. The objection fails because the alleged conclusion is not entailed by the given premises alone without an additional universal affirmative premise. Thus the condition holds.
