Enumeration of Productive Conjunctions in the Second Figure
Author enumerates 16 possible conjunctions, reduces to 4 when modality is uniform, and gives proofs for first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four productive premise combinations in the second figure under uniform modality and justify the elimination of the other twelve.

Core Idea
Only four out of sixteen premise pairs in the second figure yield valid syllogisms when modality is uniform.

In the second figure of categorical syllogism, not every combination of premises produces a valid conclusion. The author of al-Daqa'iq guides us through a systematic reduction from sixteen possible conjunctions to just four by applying two conditions: the major must be universal, and under uniform modality the premises must differ in quality. This lesson walks through that enumeration, equipping you to quickly identify a productive mood in the second figure.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the number 16
The four types of quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) for both major and minor yield 16 possible conjunctions.
2
Apply condition 1: universal major
Eliminate any conjunction where the major premise is particular (affirmative or negative). This removes 8 conjunctions.
Each of the two particular majors has four minors.
3
Apply condition 2: differing quality when modality is uniform
When modality is uniform, the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This eliminates another 4 conjunctions.
Specifically, those with both universal of opposite quality, and those with two affirmatives or two negatives when one premise is particular.
4
Identify the remaining 4 conjunctions
The author lists them: (1) both universal, major negative; (2) both universal, minor negative; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major; (4) particular negative minor + universal affirmative major.
These are the only productive conjunctions under uniform modality.
5
Understand the effect of differing modality
When modality differs, condition 2 is lifted, so the four eliminated by that condition become productive, yielding 8 conjunctions.
The author later discusses these cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قرائنه فباعتبار تركب المحصورات بعضها مع بعض فستة عشر كما عرف، غير أن اشتراط كليّة كبراه أسقط منها ما الكبرى فيها جزئية وجزئية سالبة وهي ثمانٍ، إذ كل واحدة من الكبريين فلها أربع صغريات، وهي الكلية الموجبة والكلية السالبة والجزئية الموجبة والجزئية السالبة، واشتراط اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها أسقط منها أربعة وهي الكائنة من الكليتين إيجابا وسلباً، ومن موجبتين والصغرى جزئية، ومن سالبتين والصغرى جزئية، فعادت قرائنه عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها إلى أربع وهي الكائنة من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، وكليتين وصغراهما سالبة، وجزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، وجزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة. وعند اختلاف الجهة تعود قرائنه المنتجة إلى ثمانٍ وهي هذه وما أسقطناها باشتراط اختلاف الكيفية ولنشرع في بيان كل واحد منها. فنقول : إما أن تكون الجهة متحدة أو مختلفة، فإن كانت متحدة فإما أن تكون جهة الضرورة أو الإمكان أو الإطلاق، فإن كانت جهة الضرورة فإما أن تكون مطلقة أو مشروطة، ولنقرض الكلام في الكائن من الضروري المطلق أولاً، وقد بان أن القرائن عند اتحاد الجهة لا تزيد على أربع: الاقتران الأول منها : من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، ونتيجته كلية ضرورية سالبة كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها على الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول وهو بين. الاقتران الثاني :[ منها] من كليتين والصغرى سالبة، ونتيجته كالتي قبله كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وهو خلف.
As for its [the second figure's] productive conjunctions, by considering the combination of quantified propositions with each other, there are sixteen, as known. However, the condition that its major premise be universal eliminates those in which the major is particular affirmative and particular negative, which are eight, since each of the two majors has four minors: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. And the condition that its two premises differ in quality when modality is uniform or overlapping eliminates four: those consisting of two universal premises affirmative and negative, two affirmatives with particular minor, and two negatives with particular minor. Thus its productive conjunctions when modality is uniform or overlapping reduce to four: two universals with major negative; two universals with minor negative; particular affirmative minor with universal negative major; particular negative minor with universal affirmative major. And when modality differs, its productive conjunctions return to eight, which are these plus those we eliminated by the condition of differing quality. Let us commence explaining each one. We say: modality is either uniform or different. If uniform, it is either necessity, possibility, or absolute. If necessity, it is either absolute or conditional. Let us restrict discussion to the absolute necessary first. It has become clear that the conjunctions when modality is uniform do not exceed four. The first conjunction: from two universals with major negative, its conclusion is a universal necessary negative, as in our saying: 'Necessarily every B is J' and 'Necessarily no A is J'; so the conclusion is necessarily no B is A. Otherwise (if it were possible), it would be 'by general possibility some B is A', but it was 'necessarily no A is J'; so it would be 'necessarily some B is not J'; but it was 'necessarily every B is J', which is a contradiction. Also because if you convert the major and keep it as major, the conjunction becomes the second conjunction of the first figure, which is clear. The second conjunction: from two universals with minor negative, its conclusion is like the previous, as in our saying: 'Necessarily no B is J' and 'Necessarily every A is J'; so the conclusion is necessarily no B is A. Otherwise, it would be 'by general possibility some B is A', but it was 'necessarily every A is J'; so it would be 'necessarily some B is J'; but it was 'necessarily no B is J', which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage provides a systematic enumeration of the productive conjunctions (qara'in) in the second figure of categorical syllogism. The author begins by noting that there are 16 possible combinations when considering the quantity and quality of the two premises (four for the major times four for the minor). The first condition—that the major premise must be universal—eliminates half (eight) because the major cannot be particular. A second condition when modality is uniform: the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This removes another four. Thus only four conjunctions remain when modality is uniform. The author then lists them explicitly: (1) both universal, major negative; (2) both universal, minor negative; (3) particular affirmative minor, universal negative major; (4) particular negative minor, universal affirmative major. When modality differs, the condition of differing quality is removed, so eight conjunctions become productive (the four above plus the four that were eliminated only because of uniform quality condition). The author then focuses on the uniform modality case, specifically absolute necessity. He provides proofs for the first two conjunctions using proof by contradiction (khalf). For the first conjunction (major negative), he assumes the opposite of the conclusion (that some B is A), and shows it leads to a contradiction with the premises. He also notes an alternative proof via conversion of the major premise, which transforms it into a first figure syllogism. The second conjunction (minor negative) is proved similarly.

Key Terms
القرائن (al-qara'in): Productive conjunctions of premises that yield a valid conclusion in a given figure. Here, specifically the valid combinations in the second figure. [Note: In earlier works, this term may be used for 'signs' or 'clues'; here it is a technical term for mood combinations.]
الجهة (al-jiha): Modality of the proposition (e.g., necessity, possibility, absolute). The author discusses uniform modality (jiha muttahida) and different modality (jiha mukhtalifa). [Note: Standard term in Arabic logic for modality.]
الاقتران (al-iqtiran): A specific combination of premises (e.g., from two universals with major negative). Used synonymously with qarina in this passage. [Note: Usually refers to a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many productive conjunctions are there in the second figure when the modality is uniform?
A) Sixteen
B) Eight
C) Four
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Under uniform modality, only four premise combinations in the second figure are productive: two universals with major negative, two universals with minor negative, particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, and particular negative minor with universal affirmative major.

?
List the four productive conjunctions in the second figure under uniform modality (using quality and quantity notation). For each conjunction, write a concrete example from fiqh or kalam that fits it.

Proving the First Two Conjunctions in the Second Figure
Author enumerates 16 possible conjunctions, reduces to 4 when modality is uniform, and gives proofs for first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the validity of second figure syllogisms using both proof by contradiction (khalf) and conversion to the first figure.

Core Idea
A second figure syllogism can be proven by contradiction or by converting the major premise to a first figure form.

The author does not merely list valid conjunctions; he provides rigorous proofs. For the first two conjunctions, he offers two methods: assuming the opposite of the conclusion leads to a contradiction, and converting the major premise to transform the syllogism into the first figure. This lesson walks through these proofs, which are essential for verifying syllogistic validity in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Set up the first conjunction
Identify the premises: major premise is universal negative (No A is J), minor premise is universal affirmative (Every B is J). The conclusion is universal negative (No B is A).
Using the author's variables: 'A' is the major term, 'B' is the minor term, 'J' is the middle term.
2
Proof by contradiction (khalf)
Assume the opposite of the conclusion: that some B is A (particular affirmative). From the major premise 'No A is J' and this assumption, derive 'Some B is not J'. But the minor premise says 'Every B is J', yielding a contradiction. Therefore the opposite cannot hold; the original conclusion is necessary.
The author uses necessity modality, but the logic works for categorical assertions as well.
3
Alternative proof by conversion
Convert the major premise 'No A is J' to 'No J is A' (simple conversion). The premises become: minor 'Every B is J', major 'No J is A'. This is a first figure syllogism (Celarent), which directly yields 'No B is A'.
Conversion preserves truth for universal negatives.
4
Second conjunction
Set up: major premise is universal affirmative (Every A is J), minor premise is universal negative (No B is J). Conclusion is universal negative (No B is A).
This is the second conjunction: both universal, minor negative.
5
Proof by contradiction for the second
Assume the opposite: some B is A. From the major 'Every A is J' derive 'Some B is J'. But the minor says 'No B is J'. Contradiction. Thus the conclusion holds.
The author gives this proof directly in the passage.

وأما قرائنه فباعتبار تركب المحصورات بعضها مع بعض فستة عشر كما عرف، غير أن اشتراط كليّة كبراه أسقط منها ما الكبرى فيها جزئية وجزئية سالبة وهي ثمانٍ، إذ كل واحدة من الكبريين فلها أربع صغريات، وهي الكلية الموجبة والكلية السالبة والجزئية الموجبة والجزئية السالبة، واشتراط اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها أسقط منها أربعة وهي الكائنة من الكليتين إيجابا وسلباً، ومن موجبتين والصغرى جزئية، ومن سالبتين والصغرى جزئية، فعادت قرائنه عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها إلى أربع وهي الكائنة من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، وكليتين وصغراهما سالبة، وجزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، وجزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة. وعند اختلاف الجهة تعود قرائنه المنتجة إلى ثمانٍ وهي هذه وما أسقطناها باشتراط اختلاف الكيفية ولنشرع في بيان كل واحد منها. فنقول : إما أن تكون الجهة متحدة أو مختلفة، فإن كانت متحدة فإما أن تكون جهة الضرورة أو الإمكان أو الإطلاق، فإن كانت جهة الضرورة فإما أن تكون مطلقة أو مشروطة، ولنقرض الكلام في الكائن من الضروري المطلق أولاً، وقد بان أن القرائن عند اتحاد الجهة لا تزيد على أربع: الاقتران الأول منها : من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، ونتيجته كلية ضرورية سالبة كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها على الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول وهو بين. الاقتران الثاني :[ منها] من كليتين والصغرى سالبة، ونتيجته كالتي قبله كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وهو خلف.
As for its [the second figure's] productive conjunctions, by considering the combination of quantified propositions with each other, there are sixteen, as known. However, the condition that its major premise be universal eliminates those in which the major is particular affirmative and particular negative, which are eight, since each of the two majors has four minors: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. And the condition that its two premises differ in quality when modality is uniform or overlapping eliminates four: those consisting of two universal premises affirmative and negative, two affirmatives with particular minor, and two negatives with particular minor. Thus its productive conjunctions when modality is uniform or overlapping reduce to four: two universals with major negative; two universals with minor negative; particular affirmative minor with universal negative major; particular negative minor with universal affirmative major. And when modality differs, its productive conjunctions return to eight, which are these plus those we eliminated by the condition of differing quality. Let us commence explaining each one. We say: modality is either uniform or different. If uniform, it is either necessity, possibility, or absolute. If necessity, it is either absolute or conditional. Let us restrict discussion to the absolute necessary first. It has become clear that the conjunctions when modality is uniform do not exceed four. The first conjunction: from two universals with major negative, its conclusion is a universal necessary negative, as in our saying: 'Necessarily every B is J' and 'Necessarily no A is J'; so the conclusion is necessarily no B is A. Otherwise (if it were possible), it would be 'by general possibility some B is A', but it was 'necessarily no A is J'; so it would be 'necessarily some B is not J'; but it was 'necessarily every B is J', which is a contradiction. Also because if you convert the major and keep it as major, the conjunction becomes the second conjunction of the first figure, which is clear. The second conjunction: from two universals with minor negative, its conclusion is like the previous, as in our saying: 'Necessarily no B is J' and 'Necessarily every A is J'; so the conclusion is necessarily no B is A. Otherwise, it would be 'by general possibility some B is A', but it was 'necessarily every A is J'; so it would be 'necessarily some B is J'; but it was 'necessarily no B is J', which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage provides a systematic enumeration of the productive conjunctions (qara'in) in the second figure of categorical syllogism. The author begins by noting that there are 16 possible combinations when considering the quantity and quality of the two premises (four for the major times four for the minor). The first condition—that the major premise must be universal—eliminates half (eight) because the major cannot be particular. A second condition when modality is uniform: the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This removes another four. Thus only four conjunctions remain when modality is uniform. The author then lists them explicitly: (1) both universal, major negative; (2) both universal, minor negative; (3) particular affirmative minor, universal negative major; (4) particular negative minor, universal affirmative major. When modality differs, the condition of differing quality is removed, so eight conjunctions become productive (the four above plus the four that were eliminated only because of uniform quality condition). The author then focuses on the uniform modality case, specifically absolute necessity. He provides proofs for the first two conjunctions using proof by contradiction (khalf). For the first conjunction (major negative), he assumes the opposite of the conclusion (that some B is A), and shows it leads to a contradiction with the premises. He also notes an alternative proof via conversion of the major premise, which transforms it into a first figure syllogism. The second conjunction (minor negative) is proved similarly.

Key Terms
القرائن (al-qara'in): Productive conjunctions of premises that yield a valid conclusion in a given figure. Here, specifically the valid combinations in the second figure. [Note: In earlier works, this term may be used for 'signs' or 'clues'; here it is a technical term for mood combinations.]
الجهة (al-jiha): Modality of the proposition (e.g., necessity, possibility, absolute). The author discusses uniform modality (jiha muttahida) and different modality (jiha mukhtalifa). [Note: Standard term in Arabic logic for modality.]
خلف (khalf): Proof by contradiction: assuming the opposite of the desired conclusion and deriving an impossibility. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proof by contradiction for the first conjunction (major negative), what assumption leads to the contradiction?
A) Some B is not J
B) Some B is A
C) No B is A
D) Every A is J

Key Takeaway
Second figure syllogisms can be validated by converting the major premise to first figure or by assuming the opposite conclusion and deriving a contradiction with the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Prove the validity of the following second figure syllogism using both proof by contradiction and conversion to first figure: Major: No obligatory act is sinful; Minor: Every prayer is obligatory. (Conclusion: No prayer is sinful.)

The Four Second-Figure Conjunctions under Necessity
Enumeration of four productive second-figure conjunctions under necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four productive second-figure conjunctions under unified necessity modality and state the conditions that reduce them from eight.

Core Idea
Only four of eight possible conjunctions yield a conclusion under necessity in the second figure.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates the valid conjunctions of the second figure when the modality is unified necessity. He shows that conditions on the major premise and on the quality of premises eliminate half of the possibilities, leaving exactly four. We will see how he arrives at these four and their ordering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial eight conjunctions
Start with two possible major premises: universal negative and universal affirmative. For each major, there are four possible minors: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. That gives 8 total conjunctions.
The author states explicitly: 'إذ كل واحدة من الكبريين فلها أربع صغريات'.
2
Apply the condition of differing quality
When modality is unified, the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This eliminates all conjunctions where both premises are affirmative or both are negative, including those with particular minors. The author says this eliminates four: the two-universal affirmative and negative, and two with particular minors both affirmative or both negative.
The text says 'اشتراط اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها أسقط منها أربعة'.
3
List the four surviving conjunctions
The four productive conjunctions are: (1) Two universals with major negative (universal affirmative minor, universal negative major). (2) Two universals with minor negative (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major). (3) Particular affirmative minor, universal negative major. (4) Particular negative minor, universal affirmative major.
The author lists them: 'وهي الكائنة من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، وكليتين وصغراهما سالبة، وجزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، وجزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة.'
4
Note the ordering
The author orders them by ease of proof. The first is the easiest, the second requires more steps, the third is intermediate, and the fourth is the most difficult, as it cannot be proven by conversion alone.
The author mentions this ordering explicitly for the second conjunction: 'وإن كان مساوياً للأول في إنتاج الكلي السالب فإنما جعل تلواً له وثانياً'.

الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
اشتراط كليّة كبراه أسقط منها ما الكبرى فيها جزئية موجبة وجزئية سالبة وهي ثمانٍ، إذ كل واحدة من الكبريين فلها أربع صغريات، وهي الكلية الموجبة والكلية السالبة والجزئية الموجبة والجزئية السالبة، واشتراط اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها أسقط منها أربعة وهي الكائنة من الكليتين إيجابا وسلباً، ومن موجبتين والصغرى جزئية، ومن سالبتين والصغرى جزئية، فعادت قرائنه عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها إلى أربع وهي الكائنة من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، وكليتين وصغراهما سالبة، وجزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، وجزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة.
وعند اختلاف الجهة تعود قرائنه المنتجة إلى ثمانٍ وهي هذه وما أسقطناها باشتراط اختلاف الكيفية ولنشرع في بيان كل واحد منها.
فنقول: إما أن تكون الجهة متحدة أو مختلفة، فإن كانت متحدة فإما أن تكون جهة الضرورة أو الإمكان أو الإطلاق، فإن كانت جهة الضرورة فإما أن تكون مطلقة أو مشروطة، ولنقرض الكلام في الكائن من الضروري المطلق أولاً، وقد بان أن القرائن عند اتحاد الجهة لا تزيد على أربع:
الاقتران الأول منها: من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، ونتيجته كلية ضرورية سالبة كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها على الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول وهو بين.
الاقتران الثاني: [منها] من كليتين والصغرى سالبة، ونتيجته كالتي قبله كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وهو خلف.
ولأنك إذا عكست الصغرى وهي (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وجعلتها كبرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب" وقد بان أن الكليّة الضرورية السالبة تنعكس كنفسها، فإذا عكس بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب" كان عكسه بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وهو عين المطلوب، وهذا الاقتران وإن كان مساوياً للأول في إنتاج الكلي السالب فإنما جعل تلواً له وثانياً، إذ هو يفتقر في البيان بالعكس إلى عكسين - أعني عكس المقدمة الصغرى وعكس النتيجة، وإلى نقل إحدى المقدمتين إلى مكان الأخرى - كما عرفته، ولا كذلك الاقتران الذي قبله.
الاقتران الثالث: [منها] من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، ونتيجته جزئية ضرورية سالبة؛ ولذلك كان ثالثاً ومتأخراً عن الثاني، كقولنا: (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وهو خلف. ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وهي لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وهو عين المطلوب.
الاقتران الرابع من جزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة، ونتيجته كنتيجة الاقتران الذي قبله، كقولنا بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهو خلف، وليس هذا الاقتران مما يمكن بيان إنتاجه بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول، لأنك إن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية، ولا قياس من جزئيتين، والصغرى فلا عكس لها؛ لكونها جزئية سالبة، ولأجل تبينه بالرد إلى الشكل الأول وبعده عنه، وإن كانت نتيجته جزئية سالبة كالتي قبله كان رابعاً ومتأخراً عن الاقتران الثالث، ولكنه وإن لم يتبين بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول فقد يمكن
The first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The condition of the universality of its major premise eliminates those in which the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, and they are eight, since for each of the two majors there are four minors: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The condition of the difference of its two premises in quality when the modality is unified or overlapping eliminates four: those composed of two universals affirmative and negative, of two affirmatives with the minor particular, and of two negatives with the minor particular. So its productive conjunctions when the modality is unified or overlapping reduce to four: those composed of two universals with the major negative; two universals with both negatives; a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major; and a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major. When the modality differs, its productive conjunctions become eight, which are these and those we eliminated by the condition of difference in quality. We will begin to explain each one of them. We say: Either the modality is unified or different. If it is unified, then it is either necessity, possibility, or absoluteness. If it is necessity, then either absolute or conditional. Let us restrict the discussion to the case of absolute necessity first. It is clear that the conjunctions when the modality is unified are no more than four: The first conjunction: from two universals with the major negative, and its conclusion is a universal necessary negative, as we say: (Necessarily every B is C) and (Necessarily no A is C), so the conclusion is necessarily no B is A. Otherwise, if it were possibly some B is A, and it is necessarily no A is C, then necessarily some B is not C, but it was necessarily every B is C, which is a contradiction. And also because if you convert the major and keep it as major, it reduces to the second conjunction of the first figure, which is clear. The second conjunction: from two universals with the minor negative, and its conclusion is like the previous one, as we say: Necessarily no B is C, and necessarily every A is C, so necessarily no B is A. Otherwise, if it were possibly some B is A, and it is necessarily every A is C, then necessarily some B is C, but it was necessarily no B is C, which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the minor, which is 'no B is C', and make it major, it returns to the second conjunction of the first figure, producing necessarily no A is B. It is clear that the universal necessary negative converts as itself, so if you convert 'necessarily no A is B', its converse is 'necessarily no B is A', which is the very desired conclusion. This conjunction, although equivalent to the first in producing the universal negative, is made second, because its proof by conversion requires two conversions—namely, conversion of the minor premise and conversion of the conclusion—and transferring one premise to the place of the other, as you know, unlike the preceding conjunction. The third conjunction: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and its conclusion is a particular necessary negative; therefore it is third and after the second, as we say: (Necessarily some B is C) and (Necessarily no A is C), so necessarily some B is not A. Otherwise, if it were possibly every B is A, and it is necessarily no A is C, then necessarily no B is C, but it was necessarily some B is C, which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the major, which is 'no A is C', and keep it as major, it reduces to the fourth conjunction of the first figure, producing necessarily some B is not A, which is the very desired conclusion. The fourth conjunction: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and its conclusion is like the conclusion of the preceding conjunction, as we say: Necessarily some B is not C, and necessarily every A is C, so necessarily some B is not A. Otherwise, if it were possibly every B is A, and it is necessarily every A is C, then necessarily every B is C, but it was necessarily some B is not C, which is a contradiction. This conjunction cannot be proven by conversion and reduction to the first figure, because if you convert the major, it becomes particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars, and the minor has no conversion because it is a particular negative. Because of its proof by reduction to the first figure being remote, even though its conclusion is a particular negative like the one before it, it is fourth and after the third. But even if it is not proven by conversion and reduction to the first figure, it can perhaps [be proven by other means].
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the productive conjunctions of the second figure of the categorical syllogism when the modality is unified, specifically under the necessity modality. The author first states that the condition of the universality of the major premise eliminates eight possible conjunctions down to four when modality is unified. These four are listed: 1) universal major negative with universal minor affirmative, 2) universal minor negative with universal major affirmative, 3) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, and 4) particular negative minor with universal affirmative major. For each, the author provides a proof using reduction to the first figure or conversion, often via contradiction (khulf). For the first conjunction, the argument uses the impossibility of the contradictory and conversion of the major. The second conjunction requires two conversions and a transfer of premises, making it less direct. The third conjunction is proved by contradiction and conversion of the major to the fourth conjunction of the first figure. The fourth conjunction cannot be proved by conversion and reduction because converting the major yields a particular, and two particulars do not yield a syllogism; the minor cannot be converted. Thus its proof is more indirect. The author emphasizes that these four are the only productive ones under unified necessity modality, and they are ordered by their ease of proof. Islamic science examples: The author uses placeholder letters (B, A, C) but we can understand that in a legal context, one might argue: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (B is C). No nabidh is an intoxicant (no A is C). Therefore no nabidh is forbidden (no B is A).' This is a valid second-figure syllogism with universal affirmative major and universal negative minor, corresponding to the second conjunction here.

Proof of the First Two Conjunctions
Proofs of the first two second-figure conjunctions under necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof for the first two productive second-figure conjunctions under necessity modality using contradiction and conversion.

Core Idea
The first two conjunctions yield a universal negative conclusion, proven by conversion to the first figure or by contradiction.

The author proves the first two conjunctions step by step. For the first, a single conversion of the major reduces it to the first figure. For the second, two conversions and a transfer of premises are needed. We will walk through these proofs.

Guided Path
1
First conjunction premises and desired conclusion
The first conjunction is: Necessarily every B is C (minor) and Necessarily no A is C (major). The desired conclusion is Necessarily no B is A.
The author writes: 'كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ")'.
2
Proof by contradiction
Assume the opposite: possibly some B is A. Combine with the major (no A is C) to get necessarily some B is not C, which contradicts the minor (every B is C).
The author shows this khulf.
3
Proof by conversion
Convert the major (no A is C) to no C is A (it converts as itself). Now we have: every B is C, no C is A. This is a first-figure syllogism (second mood) and yields no B is A.
The author says: 'ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها على الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول وهو بين.'
4
Second conjunction premises
The second conjunction is: Necessarily no B is C (minor) and Necessarily every A is C (major). Desired conclusion: Necessarily no B is A.
The author writes: 'بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ")'.
5
Proof by contradiction
Assume possibly some B is A. Combine with the major (every A is C) to get necessarily some B is C, contradicting the minor (no B is C).
The author demonstrates this.

الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
اشتراط كليّة كبراه أسقط منها ما الكبرى فيها جزئية موجبة وجزئية سالبة وهي ثمانٍ، إذ كل واحدة من الكبريين فلها أربع صغريات، وهي الكلية الموجبة والكلية السالبة والجزئية الموجبة والجزئية السالبة، واشتراط اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها أسقط منها أربعة وهي الكائنة من الكليتين إيجابا وسلباً، ومن موجبتين والصغرى جزئية، ومن سالبتين والصغرى جزئية، فعادت قرائنه عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها إلى أربع وهي الكائنة من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، وكليتين وصغراهما سالبة، وجزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، وجزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة.
وعند اختلاف الجهة تعود قرائنه المنتجة إلى ثمانٍ وهي هذه وما أسقطناها باشتراط اختلاف الكيفية ولنشرع في بيان كل واحد منها.
فنقول: إما أن تكون الجهة متحدة أو مختلفة، فإن كانت متحدة فإما أن تكون جهة الضرورة أو الإمكان أو الإطلاق، فإن كانت جهة الضرورة فإما أن تكون مطلقة أو مشروطة، ولنقرض الكلام في الكائن من الضروري المطلق أولاً، وقد بان أن القرائن عند اتحاد الجهة لا تزيد على أربع:
الاقتران الأول منها: من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، ونتيجته كلية ضرورية سالبة كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها على الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول وهو بين.
الاقتران الثاني: [منها] من كليتين والصغرى سالبة، ونتيجته كالتي قبله كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وهو خلف.
ولأنك إذا عكست الصغرى وهي (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وجعلتها كبرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب" وقد بان أن الكليّة الضرورية السالبة تنعكس كنفسها، فإذا عكس بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب" كان عكسه بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وهو عين المطلوب، وهذا الاقتران وإن كان مساوياً للأول في إنتاج الكلي السالب فإنما جعل تلواً له وثانياً، إذ هو يفتقر في البيان بالعكس إلى عكسين - أعني عكس المقدمة الصغرى وعكس النتيجة، وإلى نقل إحدى المقدمتين إلى مكان الأخرى - كما عرفته، ولا كذلك الاقتران الذي قبله.
الاقتران الثالث: [منها] من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، ونتيجته جزئية ضرورية سالبة؛ ولذلك كان ثالثاً ومتأخراً عن الثاني، كقولنا: (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وهو خلف. ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وهي لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وهو عين المطلوب.
الاقتران الرابع من جزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة، ونتيجته كنتيجة الاقتران الذي قبله، كقولنا بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهو خلف، وليس هذا الاقتران مما يمكن بيان إنتاجه بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول، لأنك إن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية، ولا قياس من جزئيتين، والصغرى فلا عكس لها؛ لكونها جزئية سالبة، ولأجل تبينه بالرد إلى الشكل الأول وبعده عنه، وإن كانت نتيجته جزئية سالبة كالتي قبله كان رابعاً ومتأخراً عن الاقتران الثالث، ولكنه وإن لم يتبين بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول فقد يمكن
The first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The condition of the universality of its major premise eliminates those in which the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, and they are eight, since for each of the two majors there are four minors: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The condition of the difference of its two premises in quality when the modality is unified or overlapping eliminates four: those composed of two universals affirmative and negative, of two affirmatives with the minor particular, and of two negatives with the minor particular. So its productive conjunctions when the modality is unified or overlapping reduce to four: those composed of two universals with the major negative; two universals with both negatives; a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major; and a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major. When the modality differs, its productive conjunctions become eight, which are these and those we eliminated by the condition of difference in quality. We will begin to explain each one of them. We say: Either the modality is unified or different. If it is unified, then it is either necessity, possibility, or absoluteness. If it is necessity, then either absolute or conditional. Let us restrict the discussion to the case of absolute necessity first. It is clear that the conjunctions when the modality is unified are no more than four: The first conjunction: from two universals with the major negative, and its conclusion is a universal necessary negative, as we say: (Necessarily every B is C) and (Necessarily no A is C), so the conclusion is necessarily no B is A. Otherwise, if it were possibly some B is A, and it is necessarily no A is C, then necessarily some B is not C, but it was necessarily every B is C, which is a contradiction. And also because if you convert the major and keep it as major, it reduces to the second conjunction of the first figure, which is clear. The second conjunction: from two universals with the minor negative, and its conclusion is like the previous one, as we say: Necessarily no B is C, and necessarily every A is C, so necessarily no B is A. Otherwise, if it were possibly some B is A, and it is necessarily every A is C, then necessarily some B is C, but it was necessarily no B is C, which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the minor, which is 'no B is C', and make it major, it returns to the second conjunction of the first figure, producing necessarily no A is B. It is clear that the universal necessary negative converts as itself, so if you convert 'necessarily no A is B', its converse is 'necessarily no B is A', which is the very desired conclusion. This conjunction, although equivalent to the first in producing the universal negative, is made second, because its proof by conversion requires two conversions—namely, conversion of the minor premise and conversion of the conclusion—and transferring one premise to the place of the other, as you know, unlike the preceding conjunction. The third conjunction: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and its conclusion is a particular necessary negative; therefore it is third and after the second, as we say: (Necessarily some B is C) and (Necessarily no A is C), so necessarily some B is not A. Otherwise, if it were possibly every B is A, and it is necessarily no A is C, then necessarily no B is C, but it was necessarily some B is C, which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the major, which is 'no A is C', and keep it as major, it reduces to the fourth conjunction of the first figure, producing necessarily some B is not A, which is the very desired conclusion. The fourth conjunction: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and its conclusion is like the conclusion of the preceding conjunction, as we say: Necessarily some B is not C, and necessarily every A is C, so necessarily some B is not A. Otherwise, if it were possibly every B is A, and it is necessarily every A is C, then necessarily every B is C, but it was necessarily some B is not C, which is a contradiction. This conjunction cannot be proven by conversion and reduction to the first figure, because if you convert the major, it becomes particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars, and the minor has no conversion because it is a particular negative. Because of its proof by reduction to the first figure being remote, even though its conclusion is a particular negative like the one before it, it is fourth and after the third. But even if it is not proven by conversion and reduction to the first figure, it can perhaps [be proven by other means].
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the productive conjunctions of the second figure of the categorical syllogism when the modality is unified, specifically under the necessity modality. The author first states that the condition of the universality of the major premise eliminates eight possible conjunctions down to four when modality is unified. These four are listed: 1) universal major negative with universal minor affirmative, 2) universal minor negative with universal major affirmative, 3) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, and 4) particular negative minor with universal affirmative major. For each, the author provides a proof using reduction to the first figure or conversion, often via contradiction (khulf). For the first conjunction, the argument uses the impossibility of the contradictory and conversion of the major. The second conjunction requires two conversions and a transfer of premises, making it less direct. The third conjunction is proved by contradiction and conversion of the major to the fourth conjunction of the first figure. The fourth conjunction cannot be proved by conversion and reduction because converting the major yields a particular, and two particulars do not yield a syllogism; the minor cannot be converted. Thus its proof is more indirect. The author emphasizes that these four are the only productive ones under unified necessity modality, and they are ordered by their ease of proof. Islamic science examples: The author uses placeholder letters (B, A, C) but we can understand that in a legal context, one might argue: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (B is C). No nabidh is an intoxicant (no A is C). Therefore no nabidh is forbidden (no B is A).' This is a valid second-figure syllogism with universal affirmative major and universal negative minor, corresponding to the second conjunction here.

?

The Syllogism
intermediate

Proof of the Third and Fourth Conjunctions
Proofs of the third and fourth second-figure conjunctions under necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the third and fourth second-figure conjunctions under necessity and explain why the fourth cannot be proven by conversion.

Core Idea
The third conjunction yields a particular negative and is provable by conversion; the fourth yields the same conclusion but cannot be reduced to the first figure by conversion.

The author continues with the third and fourth conjunctions, both yielding particular negative conclusions. The third is proven by contradiction and conversion of the major to the first figure. The fourth is proven by contradiction but cannot be reduced via conversion because converting the major yields a particular, and two particulars do not produce a valid syllogism. We will examine these proofs and understand the limitation.

Guided Path
1
Third conjunction premises
The third conjunction is: Necessarily some B is C (particular affirmative minor) and Necessarily no A is C (universal negative major). Desired conclusion: Necessarily some B is not A.
The author writes: '(بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ")'.
2
Proof by contradiction
Assume possibly every B is A. Combine with major (no A is C) to get necessarily no B is C, contradicting the minor (some B is C).
The author explains: 'وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وهو خلف'.
3
Proof by conversion
Convert the major (no A is C) to no C is A. Now we have: some B is C (minor), no C is A (major). This is the fourth mood of the first figure, producing some B is not A.
The author says: 'ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وهي لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وهو عين المطلوب'.
4
Fourth conjunction premises
The fourth conjunction is: Necessarily some B is not C (particular negative minor) and Necessarily every A is C (universal affirmative major). Desired conclusion: Necessarily some B is not A.
The author writes: 'بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ")'.
5
Proof by contradiction
Assume possibly every B is A. Combine with major (every A is C) to get necessarily every B is C, contradicting the minor (some B is not C).
The author shows this khulf.

الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
اشتراط كليّة كبراه أسقط منها ما الكبرى فيها جزئية موجبة وجزئية سالبة وهي ثمانٍ، إذ كل واحدة من الكبريين فلها أربع صغريات، وهي الكلية الموجبة والكلية السالبة والجزئية الموجبة والجزئية السالبة، واشتراط اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها أسقط منها أربعة وهي الكائنة من الكليتين إيجابا وسلباً، ومن موجبتين والصغرى جزئية، ومن سالبتين والصغرى جزئية، فعادت قرائنه عند اتحاد الجهة أو تداخلها إلى أربع وهي الكائنة من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، وكليتين وصغراهما سالبة، وجزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، وجزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة.
وعند اختلاف الجهة تعود قرائنه المنتجة إلى ثمانٍ وهي هذه وما أسقطناها باشتراط اختلاف الكيفية ولنشرع في بيان كل واحد منها.
فنقول: إما أن تكون الجهة متحدة أو مختلفة، فإن كانت متحدة فإما أن تكون جهة الضرورة أو الإمكان أو الإطلاق، فإن كانت جهة الضرورة فإما أن تكون مطلقة أو مشروطة، ولنقرض الكلام في الكائن من الضروري المطلق أولاً، وقد بان أن القرائن عند اتحاد الجهة لا تزيد على أربع:
الاقتران الأول منها: من كليتين والكبرى سالبة، ونتيجته كلية ضرورية سالبة كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها على الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول وهو بين.
الاقتران الثاني: [منها] من كليتين والصغرى سالبة، ونتيجته كالتي قبله كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وهو خلف.
ولأنك إذا عكست الصغرى وهي (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وجعلتها كبرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب" وقد بان أن الكليّة الضرورية السالبة تنعكس كنفسها، فإذا عكس بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب" كان عكسه بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وهو عين المطلوب، وهذا الاقتران وإن كان مساوياً للأول في إنتاج الكلي السالب فإنما جعل تلواً له وثانياً، إذ هو يفتقر في البيان بالعكس إلى عكسين - أعني عكس المقدمة الصغرى وعكس النتيجة، وإلى نقل إحدى المقدمتين إلى مكان الأخرى - كما عرفته، ولا كذلك الاقتران الذي قبله.
الاقتران الثالث: [منها] من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، ونتيجته جزئية ضرورية سالبة؛ ولذلك كان ثالثاً ومتأخراً عن الثاني، كقولنا: (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وهو خلف. ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وهي لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وهو عين المطلوب.
الاقتران الرابع من جزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة، ونتيجته كنتيجة الاقتران الذي قبله، كقولنا بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهو خلف، وليس هذا الاقتران مما يمكن بيان إنتاجه بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول، لأنك إن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية، ولا قياس من جزئيتين، والصغرى فلا عكس لها؛ لكونها جزئية سالبة، ولأجل تبينه بالرد إلى الشكل الأول وبعده عنه، وإن كانت نتيجته جزئية سالبة كالتي قبله كان رابعاً ومتأخراً عن الاقتران الثالث، ولكنه وإن لم يتبين بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول فقد يمكن
The first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The condition of the universality of its major premise eliminates those in which the major is particular affirmative or particular negative, and they are eight, since for each of the two majors there are four minors: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The condition of the difference of its two premises in quality when the modality is unified or overlapping eliminates four: those composed of two universals affirmative and negative, of two affirmatives with the minor particular, and of two negatives with the minor particular. So its productive conjunctions when the modality is unified or overlapping reduce to four: those composed of two universals with the major negative; two universals with both negatives; a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major; and a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major. When the modality differs, its productive conjunctions become eight, which are these and those we eliminated by the condition of difference in quality. We will begin to explain each one of them. We say: Either the modality is unified or different. If it is unified, then it is either necessity, possibility, or absoluteness. If it is necessity, then either absolute or conditional. Let us restrict the discussion to the case of absolute necessity first. It is clear that the conjunctions when the modality is unified are no more than four: The first conjunction: from two universals with the major negative, and its conclusion is a universal necessary negative, as we say: (Necessarily every B is C) and (Necessarily no A is C), so the conclusion is necessarily no B is A. Otherwise, if it were possibly some B is A, and it is necessarily no A is C, then necessarily some B is not C, but it was necessarily every B is C, which is a contradiction. And also because if you convert the major and keep it as major, it reduces to the second conjunction of the first figure, which is clear. The second conjunction: from two universals with the minor negative, and its conclusion is like the previous one, as we say: Necessarily no B is C, and necessarily every A is C, so necessarily no B is A. Otherwise, if it were possibly some B is A, and it is necessarily every A is C, then necessarily some B is C, but it was necessarily no B is C, which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the minor, which is 'no B is C', and make it major, it returns to the second conjunction of the first figure, producing necessarily no A is B. It is clear that the universal necessary negative converts as itself, so if you convert 'necessarily no A is B', its converse is 'necessarily no B is A', which is the very desired conclusion. This conjunction, although equivalent to the first in producing the universal negative, is made second, because its proof by conversion requires two conversions—namely, conversion of the minor premise and conversion of the conclusion—and transferring one premise to the place of the other, as you know, unlike the preceding conjunction. The third conjunction: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and its conclusion is a particular necessary negative; therefore it is third and after the second, as we say: (Necessarily some B is C) and (Necessarily no A is C), so necessarily some B is not A. Otherwise, if it were possibly every B is A, and it is necessarily no A is C, then necessarily no B is C, but it was necessarily some B is C, which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the major, which is 'no A is C', and keep it as major, it reduces to the fourth conjunction of the first figure, producing necessarily some B is not A, which is the very desired conclusion. The fourth conjunction: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and its conclusion is like the conclusion of the preceding conjunction, as we say: Necessarily some B is not C, and necessarily every A is C, so necessarily some B is not A. Otherwise, if it were possibly every B is A, and it is necessarily every A is C, then necessarily every B is C, but it was necessarily some B is not C, which is a contradiction. This conjunction cannot be proven by conversion and reduction to the first figure, because if you convert the major, it becomes particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars, and the minor has no conversion because it is a particular negative. Because of its proof by reduction to the first figure being remote, even though its conclusion is a particular negative like the one before it, it is fourth and after the third. But even if it is not proven by conversion and reduction to the first figure, it can perhaps [be proven by other means].
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the productive conjunctions of the second figure of the categorical syllogism when the modality is unified, specifically under the necessity modality. The author first states that the condition of the universality of the major premise eliminates eight possible conjunctions down to four when modality is unified. These four are listed: 1) universal major negative with universal minor affirmative, 2) universal minor negative with universal major affirmative, 3) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, and 4) particular negative minor with universal affirmative major. For each, the author provides a proof using reduction to the first figure or conversion, often via contradiction (khulf). For the first conjunction, the argument uses the impossibility of the contradictory and conversion of the major. The second conjunction requires two conversions and a transfer of premises, making it less direct. The third conjunction is proved by contradiction and conversion of the major to the fourth conjunction of the first figure. The fourth conjunction cannot be proved by conversion and reduction because converting the major yields a particular, and two particulars do not yield a syllogism; the minor cannot be converted. Thus its proof is more indirect. The author emphasizes that these four are the only productive ones under unified necessity modality, and they are ordered by their ease of proof. Islamic science examples: The author uses placeholder letters (B, A, C) but we can understand that in a legal context, one might argue: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (B is C). No nabidh is an intoxicant (no A is C). Therefore no nabidh is forbidden (no B is A).' This is a valid second-figure syllogism with universal affirmative major and universal negative minor, corresponding to the second conjunction here.

الاقتران
العكس
الشكل الأول

The Third Conjugation: Form and Mood
Definition of the third conjugation mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third conjugation mood of a categorical syllogism based on its premise types and conclusion.

Core Idea
The third conjugation combines a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major to yield a particular negative.

In this passage, the author defines the third conjugation of the categorical syllogism. Understanding its form allows you to recognize this mood in logical arguments. We will break down the components and verify the conclusion type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Check if it is a particular affirmative proposition (some B is C).
In the passage, the minor is 'necessarily some B is C'.
2
Identify the major premise
Check if it is a universal negative (no A is C).
The passage gives 'necessarily no A is C'.
3
Determine the conclusion
It should be a particular negative (some B is not A).
The author states the conclusion is 'necessarily some B is not A'.
4
Confirm the order
The conjunctions are ordered by generality; the third conjugation comes after the second.
The passage explains it is third and posterior to the second.

الاقتران الثالث : من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة، ونتيجته جزئية ضرورية سالبة؛ ولذلك كان ثالثاً ومتأخراً عن الثاني، كقولنا: (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وهو خلف. ولأنك إذا عكست الكبرى وهي لا شيء من "أ" "ج") وأبقيتها كبرى بحالها عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وهو عين المطلوب.
The third conjunction: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and its conclusion is a particular necessary negative; therefore it is third and posterior to the second, as we say: (necessarily some B is C) and (necessarily no A is C) so (necessarily some B is not A). Otherwise (by universal possibility every B is A) and (necessarily no A is C) so (necessarily no B is C) and (necessarily some B is C) which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the major, which is 'no A is C', and keep it as major, the conjunction returns to the fourth conjunction of the first figure, producing necessarily some B is not A, which is exactly the desired conclusion.

Commentary
This passage defines the third conjugation (mood) of the syllogism. It specifies its premises: a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and its conclusion: a particular necessary negative. The author explains why it is third, after the second. He then provides an example using the variables B, C, A. He demonstrates the conclusion through a reductio ad absurdum (khalaf): if the conclusion were false, then by universal possibility every B would be A, but then with the given major, we would derive a contradiction. Then he shows how the same mood can be reduced to the fourth conjugation of the first figure by converting the major premise and keeping it as major, yielding the same conclusion. This illustrates the interdependence of moods and the method of reduction. In Islamic legal reasoning, one might analogize: 'Some scholars (B) are Hanafis (C). No Maliki (A) is a Hanafi (C). Proofs of the Third Conjugation: Reductio and Reduction
Proof of the third conjugation's conclusion via reductio and reduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the conclusion of the third conjugation syllogism using either reductio ad absurdum or reduction to the first figure by conversion.

Core Idea
The conclusion of the third conjugation can be proved by contradiction or by converting the major and reordering to the first figure.

After defining the third conjugation, the author provides two methods to prove its conclusion: a reductio ad absurdum (khalaf) and a reduction by converting the major premise and moving to the first figure. Mastering these proofs deepens your understanding of syllogistic necessity.

Guided Path
1
Set up the premises
Recall the premises: minor 'some B is C', major 'no A is C'.
2
Assume the opposite of the conclusion
Temporarily assume 'every B is A' (the contradictory of 'some B is not A').
This is the start of the reductio proof.
3
Derive a contradiction
Combine the assumption with the major: if every B is A and no A is C, then no B is C (by first figure). This contradicts the minor 'some B is C'.
Therefore, the assumption is false, so the original conclusion follows.
4
Perform the reduction
Convert the major premise 'no A is C' to 'no C is A' (simple conversion). Keep it as major, and keep the minor 'some B is C' as minor.
5
Identify the resulting figure
The new syllogism is: no C is A, some B is C, therefore some B is not A. This is a valid first figure syllogism (Ferio).
The reduction demonstrates the mood's validity by reducing it to an already-valid form.

Source Text

The third conjunction: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and its conclusion is a particular necessary negative; therefore it is third and posterior to the second, as we say: (necessarily some B is C) and (necessarily no A is C) so (necessarily some B is not A). Otherwise (by universal possibility every B is A) and (necessarily no A is C) so (necessarily no B is C) and (necessarily some B is C) which is a contradiction. The Fourth Conjugation and Proof by Assumption
Defines the fourth conjugation and proves it by assumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate the validity of the fourth conjugation categorical syllogism using the method of assumption (iftirad) when conversion fails.

Core Idea
When conversion fails, assumption rescues the syllogism.

We have seen that some syllogistic moods can be reduced to the first figure by conversion. However, the fourth conjugation—with a particular negative minor and universal affirmative major—resists such reduction. The logician then employs a technique called proof by assumption (iftirad), introducing a hypothetical individual to derive the conclusion through valid syllogisms. This lesson unpacks that method.

Guided Path
1
Identify premises and conclusion
State the given: minor premise 'some B is not C' (particular negative), major premise 'every A is C' (universal affirmative), conclusion 'some B is not A' (particular negative).
2
Check why conversion fails
Convert the major: 'every A is C' becomes 'some C is A' (particular). The minor 'some B is not C' cannot be converted. A syllogism cannot have two particular premises. Hence no reduction directly.
Conversion of the major yields a particular; two particulars yield no valid syllogism.
3
Introduce the assumption
Assume a specific individual D that is part of B and not C: i.e., 'some B is D' and 'no D is C'.
D is a hypothetical concrete instance of the exception.
4
Derive first intermediate conclusion
From 'no D is C' (minor) and 'every A is C' (major), apply the second conjugation (first figure) to get 'no D is A' (universal negative).
This is a valid Celarent-like syllogism.
5
Derive final conclusion
From 'some B is D' (minor) and 'no D is A' (major), apply the fourth conjugation of the first figure (Ferio-like) to get 'some B is not A' – the desired conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الرابع من جزئية صغرى سالبة وكلية كبرى موجبة، ونتيجته كنتيجة الاقتران الذي قبله، كقولنا بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") و(بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "أ") وكان بالضرورة) كل "أ" "ج") فـ (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وكان (بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهو خلف، وليس هذا الاقتران مما يمكن بيان إنتاجه بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول، لأنك إن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية، ولا قياس من جزئيتين، والصغرى فلا عكس لها؛ لكونها جزئية سالبة، ولأجل تبينه بالرد إلى الشكل الأول وبعده عنه، وإن كانت نتيجته جزئية سالبة كالتي قبله كان رابعاً ومتأخراً عن الاقتران الثالث، ولكنه وإن لم يتبين بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول فقد يمكن بيانه بالافتراض وهو أن تفرض البعض من "ب" الذي ليس هو "ج" شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً "د" فيلزمه (بعض "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "د" "ج")، فتأخذ أحد اللازمين وهو (لا شيء من "د" "ج")، وتجعله صغرى للكبرى فينتج من الاقتران الثاني من هذا الشكل كلية سالبة وهي (لا شيء من "د" "أ")، ثم تأخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعلها كبرى للازم الآخر وهو (بعض "ب" "د") فينتج من الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول جزئية سالبة هي (بعض "ب" ليس ("أ") وهو عين المطلوب.
The fourth conjunction: from a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and its conclusion is like the conclusion of the preceding conjunction, as we say: necessarily some B is not C, and necessarily every A is C, so necessarily some B is not A. Otherwise (by universal possibility every B is A) and necessarily every A is C, so necessarily every B is C, and necessarily some B is not C, which is a contradiction. And this conjunction cannot be demonstrated for its production by conversion and reduction to the first figure, because if you convert the major it becomes particular, and there is no syllogism from two particulars, and the minor has no conversion due to being a particular negative. And because its demonstration by reduction to the first figure is far from it, and although its conclusion is a particular negative like the one before it, it is fourth and posterior to the third conjunction. But even though it is not demonstrated by conversion and reduction to the first figure, it can be demonstrated by assumption, which is that you assume the part of B that is not C to be a specific thing, let it be D, so it follows that some B is D and no D is C. Then you take one of the two consequences, namely 'no D is C', and make it the minor for the major, producing from the second conjunction of this figure a universal negative, namely 'no D is A'. Then you take this result and make it the major for the other consequence, namely 'some B is D', producing from the fourth conjunction of the first figure a particular negative, namely 'some B is not A', which is exactly the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth conjugation, which has a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major. Its conclusion is also a particular negative, like the third. The author provides an example and proves it by contradiction. He then explains why this mood cannot be reduced to the first figure by conversion: converting the major makes it particular, resulting in two particulars (invalid), and the minor cannot be converted because it is a particular negative. So he introduces another method: proof by assumption (iftirad). He assumes a specific member 'D' of B that is not C, then shows that from 'no D is C' and the given major 'every A is C', we deduce 'no D is A' via the second conjugation (first figure). Then from 'some B is D' and that result, we get 'some B is not A' via the fourth conjugation of the first figure. This illustrates the use of hypothetical reasoning when direct reduction fails. For example, 'Some Hanafis (B) are not Malikis (C). Every scholar (A) is a Maliki (C). Therefore, some Hanafis (B) are not scholars (A).'

Proof by Hypothetical (Farḍ) in Categorical Syllogisms
Explains method of proof by hypothetical assumption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by farḍ for a particular negative conclusion in the first figure using two syllogistic steps.

Core Idea
Farḍ uses an assumed part to derive a conclusion that is not immediately obvious.

The passage introduces a powerful technique called farḍ (hypothetical assumption) for proving categorical syllogisms. Instead of relying on the obviousness of the conclusion, you introduce a new term D, a part of one extreme that does not share the middle term. By deriving two consecutive syllogisms, you obtain the desired conclusion. This method ensures logical rigor even when the conclusion seems 'near to self-evidence'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion
The desired conclusion is 'some B is not A' (particular negative). In the example, B is the subject term and A is the predicate term. The middle term is C, which is affirmed of B and negated of A.
Check that the conclusion matches the form given: some B is not A.
2
Assume a hypothetical part D
Assume a portion D of B that is not C. This yields two necessary consequences: 'some B is D' and 'no D is C'. D is an arbitrary part of B that excludes C.
The existence of such a D is required for the proof to work.
3
First syllogism: derive 'no D is A'
Take 'no D is C' as the minor premise and a major premise (not explicitly given) that relates C and A. Using the second mood (conjugation) of the first figure, we deduce 'no D is A' (universal negative).
The major premise is likely 'All C is not A' or equivalent, since C is negated of A.
4
Second syllogism: derive 'some B is not A'
Use 'some B is D' as the minor premise and 'no D is A' as the major premise. From the fourth mood of the first figure, we obtain the conclusion 'some B is not A', which is exactly the desired conclusion.
This is a valid compound syllogism; the conclusion follows necessarily.
5
Verify chain of reasoning
Check that each intermediate step is a valid syllogism. The first step uses the second mood (Celarent-like) and the second uses the fourth mood (Ferio-like). Together they prove the original claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. بيانه بالافتراض وهو أن تفرض البعض من "ب" الذي ليس هو "ج" شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً "د" فيلزمه (بعض "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "د" "ج")، فتأخذ أحد اللازمين وهو (لا شيء من "د" "ج")، وتجعله صغرى للكبرى فينتج من الاقتران الثاني من هذا الشكل كلية سالبة وهي (لا شيء من "د" "أ")، ثم تأخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعلها كبرى للازم الآخر وهو (بعض "ب" "د") فينتج من الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول جزئية سالبة هي (بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وهو عين المطلوب. وربما قيل: إنه لما كان الحد الأوسط ثابتاً لأحد الطرفين ومسلوباً عن الطرف الآخر تبين أن النسبة بين الطرفين في جميع هذه الاقترانات بالسلب من غير افتقار إلى بيان من عكس أو خلف أو فرض وهو وإن كان قريباً من البيان فليس بيناً مستغنياً عن البيان فإنه فرق بين البين والقريب من البين، وكل ما يتخيل في الاحتجاج على كونه بيناً فلا يزيد على نفس الدعوى.
The first art: on defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. Its explanation by hypothetical: that you assume a part of 'B' which is not 'C' as a particular thing, let it be for example 'D'. Then it follows: (some B is D) and (no D is C). Then you take one of the two necessary consequences, namely (no D is C), and make it the minor premise for the major premise, and there results from the second conjugation of this figure a universal negative: (no D is A). Then you take this conclusion and make it the major premise for the other necessary consequence, namely (some B is D), and there results from the fourth conjugation of the first figure a particular negative: (some B is not A), which is exactly the desired conclusion. And it might be said: since the middle term is affirmed of one extreme and negated of the other, the relation between the two extremes in all these conjugations is one of negation without needing a proof by conversion, reductio, or hypothesis; and although it is near to proof, it is not self-evident independent of proof, for there is a difference between the self-evident and that which is near to self-evidence. And everything imagined in arguing for its being self-evident does not exceed the very claim itself.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage explains a method of proof by hypothetical assumption (farḍ) for categorical syllogisms in the first figure. The author begins by describing the procedure: to prove that some B is not A (particular negative), one assumes a part D of B that is not C. This yields two premises: some B is D, and no D is C. Using no D is C as a minor premise with a suitable major premise (not stated here), one derives no D is A from the second mood of the first figure. Then, using that conclusion as a major premise with some B is D as the minor, one obtains some B is not A from the fourth mood of the first figure. The author then presents an objection: since the middle term (presumably C) is affirmed of one extreme (B) and negated of the other (A), the conclusion is obvious without such a complex proof. The author responds that although the method is near to self-evidence, it is not self-evident; there is a distinction between that which is self-evident and that which is merely close to it. Any attempt to argue that it is self-evident amounts to no more than the claim itself. This passage stresses that logical procedures must be explicit and cannot rely on intuition.

Key Terms
الافتراض
الاقتران
الحد الأوسط

Key Takeaway
Proof by farḍ uses a hypothetical part to derive a conclusion through two valid syllogisms, demonstrating that even conclusions 'near to self-evidence' require explicit logical demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a proof by farḍ for the statement 'Some intoxicant is not grape wine' using B = intoxicant, A = grape wine, C = forbidden. Specify the assumed D and derive the two syllogisms.
?
In a kalam debate, use farḍ to prove that 'Some body is not temporally originated' (if the conclusion is particular negative). Identify B, A, C, and the assumed D.

Self-Evident vs. Near-to-Self-Evident in Logical Proof
Refutes objection that proof by farḍ is unnecessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a claim that is bayyin (self-evident) and one that is qarīb min al-bayān (near self-evidence), and explain why the latter still requires explicit proof.

Core Idea
Nearness to self-evidence is not identity; logical rigor demands demonstration.

The passage addresses a common objection: if the middle term is affirmed of one extreme and negated of the other, isn't the negative conclusion obvious? The author firmly rejects this. He distinguishes between the self-evident (al-bayyin) and that which is merely close to it (al-qarīb min al-bayān). This epistemological distinction has profound implications: even when a conclusion seems obvious, a valid syllogism is required to establish it with certainty.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector claims: since the middle term C is affirmed of B and negated of A, the negative relation between B and A is obvious without needing any proof by conversion, reductio, or hypothesis.
The objector equates 'obvious' with 'self-evident'.
2
Identify the author's rebuttal
The author acknowledges the proof is 'near to self-evidence' (qarīb min al-bayān) but insists it is not self-evident (bayyin). He makes a sharp distinction between the two.
The key phrase: 'فإنه فرق بين البين والقريب من البين'.
3
Analyze the consequence of confusion
If one conflates the two, one might accept a claim without proof, which is epistemologically risky. The author says that any argument for self-evidence is just a repetition of the claim itself, not a valid proof.
The phrase 'لا يزيد على نفس الدعوى' emphasizes that mere insistence does not constitute demonstration.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
In usul al-fiqh, a ruling that seems 'obvious' from a general principle still requires a proper qiyās to be legally binding. The distinction ensures that no ruling is accepted without explicit reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. بيانه بالافتراض وهو أن تفرض البعض من "ب" الذي ليس هو "ج" شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً "د" فيلزمه (بعض "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "د" "ج")، فتأخذ أحد اللازمين وهو (لا شيء من "د" "ج")، وتجعله صغرى للكبرى فينتج من الاقتران الثاني من هذا الشكل كلية سالبة وهي (لا شيء من "د" "أ")، ثم تأخذ هذه النتيجة وتجعلها كبرى للازم الآخر وهو (بعض "ب" "د") فينتج من الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول جزئية سالبة هي (بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وهو عين المطلوب. وربما قيل: إنه لما كان الحد الأوسط ثابتاً لأحد الطرفين ومسلوباً عن الطرف الآخر تبين أن النسبة بين الطرفين في جميع هذه الاقترانات بالسلب من غير افتقار إلى بيان من عكس أو خلف أو فرض وهو وإن كان قريباً من البيان فليس بيناً مستغنياً عن البيان فإنه فرق بين البين والقريب من البين، وكل ما يتخيل في الاحتجاج على كونه بيناً فلا يزيد على نفس الدعوى.
The first art: on defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. Its explanation by hypothetical: that you assume a part of 'B' which is not 'C' as a particular thing, let it be for example 'D'. Then it follows: (some B is D) and (no D is C). Then you take one of the two necessary consequences, namely (no D is C), and make it the minor premise for the major premise, and there results from the second conjugation of this figure a universal negative: (no D is A). Then you take this conclusion and make it the major premise for the other necessary consequence, namely (some B is D), and there results from the fourth conjugation of the first figure a particular negative: (some B is not A), which is exactly the desired conclusion. And it might be said: since the middle term is affirmed of one extreme and negated of the other, the relation between the two extremes in all these conjugations is one of negation without needing a proof by conversion, reductio, or hypothesis; and although it is near to proof, it is not self-evident independent of proof, for there is a difference between the self-evident and that which is near to self-evidence. And everything imagined in arguing for its being self-evident does not exceed the very claim itself.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage explains a method of proof by hypothetical assumption (farḍ) for categorical syllogisms in the first figure. The author begins by describing the procedure: to prove that some B is not A (particular negative), one assumes a part D of B that is not C. This yields two premises: some B is D, and no D is C. Using no D is C as a minor premise with a suitable major premise (not stated here), one derives no D is A from the second mood of the first figure. Then, using that conclusion as a major premise with some B is D as the minor, one obtains some B is not A from the fourth mood of the first figure. The author then presents an objection: since the middle term (presumably C) is affirmed of one extreme (B) and negated of the other (A), the conclusion is obvious without such a complex proof. The author responds that although the method is near to self-evidence, it is not self-evident; there is a distinction between that which is self-evident and that which is merely close to it. Any attempt to argue that it is self-evident amounts to no more than the claim itself. This passage stresses that logical procedures must be explicit and cannot rely on intuition.

البين
القريب من البين
البيان

Key Takeaway
A claim that is 'near to self-evidence' still requires explicit logical demonstration; conflating it with genuine self-evidence undermines the rigor of proof.
